Date: Mon, 01 Nov 93 08:13:48 EST
From: E_J_Blassic%CAASD1@mwmgate1.mitre.org
Subject: Re: New IMSA RSR Race Team for 1994

Christopher Welty writes--

>I am proud to announce that Roger Morse Racing of East Greenbush, 
>NY, will be running three 3.8 RSRs in the 1994 IMSA GT Series.  The 
>prime sponsor will be Toyo tires, and to date Joe Danaher (IMSA 
>Firehawk point leader) and John Paul Jr. have agreed to be drivers.  
>Driving teams for Daytona and Sebring as well as the third principle 
>driver are still being finalized.

>Joe Danaher, whose Mazda RX7 TT has dominated the Firehawk series 
>(ending the reign of the 944S2) <text deleted>

It should be pointed out that neither Joe Danaher nor those dominating RX7s were
able to win the Firehawk Sports division championship.  The final race of the 
season was a 12hr one and there were no RX7s left at the end for Joe to climb 
into to earn the final points he needed to win the title.  His co-driver Peter 
Farrell was not permitted to drive due to a disqualification suffered earlier in
the year. Joe talked to his old team running Oldsmobiles and they gave him a 
ride, but even without the RX7s around his final position was not good enough. 
The Sports division was won by a Honda Prelude - not the RX7.

Porsche competes in the Grand Sports division and won with one of those 944S2s 
with a 4 cylinder engine - maybe not dead yet.  Also I think Porsche finished 
third place thru seventh place mainly in 968s -more of those 4 cylinders.  
Second place went to a Pontiac Firebird which is the car that ended the reign of
domination by Porsche.  Porsche still dominates endurance racing because they 
just go forever with little pit work required.  This year they suffered overall 
because IMSA shortened most of the races and the new Pontiac V8s were very 
quick.

Keep in mind that IMSA constantly changes the weight that the various cars must 
carry and will not allow any one make to dominate forever.  There are a lot of 
variables that determime who wins the titles, but one thing should be obvious 
and that is the reliability of the Porsches.  They are always there at the end.

Emil

emil@mitre.org

Yes Ferg, '84 Carreras do have a drain plug on the side of the case.
+++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++

Now for your help:

I noticed that my '84 Carrera Targa has developed a slight FE play. I was able
to trace it to the right tie rod. Specifically, the end of the tie rod that
attaches to the steering rack.  Left side checks ok.

I believe there is a rubber isolated joint, designed to eliminate road shocks 
up through the steering wheel. I looked at some aftermarket supplier catalogs
and they are not advertising the part, just outer tie rods or complete assembly. 
Does any one know if you can just buy this inner tie rod part? The car has only 
26K miles on it. I feel no play on the actual tie rod end that attaches to the
wheel carrier. 

After looking at some catalogs, I saw pictures of "Turbo" tie rods. They seem to
be threaded to the steering rack. Looking at Hayne's manual seems to confirm
this. Does any one have any experience replacing/removing the tie rod?

Specifically, what size of wrench do you need to remove the inner part from the 
rack? I'm assumming that the whole tie rod must come out to replace the inner
part. There is a rubber booth covering the part, that seems to be held in place
with a spring. Can this be peeled back to get to the tie rod/rack attachment
point?

I know I will have to do a wheel alignment after the part is replaced.

Has anyone encountered this type of FE play in their cars? 26K miles seems low 
for this malady! Any experiences with "Turbo" tie rods? Pictures seem to show
a metal/metal joint replacing the metal/rubber joint on non-Turbo models.

Your help is appreciated!

Lou Nicotra

Date: 1 Nov 93 08:15:31-0800
From: STEVE.STOMSKI@gsa2.sprint.com
Subject: 911 Video

    Just a quick note of concern re German/Eurpoean video:  A
    few months ago a friend of mine sent me a video he made (in car at
    Nurburgring, etc.).  The tape, however, would not play in my
    machine despite switching it from LP to SP to EP, etc.  Finally I
    brought it to a video repair place and the guy said European
    standard is different and that the tape needed to be
    "translatted" before I could play it. $20 later the
    copied version worked.  This may or may not be a problem with the
    911 video but I thought I'd just give the heads up (not to mention
    that if it is a problem and you can't watch it on your machine
    at home you will have to have it translated, but to do so you have
    to have it copied, which will certainly violate Porsche's
    copyright protection).

    If anyone does get this video (I am tempted), please let me know
    if it works and if it is worth the 59DM.

    Thanks

    Steven Stomski

Date: Wed, 27 Oct 1993 13:06:24 EST
From: zeigler@merck.com
Subject: colors



>re early Porsche colors...the ones being described are tangerine
>and, I think, conda green(my least favorite of all)...which is sort
>of a lime or kelly green.

>Jim


Also, the bright yellow color is just called "Light Yellow" 
There is a plate attached to the chassis that says "KUNTZHARZLACK"
with a paint code for the original color. Mine (on a 911) is on the
driver's side door frame on the hinge side.

The late '60's and early '70's colors were considered safety colors
at the time, and I think they still look great on the track. I get
strong reactions BOTH ways to my light yellow color, but no one is
ever neutral.

I missed the thread on cost of painting when I was out last week, but
you generally get what you pay for. A Porsche that obviously looks 
repainted is devalued by several thousand dollars, so it's a "pay me now 
or pay me later" issue. The value and collectability of your
car is a consideration. A $7,000 paint job on a '73 911RS might not be 
enough, whereas not too many people would spend that amount on a
high volume, non collectable model.

Often times you can work with a shop and do a lot of the labor intensive 
stuff like clean-up and disassembly/reassembly yourself. That will cut
the cost by approx. 30-40% and allow you to get an outstanding 
value-preserving paint job for the cost of painting a normal car.


Reid

I was just reading my Chicago Tribune and there is going to be this new
product on the market.  Basically, it is some type of thine film clear
adhesive coating that is put onto your windows.  When the window is hit by
a would be car jacker with a tire iron, basball bat, rock, etc...the
window does not shatter.  In fact, ll you get is this whole layer of
shattered glass that I wouldn't want to stick my finger through.  The product
is not designed to stand up forever to unlimited blows from whatever the
car jacker is swinging.

it is however designed to give you enough time to wake out of your stupor and shock and get teh pedal to the metal.  For example, only after 8 blows from
a tire iron...did a small hold large enough to put one's hand through
appear.  

the article mentioned that for a chevy cavalier, the treatment of all side
window would cost approx $200.  Other cars would vary according to surface
areas,etc.  The company did not recommend coating the front windshiel
as supposedly most cars today have a front windshield already manufactured
not to break and scatter in a million pieces, but this would obviously
not apply to old vintage porsches.

the only problem with the product?  what if u drove off a cliff in CA and
your door was stuck and the windows were jammed?  But then that's like
saying u won't were your seatbelt becasue it wrinkles your suit.  Anyways,
if i was in a bad area...i'd take my chances and get this product.  I'm
typing from memory and with little sleep...sorry about the post length.

if there is interest in this article...i can look up the company name and
post it and perhaps mail the article to those interested

eric

Date: Mon, 1 Nov 1993 14:02:02 -0500
From: sundt@cc.bellcore.com (Mark Sundt)
Subject: Re: 86' 944 turbo Wheel Shimmy

>was not there. As far as I can see all balance weights are still intact.I did
>not put the wheels on the hub exactly as they were,is this critical? I also
>used a torque wrench set @ 45 ft. lbs. per spec. ,and cross tightned all lug
>nuts. Is ths a common problem when removing wheels, for I've heard that front
>end shimmy in early 944's was a problem? If this helps I Have 16" "phone dial
>type wheels.

As you suspect, you should be able to put the wheel on any way you please
(except, of course, inside out :-).

Actually the spec. for 944/951 lug nuts is more like 95 ft. lbs. This is
likely your problem, if it is not a typo. Front end shimmy is not a common
problem on these cars.

If the lug torque is not the problem then it sounds like your brake disk
may be unbalanced. Perhaps the wheel was previously balanced "on the car"
with a dynamic balancer??? There are spring-like pieces of wire stuck up
into the inside of disks to balance them. They sometimes fall out. For me,
I've had at least one fall out of a rear disk (haven't felt any big
vibration back there, though). In your case, assuming the wheel was
dynamically balanced, there are four other possible positions for the
wheel. I would rotate it two lugs and try again. If this does help then
look for reasons to replace the front rotors (like measure the thickness of
them...).

Hope this helps.

Mark

- --
Mark Sundt, Bellcore, (908) 758-3134
sundt@cc.bellcore.com or uunet!bcr!sundt

Date: Mon, 1 Nov 1993 12:32:06 EST
From: zeigler@merck.com
Subject: wheel balance and locks



Mike said

>As far as I can see all balance weights are still intact.I did
>not put the wheels on the hub exactly as they were,is this 
critical? 

It can make a difference. Check your wheel studs to see if one is
painted for each wheel. It should line up with the closest hole to
the valve stem.

Regarding the HRS wheel locks, I used them for 2 or 3 years and
they wore out. The holes elongated around the pins and they just
fell out. I was afraid that the tool would get so bad that I 
couldn't get my wheels off, so I quit using them. This was caused
primarily by using the removal tool without bothering to tighten it 
down over the nut (impatience) I wasn't too concerned because I
never heard of anyone stealing cookie cutters - until today. Those
wheels were probably sitting in the bushes while the purps had 
another joint, trying to figure out how to get 4 studs into 5 
holes!  - or maybe they got someone else's struts the day before.

Reid

Date: Mon, 1 Nov 93 9:54:01 PST

>incl. shipment.

>System:  NTSC
>         PAL
		
*************************************************************************

If anyone is interested:

PAL refers to several similar standards, B, I, G, D, K.

In general, PAL = European Television, except for France which is SECAM.

PAL = 50hz vertical, 15625hz horizontal, 625 lines per picture frame.

NTSC = USA Television = 60hz vertical, 15750hz horizontal
      525 lines per picture frame. 

I found that PAL has a noticeably better picture than NTSC.  

So, for USA customers, the hot system would be to buy the PAL video,
a PAL VCR, a PAL compatible Monitor and a 110V to 220V transformer to
run the European gear.

Date: Wed, 27 Oct 1993 17:28:55 EST
From: zeigler@merck.com
Subject: colors



>Let me get this straight. Lime green? Ugly  orange? Pink? 
>Boy, I am sure glad I wasn't old enough to drive in the 70's

I was old enough to drive, but not to buy. Now that I am,
I chose one of those "ugly" colors.

It seems to be an American thing to only like red, white, black
or silver Porsches. In Europe, you see some really interesting 
car colors, especially a medium-dark blue that looks very sharp
on all models from 356's to present. About the only color that 
still looks strange to me is that aubergine (eggplant purple)
 - especially when combined with the op-art interior in a 928.

When people ask me what color my car is, I say "certainly yellow"
because of all the times perfect strangers have come up to me and
said "wow, that car is certainly yellow!" I get thumbs up everywhere
I go, and my wife won't drive the car because "everyone stares at her"
So, ugly as they may be to some people, Porsche created some cars that 
demand to be noticed.


 -Reid

Date: Mon, 1 Nov 1993 13:42:08 -0500
From: John N Berry <jberry@afit.af.mil>
Subject: Re: 914 Injectors


Hello!

Here's my experience w/ my 1.8L:

1)  Look out for the little O-ring seals that are seated in the manifolds.
You probably want to replace these to avoid air leaks.  I usually put a little
grease on them and placed the ring on the injector before installation.

2)  Plug the hole left by the injector!  I used a small piece of rolled up
cloth.  This prevents *crap* from finding it's way into the cylinder.  (and it
*will* get in there otherwise - see all the sandblasting stories in the 
archieves! (and I've got a *motor locked up* story to add to that!)

3)  You probably want to replace the short rubber hoses that go from the
injector to the manifold.  I didn't once...  Lucky for me it sprang a leak 
while still in the shop!  Disturbing that old rubber is always risky.  Be sure
to use something rated for the high-pressure of the system (40+ psi)

4)  Don't drop the 10 mm nuts and washers!  They can go all sorts of places in
a 914 engine compartment!

It's been about 4 years since I've done this so might have missed something.
Use some common sense and watch out for gas leaks.  You can avoid a *spurt* of 
gas when you break open the line by disabling the fuel pump and running the car
till it stops.  You will still get some spillage but it won't be as bad.

Good Luck!

John Berry
jberry@afit.af.mil

From: porschephiles-request
To: PORSCHEPHILES
Subject: 911 Video
Date: Monday, November 01, 1993 12:00AM

    Just a quick note of concern re German/Eurpoean video:  A
    few months ago a friend of mine sent me a video he made (in car at
    Nurburgring, etc.).  The tape, however, would not play in my
    machine despite switching it from LP to SP to EP, etc.  Finally I
    brought it to a video repair place and the guy said European
    standard is different and that the tape needed to be
    "translatted" before I could play it. $20 later the
    copied version worked.  This may or may not be a problem with the
    911 video but I thought I'd just give the heads up (not to mention
    that if it is a problem and you can't watch it on your machine
    at home you will have to have it translated, but to do so you have
    to have it copied, which will certainly violate Porsche's
    copyright protection).

    If anyone does get this video (I am tempted), please let me know
    if it works and if it is worth the 59DM.

    Thanks

    Steven Stomski

Date: Mon, 1 Nov 93 14:57:31 EST
From: sprattli@oceana.crd.ge.com (Rod Sprattling)
Subject: Re: Porschephiles #553 Sat Oct 30 02:00:02 CDT 1993

Christopher "LetGodSortEmOut" Welty (weltyc@cs.rpi.edu) writes:

> I'm looking to install half-tracks on my 911 targa, and a bradley-type
> 25mm chain-gun.  I've seen the kit for the machine gun (which requires
> no drilling, *whew*), but I can't find it.  Has anyone else seen it?
> Also, the half-tracks were advertised in Mercenary last month, but I
> threw out that issue and it's not in this month - did anyone else see
> it?  The nice thing about the kit I saw was it fit under the stock
> fenders (another *whew*).

Don't believe that Mercenary ad.  I got the 928 version of the
tracks, installed them, and I found out after my first 35 mph
run over and through tollbooths at Exit 24 of the NYS Thruway
that Panzerwaggon Retro did a half-assed job engineering the
bogies and tensioners that guide the top track underneath the
bodywork.  I now have a nice 2" long gouge in the new cammo
paintwork of my 87 GTS due to a collapsed bogie and loosened
tensioner, and have a bit of undercarriage welding work to
do. Sheesh.  You can order the track kit from:

	Panzerwaggon Retro, GmBH
	Gottmitunsstrasse
	D-45293 Berlin
	Germany

Be prepared to supply your Dealer In Munitions ID upon request.

Instead of the 25mm gun I installed the .223 Gatling gun on a
remote gymbal slaved to an iris tracker, such that I only need
look at a target to aim the weapon.  Acceleration is a
bit off from the weight of the continuous-feed 2000-rd belt,
but I don't really miss it.

Mileage has actually improved because now I can literally slice
through even stop-n-go traffic and maintain a constant -
and fuel-efficient - 75 mph.

As always, your milage may vary.

Rod

Date: Wed, 27 Oct 1993 13:27:09 EST
From: zeigler@merck.com
Subject: gas mileage



I also got my best mileage at 90+ (sometimes ++)
with my '73 911E (MFI)

I chalked it up to non-Newtonian physics. I never
quite reached the speed of light though.


John said:

>It's nice to have the 911's large gas tank and 
>ability to cruise at 75 - 80 for 600 miles with no need >to stop for >gas.

Either you have steamroller tires with twice normal radius, you have
a fuel cell that holds 30 gallons or you only travel downhill. I haven't 
seen any 911 that will average 40MPG!

My best mileage, cross-country was approx. 26 MPG and in mixed driving
I get 14 to 19 depending on how heavy my foot feels. My cruising range,
before I hit reserve is only about 250 miles! I'm open to suggestions,
I'd love to double my mileage.

Reid

Date: 1 Nov 1993 13:04:42 U
From: "Lance Keigwin" <lance_keigwin@engtwomac.synoptics.com>
Subject: Re: wheel locks

        Reply to:   RE>wheel locks
(other stuff deleted)
> Regarding the HRS wheel locks, I used them for 2 or 3 years and
> they wore out. The holes elongated around the pins and they just
> fell out. I was afraid that the tool would get so bad that I 
> couldn't get my wheels off, so I quit using them. This was caused
> primarily by using the removal tool without bothering to tighten it 
> down over the nut (impatience) I wasn't too concerned because I
> never heard of anyone stealing cookie cutters - until today.

Let's not get everyone confused.  The Automotion locks are the
ones with pins (that apparently wore out).  The HRS locks have
no pins at all, just a uniquely-shaped  blunt male end that won't
accept a standard lug wrench.  Place the "key" female end over
male before using a std lug wrench.

Lance.

Date: 1 Nov 93 14:57:00 CST
From: "ETD::A188898" <A188898%ETD.decnet@mdcgwy.mdc.com>
Subject: INSURANCE CLAIM, PORSCHE

OUCH!  So many Porsches getting wrecked amongst this crowd!  Careful!!

Whilst I owned my '81 928 I was whacked by another car run amok.  A few
lessons learned may be in order.  First, assuming you have reasonably good
insurance (AAA of Northern California is reputed to be quite good, I had
Farm Bureau, they think Porsche is a rare tractor but pay well, lots of
combines costs more than Porsches).  Rule #1:  Press the rental agency for
a like-replacement.  I negotiated up from an econobox (Mazda 323ish) to
a pseudo-sport sedan (Celica) with the insurance company taking the hit.
Rule #2:  Demand Porsche replacement parts.  It is your right.  Sometimes
the insurance company will push the repair shop towards non-Porsche parts
(unless its a Porsche dealer, then PCNA supposedly threatens to yank their
distributorship).  Rule #3:  Get a police record ... only somewhat non-
biased person involved at the scene, often.  Rule #4:  Ask how long the
insurance company will pay for the rental, they may well neglect to tell
you that your time ran out weeks ago!  Rule #5:  When the work is done,
ask the people involved in the repair if you oughta sell the car ASAP
or keep it, if it is marginal they will recommend sell (sometimes).

Whatever, good luck!

Oh, I think Pano just had an article entitled "Confessions of an
Insurance Man" or something close within the last few months.

John Belk
Currently '93 BMW 325i, '85 BMW 325e (lots of "M" stuff), '76 Lancia
  Scorpion (just turned in the '88 Celica Turbo All-Trac).  Black,
  silver, red, black, respectively.  928 was black, too.

US VHS tapes are recorded in NTSC format. That does NOT imply that a
European NTSC tape will work, though, as they may have a funny encoding scheme.
Check the big video stores, as they often have conversion services. I've
seen $25 quoted.

Date: Mon, 1 Nov 1993 17:28:25 EST
From: zeigler@merck.com
Subject: 911 video

Porschephiles,

Steven Stomski's format problems were caused by trying to
play a European PAL format tape on our NTSC equipment.

Most companies that distribute tapes to International
customers can supply either format (or a third format, 
SECAM, used mostly in France) 

Although the cost of conversion equipment has come down,
it's not unusual to pay $1.00 per minute of recorded
tape for format conversion, because it has to be done 
"real time"

That can be avoided by asking for 1/2" NTSC format tapes.

 - Reid Zeigler(distributor of videotapes internationally@merck.com)

Date: 01 Nov 1993 16:10:32 -0400 (EDT)
From: "Andrew J. Toth" <TOTHAND@hsdwl.utc.com>
Subject: Re: 914 Injectors

    
    Greetings!

	John Berry had some very good info on injectors.  I'll add my bit.

|  2)  Plug the hole left by the injector!  I used a small piece of rolled up
|  cloth.  This prevents *crap* from finding it's way into the cylinder.  (and it
|  *will* get in there otherwise - see all the sandblasting stories in the 
|  archieves! (and I've got a *motor locked up* story to add to that!)

    Before you remove the injectors, use compressed air to remove any
    accumulation of *schmeg*. 

|  3)  You probably want to replace the short rubber hoses that go from the
|  injector to the manifold.  I didn't once...  Lucky for me it sprang a leak 
|  while still in the shop!  Disturbing that old rubber is always risky.  Be sure
|  to use something rated for the high-pressure of the system (40+ psi)

    Porsche is still honoring a recall on these rubber hoses which have been
    known to cause fires in the engine compartment.  Check with your local
    dealer to see if this service has been done on your car.  (yYou'll need
    to bring your VIN.)  While you're at it, another recall is to install a 
    cover over the battery.

    Good luck!


- --------------------------------------------------------------------------------
    Andrew J. Toth                             Katana Pilot
    tothand@hsdwl.utc.com                                  ===o&o>

Date: Tue, 2 Nov 93 01:39 GMT
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: 86' 944 turbo Wheel Shimmy

Michael -

The shimmy problem really existed only for the MY82 and 83 944 cars.  It
was largely cured with the advent of power steering for MY84, and was
also helped by an updated so-called "flexible" tie rod design.

Correct torque for your wheels is 96 ftlb, and the fact that you've
undertorqued them may be the root cause of your problem.  In fact, when
mounting them you should first snug all five lug nuts to, say, 20 to 30
ftlb, then retorque to 96.  This is important because it centres the lug
hole at each lug bolt; note the conical shape to the nut's bearing
surface.  It's also important that you always tighten in a "star"
pattern, like drawing a pentagram.

Hope this helps,

Kevin

> So, for USA customers, the hot system would be to buy the PAL video,
> a PAL VCR, a PAL compatible Monitor and a 110V to 220V transformer to
> run the European gear.

Still won't work - you need an AC<>AC converted or motor/generator 
affair to convert the U.S. 60Hz to 50Hz for the power supply, or buy a 
DC powered TV.

Better yet, wait a few more years and get a new wide-screen digital 
HDTV stero hi-fi system.  Of course, you'll have to sell a car to afford it!

Date: Mon,  1 Nov 93 21:41:32 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: 911 Video

>     Finally I
>     brought it to a video repair place and the guy said European
>     standard is different and that the tape needed to be
>     "translatted" before I could play it. $20 later the
>     copied version worked.

Ah ha!  I knew I'd use this TV knowledge someday!

We in the U.S. have NTSC (Never The Same Color - oops, I mean National 
Television Standards Committee) television, and in most of western 
Europe they have PAL (Phase Alternate Lines).  Ex-USSR and other 
eastern block countries have SECAM.  Each needs to be converted at a 
special video lab.

Make sure the tape they sell you is NTSC format!

		JBS

Date: Tue, 2 Nov 93 11:34:44 EST
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: Hazet Assistant Toolbox


The Hazet Assistant is a beautiful light-blue colored folding toolbox
which is on wheels and has three collapsing trays and side posts which
collapses down on itself into a very small package. The top shelf also
doubles as a temporary work surface in the upright position and the
numerous small compartments in the thing can hold most of your
frequently used hand tools. It (like other Hazet tools and chests) is
*very* high quality and it's expensive, at around $450. I bought one 2
years ago and have never regretted it...until now.

I just got the latest ENCO tool catalogue and I was browsing through
until I came to page 261 and I gasped! There it is, a picture of *the*
Hazet Assistant, sans the Hazet name or logo, but looking identical
with a matching description for an unbelievable price of ... $64.75.
For those of you who know the Hazet Assistant you're drooling now and
you're calling ENCO (800-873-3626). But wait, I called ENCO and they
said it's a Taiwanese copy but exactly like the original. Has anyone
seen this copy? How is the quality? Does it compare to the original?

- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

went for price last time and will pass on Goodyears this time.

Date: Tue, 2 Nov 1993 10:34:06 -0800
From: ken@esca.com (My "BUG" can beat your "bug"!)
Subject: RE: Street tires, again


Hi there,

I'm new to this mail list but I thought I'd add my two cents worth on tires for
street use.  I am an avid autocrosser and have been playing a bit with tires
for the last 4 years.  When I started, I was on a tight budget so I tried to
get a tire to cover all aspects of my driving.  The Bridgestone RE-71 standard
compound tire was my choice.  It was reasonable on the track and seemed decent
for the street.  But, it only lasted about 10,000 miles (< 1 year).  Must have
something to do with my racing.  

Anyway, the next year, I decided that I'd get another set of rims for track.  I
put some excellent Yokohama A008 RS tires on them (They are incredible and
their already out of date)!  For the street, a fellow autocrosser (one of the
top in our region) and performance tire shop owner recommended that I try the
Michelin XGT-V which is a V speed rated tire (for speeds < 150 mph).  He said
that it was very good (for a street tire) in wet and dry and that it would last
much longer.

He was correct.  I have found it to be a very good street tire.  It allows me
to have fun on the street while not going through a set of tires every year. 
So far, they have lasted 2 1/2 years.  The rears are getting close to
replacement time but the fronts still have at least 50% tread!  I have been
very happy with this set-up and would recommend it to others.

I have to admit though, I don't know if the XGT-H4 is a follow on to the XGT-V
or what.  You might ask your local tire dealer what the differences are.  Good
luck in your hunt and I hope this info is helpful.

 -Ken

ken@esca.com
White 1978 911 SC Targa w/tail

------------------------------

Date: Tue, 2 Nov 93 09:28:21 -0600
From: Kyle Ehler <kehler@daisy.wichitaks.NCR.COM>
Subject: RE; 914 injectors

>Subject: 914 Injectors
>
>Also, can anyone reccommend a place that will *thour-
>oughly* clean the injectors (horribly toxic chemicals
>at N psi, where N is a *large* number) in the South SF Bay
>area?
>
>--DD
 Dave,
 Greetings -its been a while.
I trust you've suffered thru my postings...     =:>
On your injectors,
I been reading the hotrod list and in the depths of PE (Performance
Engineering) John De Armond's rag has an article on testing and cleaning
injectors.  The article goes into detail of constructing an electronic
circuit for exercising an injector whilst gas/techron is cycled thru it 
at ~40psi pressure.  The article 'Fuel Injector Flow bench' is well written
and might appear in the early issues (my issue is a miniature sample 
I picked up at a PCA tech session last year).

There is also a BBS # for details and perhaps the article itself, that
number is: 501-985-0059 'Courts of Chaos BBS'.  John can be reached at:
jgd@dixie.com

I'm looking at the article right now...VERY simple circuit, less than 10
parts, couple of darlington's for switching the injector coil in and out
of circuit.  Uses a PC's parallel port to control the pulse width, rep 
rates, etc. this could be a great diagnostic and cleaning tool.

Xylene (carb cleaner) is very easy to get and works great on cleaning
gink out of fuel systems and other places, its possible your inlet
screens are filled.  These are in the injector body's fuel nipple
if memory serves, and can be exposed by removing the fuel line.
Hope this was of use.
- -- 
  -kyle
  <kehler@daisy.wichitaks.ncr.com>
   73 914 2.0         now rust guard black (Phoenix red L32K)
   77 Olds Cutlass    Rustic Maroon musclecar
   83 Chevy Cavalier  White (wifewagen)
   81 Yamaha DT 175   Red/black  Almost a trials 'plonker'.
       Phoenix in more ways than one...

Date: Tue, 2 Nov 93 15:47:19 EST
From: lmcanro@LMC.ericsson.se (Andre Rotchel)
Subject: RE: Street tires, again


Hi there,

I would like to comment on the Michelin XGT H4 (215/60HR15,
four season). I have them on my 944 ('86) since 
last year. They are excellent in the Canadian winter 
(which is the harshest you can possibly have). They 
are also excellent on wet. However, on dry, I can not 
take the tight turns I used to enjoy taking with my
old Yokohama Y 352s. 

Overall though, they are an excellent durable (treadwear 
is minimal up to now) COMPROMISE for the winter inclined
climates. Recently, I went from New York to Montreal at
90 miles/hour for most of the time under very heavy rain.
The tires felt very secure during deceleration, change 
of lanes etc... All other cars, including a 928 S4 
could not keep up with my speed. I don't feel I was 
taking a risk because of the feeling of control these 
tires gave. 

Regards, Andre Rotchel,
Ericsson communications R&D,
Montreal, Canada.

Date: 2 Nov 1993 14:41:51 U
From: "Lance Keigwin" <lance_keigwin@engtwomac.synoptics.com>
Subject: 911 street tires

                       Subject:                               Time:2:27 PM
  OFFICE MEMO          911 street tires                       Date:11/2/93
I would like to hear from other 911 owners about their "impressions"
of popular performance tires.  I am mostly concerned about dry pavement
performance (non-autocross) since the rainy season is pretty short here
and I either don't drive 911's in the rain or I take it easy. 

I currently have RE71's on my wife's '89 911 and D40/M2's on my '88 turbo
look.  The wear and price is better on the D40's but the RE71's seem stickier.
I had P7's on an '84 cab and didn't like them much.  Also, is it worth it to go
for Z-rating?  I usually do but I rarely drive over 120 and mostly stay under
100.

What would are recommendations for a nice compromise between wear,
price and performance?  I would like to get 20-25k or better on a set.
Thanks.

Lance.

Date: Tue, 2 Nov 93 21:04:08 CST
From: cork@cs.rice.edu (Corky Cartwright)
Subject: Porsche street tires

I second Scott Winders' recommendation of Dunlop SP8000's.  I have
tried Goodyear Eagles (OE), Dunlop D40/M2's, and Dunlop SP8000's
on my 951S.  The SP8000's are the best tire of the group, with very
good grip in both the wet and the dry.  I also like the fact that they are 
available in size 225/45-16.

Corky Cartwright

Date: Tue, 2 Nov 93 23:32:50 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: Street tires, again

My votes, for the air-cooled faction:

911, et al.:  Yoko AVS Intermediates. Good enough for track and autox, but
	mainly a street tire. Expect to buy new rears every year, new
	fronts every 3. I'm on my 4th set of rears, second set of fronts.
	Typically on 12-15K street miles, a couple of autox, and one track
	day per year. I usually run the second set of wheels on the autox
	and track days, but sometimes, I get lazy.... Doesn't hurt my times
	too much, either...

914:	Yoko AVS U+4. Slightly rounder shoulders seem to work a bit
	better. Have also used Pirelli P600 with some pleasure, P44s	
	and P6s with some distaste.

356:	CN36 (Yeah, right...) Actually, Yoko 509s work well, as do
	Comp T/As in 185/65x15

I hear that Michelien and Dunlop are great on WaterPumpers. I have no
opinion on that. Well, you *know* what I mean... 8{P

Oh, all recomendations take into account driving hard and fast in the
rain. Frequently. This is Houston, you know...

Stan

>    911 video but I thought I'd just give the heads up (not to mention
>    that if it is a problem and you can't watch it on your machine
>    at home you will have to have it translated, but to do so you have
>    to have it copied, which will certainly violate Porsche's
>    copyright protection).
>
>    If anyone does get this video (I am tempted), please let me know
>    if it works and if it is worth the 59DM.

It does not work if it is not in NTSC format, we Europeans mostly have
PAL. I'd guess Porsche is making this tape in NTSC too.

Some of the more modern VCR's *do* have switching possibillities between
different standards though!

Date: Mon,  1 Nov 93 21:41:32 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: 911 Video

>     Finally I
>     brought it to a video repair place and the guy said European
>     standard is different and that the tape needed to be
>     "translatted" before I could play it. $20 later the
>     copied version worked.

Ah ha!  I knew I'd use this TV knowledge someday!

We in the U.S. have NTSC (Never The Same Color - oops, I mean National 
Television Standards Committee) television, and in most of western 
Europe they have PAL (Phase Alternate Lines).  Ex-USSR and other 
eastern block countries have SECAM.  Each needs to be converted at a 
special video lab.

Make sure the tape they sell you is NTSC format!

		JBS

Date: Tue, 2 Nov 93 11:34:44 EST
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: Hazet Assistant Toolbox


The Hazet Assistant is a beautiful light-blue colored folding toolbox
which is on wheels and has three collapsing trays and side posts which
collapses down on itself into a very small package. The top shelf also
doubles as a temporary work surface in the upright position and the
numerous small compartments in the thing can hold most of your
frequently used hand tools. It (like other Hazet tools and chests) is
*very* high quality and it's expensive, at around $450. I bought one 2
years ago and have never regretted it...until now.

I just got the latest ENCO tool catalogue and I was browsing through
until I came to page 261 and I gasped! There it is, a picture of *the*
Hazet Assistant, sans the Hazet name or logo, but looking identical
with a matching description for an unbelievable price of ... $64.75.
For those of you who know the Hazet Assistant you're drooling now and
you're calling ENCO (800-873-3626). But wait, I called ENCO and they
said it's a Taiwanese copy but exactly like the original. Has anyone
seen this copy? How is the quality? Does it compare to the original?

 - --

Date: Tue, 2 Nov 1993 10:34:06 -0800
From: ken@esca.com (My "BUG" can beat your "bug"!)
Subject: RE: Street tires, again


Hi there,

I'm new to this mail list but I thought I'd add my two cents worth on tires for
street use.  I am an avid autocrosser and have been playing a bit with tires
for the last 4 years.  When I started, I was on a tight budget so I tried to
get a tire to cover all aspects of my driving.  The Bridgestone RE-71 standard
compound tire was my choice.  It was reasonable on the track and seemed decent
for the street.  But, it only lasted about 10,000 miles (< 1 year).  Must have
something to do with my racing.  

Anyway, the next year, I decided that I'd get another set of rims for track.  I
put some excellent Yokohama A008 RS tires on them (They are incredible and
their already out of date)!  For the street, a fellow autocrosser (one of the
top in our region) and performance tire shop owner recommended that I try the
Michelin XGT-V which is a V speed rated tire (for speeds < 150 mph).  He said
that it was very good (for a street tire) in wet and dry and that it would last
much longer.

He was correct.  I have found it to be a very good street tire.  It allows me
to have fun on the street while not going through a set of tires every year. 
So far, they have lasted 2 1/2 years.  The rears are getting close to
replacement time but the fronts still have at least 50% tread!  I have been
very happy with this set-up and would recommend it to others.

I have to admit though, I don't know if the XGT-H4 is a follow on to the XGT-V
or what.  You might ask your local tire dealer what the differences are.  Good
luck in your hunt and I hope this info is helpful.

 -Ken

ken@esca.com
White 1978 911 SC Targa w/tail

Date: Tue, 2 Nov 93 09:28:21 -0600
From: Kyle Ehler <kehler@daisy.wichitaks.NCR.COM>
Subject: RE; 914 injectors

>Subject: 914 Injectors
>
>Also, can anyone reccommend a place that will *thour-
>oughly* clean the injectors (horribly toxic chemicals
>at N psi, where N is a *large* number) in the South SF Bay
>area?
>
>--DD
 Dave,
 Greetings -its been a while.
I trust you've suffered thru my postings...     =:>
On your injectors,
I been reading the hotrod list and in the depths of PE (Performance
Engineering) John De Armond's rag has an article on testing and cleaning
injectors.  The article goes into detail of constructing an electronic
circuit for exercising an injector whilst gas/techron is cycled thru it 
at ~40psi pressure.  The article 'Fuel Injector Flow bench' is well written
and might appear in the early issues (my issue is a miniature sample 
I picked up at a PCA tech session last year).

There is also a BBS # for details and perhaps the article itself, that
number is: 501-985-0059 'Courts of Chaos BBS'.  John can be reached at:
jgd@dixie.com

I'm looking at the article right now...VERY simple circuit, less than 10
parts, couple of darlington's for switching the injector coil in and out
of circuit.  Uses a PC's parallel port to control the pulse width, rep 
rates, etc. this could be a great diagnostic and cleaning tool.

Xylene (carb cleaner) is very easy to get and works great on cleaning
gink out of fuel systems and other places, its possible your inlet
screens are filled.  These are in the injector body's fuel nipple
if memory serves, and can be exposed by removing the fuel line.
Hope this was of use.
 - -- 
  -kyle
  <kehler@daisy.wichitaks.ncr.com>
   73 914 2.0         now rust guard black (Phoenix red L32K)
   77 Olds Cutlass    Rustic Maroon musclecar
   83 Chevy Cavalier  White (wifewagen)
   81 Yamaha DT 175   Red/black  Almost a trials 'plonker'.
       Phoenix in more ways than one...

Date: Tue, 2 Nov 93 15:47:19 EST
From: lmcanro@LMC.ericsson.se (Andre Rotchel)
Subject: RE: Street tires, again


Hi there,

I would like to comment on the Michelin XGT H4 (215/60HR15,
four season). I have them on my 944 ('86) since 
last year. They are excellent in the Canadian winter 
(which is the harshest you can possibly have). They 
are also excellent on wet. However, on dry, I can not 
take the tight turns I used to enjoy taking with my
old Yokohama Y 352s. 

Overall though, they are an excellent durable (treadwear 
is minimal up to now) COMPROMISE for the winter inclined
climates. Recently, I went from New York to Montreal at
90 miles/hour for most of the time under very heavy rain.
The tires felt very secure during deceleration, change 
of lanes etc... All other cars, including a 928 S4 
could not keep up with my speed. I don't feel I was 
taking a risk because of the feeling of control these 
tires gave. 

Regards, Andre Rotchel,
Ericsson communications R&D,
Montreal, Canada.

                       Subject:                               Time:2:27 PM
  OFFICE MEMO          911 street tires                       Date:11/2/93

of popular performance tires.  I am mostly concerned about dry pavement
performance (non-autocross) since the rainy season is pretty short here
and I either don't drive 911's in the rain or I take it easy.

100.

price and performance?  I would like to get 20-25k or better on a set.
Thanks.

Lance.

Date: Tue, 2 Nov 93 23:32:50 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: Street tires, again

My votes, for the air-cooled faction:

911, et al.:  Yoko AVS Intermediates. Good enough for track and autox, but
	mainly a street tire. Expect to buy new rears every year, new
	fronts every 3. I'm on my 4th set of rears, second set of fronts.
	Typically on 12-15K street miles, a couple of autox, and one track
	day per year. I usually run the second set of wheels on the autox
	and track days, but sometimes, I get lazy.... Doesn't hurt my times
	too much, either...

914:	Yoko AVS U+4. Slightly rounder shoulders seem to work a bit
	better. Have also used Pirelli P600 with some pleasure, P44s	
	and P6s with some distaste.

356:	CN36 (Yeah, right...) Actually, Yoko 509s work well, as do
	Comp T/As in 185/65x15

I hear that Michelien and Dunlop are great on WaterPumpers. I have no
opinion on that. Well, you *know* what I mean... 8{P

Oh, all recomendations take into account driving hard and fast in the
rain. Frequently. This is Houston, you know...

Stan

Date: Wed, 03 Nov 93 11:06:59 GMT+1
From: cc <cc@cs-po.avi.af.mil>
Subject: Re[2]: re 911 video

     Re: 60->50Hz conversion: In most cases, overkill. Virtually all the TV 
     or VCR systems we have over here are not 50/60 Hz dependent, and in 
     most cases will work on various line frequencies. At worst, the little 
     clock on the VCR will not be right... but nobody knows how to set 
     those, anyway. 
     
     You may not even have to convert the voltage. (Adaptive power supplies 
     are coming.) But you will probably need new AC plugs on the power 
     cords. Brits don't match Germans, who don't match Italians.. and 
     nobody matches North American!
     
     Pete Jones

Date: Wed, 3 Nov 1993 08:50:07 EST
From: zeigler@merck.com
Subject: street tires



I had Yokohama A008-R TU's on my 911 for 4 years (25,000 mi.)
of non-track use. They were excellent tires in wet or dry 
conditions - never hydroplaned even when they looked like slicks.
Never tried them in snow, but they have so little tread, I don't
imagine they would work.

Currently using RE-71's and IMHO they're not in the same class.
I've noticed that they "chirp and complain" under heavy braking
and I've drifted around some backroad turns that didn't even seem
fast with the A008's. They do *look* a lot better though, I don't
have people telling me my tires are bald all the time. And I'm
sure the RE-71's must be better in the wet, although they seem 
the same.

 - Reid

Date: Wed, 03 Nov 93 08:57:00 MST
From: Frith Robert <p26051@gegpo6.geg.mot.com>
Subject: re:  tires


last year i was looking for a set of tires for my 83 911SC.  i had comp 
t/a's and did not like them at all.  after a lot of checking around, 
including with various tire reps and club members, i decided that dunlop 
sp8000 would be the best all around for combined street and occational 
xcrossing - - mileage, handling and price.  however, i got a great price on 
a set of michelin xgt-v.  these were my second choice anyway so i bought 
them.  i've been real happy with them, both it dry and wet.  although 
phoenix is not thought of as "wet", when it rains here it really rains, the 
streets flood and all that stuff.  no experience with them in snow, however. 
 my comp t/a's were terrible in snow.

i bought "v" rated since "v" is rated at sustained up to 140mph.  i rarely 
drive sustained speeds higher that that even on ariz back roads 8:).  "z" 
rated are quite a bit more expensive.

Date: 3 Nov 1993 11:21:33 U
From: "Lance Keigwin" <lance_keigwin@engtwomac.synoptics.com>
Subject: Re: Hazet Assistant Toolbox

        Reply to:   RE>Hazet Assistant Toolbox
I stopped by Enco to look at their Hazet Toolcart knockoff ($64.75).  I had a
bit
of a handicap since I'd never seen the German Hazet (~$450) but John Colasante
provided me with a very good detailed description.

FYI, the toolcart is designed as a 13"X26" tub on wheels with two tower tubes
to allow two trays (and a third flat work surface) to be lifted and fixed into
positions for holding small tools.  When expanded it stands almost 3' tall.
It is constructed of steel and weighs 30lbs.

This asian knockoff seemed to me to be priced about right.  Functionally, it
appears to be much like the German version described, except that the two
towers supports are bolted in place and not telescopic so there's no way the
unit can be folded up for storing in trunk.  They seem a little wobbily too.
Nothing about it suggested very high quality, although I couldn't see any fatal
flaws.  The tub finish did not look scratch resistant but hey, it's a toolcart.
The steel towers and trays were not flimsy gauge, but neither did they seem
strong enough to stand on for instance (why you would stand on it is not the
point).  The rubber locking wheels seemed of average quality.  The tray
dividers were definitely cheap and easily bendable.

I am having trouble understanding what could turn this into a $450 cart
(but then I have trouble with $70 911 rearview mirrors, $4,000 targa tops,
$800 cab boots, etc.).  My guess is that the German unit is far superior but
for those on a budget, or those who wonder whether the design is useful,
take a chance and risk little.

Lance. (lkeigwin@synoptics.com)
'88 turbo look targa, diamond blue
'89 coupe, metallic black
'85 535i, everyday grey




------------------------------

Date: Wed, 3 Nov 93 09:21:35 PST
From: prado@snowtires.corp.sun.com (Pete Rado)
Subject: RE: Street tires, again

My 87 911 originally came with D40's.  When they wore out at about
18K miles I put on XGTV's.  At 33K miles they still have plenty of
tread.  I have used them on the track, but got tired of having to
rebalance tires after each event so my second set of wheels/tires
are RE71's.


I hate the RE71's and will replace them just as soon as they wear out.
They are very noisy when pushed and I have swapped ends with no warning
a couple of times, even with lower tire pressures.  This never happend
with the D40's or XGT's.

For a street tire, I think one of the best features of the XGTV is
its softer sidewall.  The ride over  the road irregularities is
much smoother than with the other 2 tires.  When I put the RE71's on
to drive to the track, I feel every stone and expansion joint.  Personally,
I find this very annoying. 

So, I think the XGTV is a great street tire...good wear, excellent
ride and good grip, braking and turning, wet and dry.

Never Drove in the snow...in New England I parked the car for the winter,
in California, I take my 4Runner up to the mountains.

Now, If I could decide on a good track tire without moving out of the
box stock class....

Pete

Hi 944'ers,

Paul and I have been discussing this 944 hatch issue for quite some time and

is the piece that attaches to the roof, and holds the rear hatch glass.

to the car are in place......amazing what time and rough roads do to

happened to look there when I was removing an old parking sticker.

Date: Wed, 3 Nov 93 15:44:36 GMT
From: ben@wet.sbi.com (Ben Evans)
Subject: Re: Tyre choice


I have Yoko A008p front and back on my 911. I have been very
happy with these. The grip is outstanding in my opinion. Some
people suggest that they have a very high wear rate, but mine have
over 6000 miles and are less than half worn. I think that on
a very powerful 911 (mine is a 3.2 Carrera) they would get
scrubbed off much faster, but for regular use they are fine IMHO.

I did take the car to Silverstone recently, and they held up very well.
At the end of each session I checked them out and was amazed a how
sticky the rubber gets at track temperatures.

my '93 MX-6.  I drive 70 miles/day on an interstate consisting of numerous

>Anybody have anything good to say about the RE71s?

As someone correctly pointed out, my comment about the
RE-71's not being as "sticky" as A008-R TU's (basically
track tires) wasn't entirely fair. I'm basically very
happy with the Bridgstones, just more cautious about
car control. They're maybe even a little safer for the
street once you get used to them, because unlike the 
Yokahamas, they telegraph the limits to you well before 
you get there. $$$

Date: Wed, 3 Nov 93 18:03:49 CST
From: cork@cs.rice.edu (Corky Cartwright)
Subject: Street tires

Among major brand (Yokohama, BFG, Bridgestone, Michelin, Dunlop)
VR and ZR rated tires, there are only two models that I have heard 
consistently bad things about:  the Goodyear Eagle (gatorback)
and the Dunlop D4OM (not D40/M2).  In both cases, the manufacturers
have introduced new designs: the Goodyear GS-C and the Dunlop D40/M2.  
If someone gave me a new set of RE71's for my car, I would certainly use 
them.  Expedia's would be even better (assuming they came in the right sizes),
but I would be happy to receive a gift set of RE71's.

- -- Corky Cartwright

Date: Wed, 3 Nov 1993 16:26:43 -0800 (PST)
From: LAFORCE@XENON.ARC.NASA.GOV
Subject: re:tires

Okay, I'll put in my $.02 too.

I've got just under 30k miles on a set of yoko a008's (that's plural,
not model).

They are great dry.  They are also the first tire I have ever had
truely hydroplane.  I don't mean they got a little vague, they felt
like a boat rudder, only more squishy.

I have a second set of wheels for competition, so these only see 
street use, which is largely highway.  I'll push them a little 
but it's kind of rare: throttle steer the occasional on ramp, pull 
away from the occasional motorcycle on skyline, but mostly I drive
fast and loose off the highway.

Next question is:

Anyone got a suggestion for a high mileage tire?  I got a good deal 
on the yoko's but don't really feel like spending $800 to replace 
them.

I've asked this before, but there seems to be some new blood on 
the list lately, perhaps with some new suggestions.

I'm looking for lower cost/mile.  Current favorite is a Falken.
Particularly since I just had a student at the zone auto-x
school drive **very** well on a set.  These had a treadwear
rating of 220 (160 on the a008).

Comments?

Suggestions?

> Hi All...

> I notice Stan referred to changing rear tires twice as often as 
> fronts on 911's.  That IS the way they wear... but when I tried this a few
> years ago the results were scary!  Eventually I figured that the rears
> were riding on new, tall, squishy tread blocks, while the fronts were
> riding on old, short, hard ones... and the mismatch in the slip angle
> vs. side force curves made the rear feel *really* loose, like driving on
> jello.  Since then I've changed all four together.  Am I an old lady, or
> have others noticed this, and only Der List Meister's super skills keep
> him from spinning because of it?  My bank account wouldn't mind buying
> 25% fewer tires, that's for sure.

Date: 4 Nov 93 08:39:00 CST
From: "ETD::A188898" <A188898%ETD.decnet@mdcgwy.mdc.com>
Subject: A/C Glovebox

Porsche Peers, even my '81 928 had an air conditioned glovebox!  All that
"A/C" consisted of was an additional vent opening that happened to be in
the glovebox.  No high tech required, just room behind the dash to route
the air.  And this was factory stock, not a hatchet job.

Sometimes, low tech masquerades as high tech.  Frequently with the aid of
elegant engineering of a simple concept.

Cheers.  John Belk.

insects come by the name centipede - not because they have a hundred feet,
but because most people can't count above fourteen." G. C. Lichtenberg

  [included note of Nov  3, zeigler@merck.com:]
} 
} All the traffic about tires made me think about wheels.
} Anderson says that all the "imitation" Fuchs alloys are
} #$%*  Is that still true? Performance Products now has a
} higher grade of "lookalike" that is pressure cast - still
} not forged (at least not in the mettalurgical sense) but
} supposedly stronger and straighter than gravity cast.
} 

The reason for the problems with FUCHS replicas is that forged metal si
SO much stronger than cast (pressure or not).  When companies made the
fuchs replicas they simply copies the fuchs design without reinforcing
for lower strength material.

The wheels that are pressure cast replicas of other pressure cast wheels
are generally OK because they were engineered with that material in mind
and are appropriately reinforced.

Imagine taking a '60s steel wheel from a US passenger car, only when you
made it you just took a cast of the steel wheel and poured in some moten
Aluminum.  You could prob. bend it by stepping on the rim.  The problem
with the copying of fuchs is just that.

Porsche has only ever made 3 popular forged wheel styles - Fuchs, 928
flat style wheels, and the 951S forged wheels.

If you are still not convinced that this is the case compare a 944 turbo
cup (magnesium) wheel to a 1987 phone dial of the same width (8"). If you do so
you'll notice that the 8" wheel actually LOOKS like a 7" wheel from the
outside because the material in the webbing is about 3/8" thicker and
the whole center sticks out that much farther.  We noticed this when we
put on wheel fans. The front 8" magnesium wheel really doesn't accept a
fan since the center sticks out farther than the rim.  The rear 9" mag
wheel is nearly identical to the 951 (87) 8" wheel and accepts the same
wheel fan.

FYI,
	Steve


- --

From: INGRAM_GLENN/HPBOI1_03@hpdmd48.boi.hp.com
Subject: RE71....and the RE71R's

Well, couldn't resist commenting on the RE's....

Well, there are 2 types- the RE71 is a street tire that (like Drew mentioned)
is a very good tire, especially in the wet.  Good wear, with excellent
feedback at the limit (they are not twitchy at all).

NOW FOR THE GOOD NEWS: The 'other type' is...

The RE71R ('R' stands for race, i guess...) is an EXCELLENT HIGH PERFORMANCE
TIRE.  PERIOD!!! I used to run these tires and the have excellent stick &
wear great!!!  As for how they compare to YOKO's (please understand that
I am a YOKOHAMA contract driver [no big deal...I get free tires for my
AX car ((was a 911, now a BMW-bummer here...))] so I will tell the
trueth about these tires..), the RE71R's are 1-2 seconds faster at Sears
Point (ITS road racing on a Porsche 944) than the YOKO RS's.  This is actual
data for a company that sets up 944/911's (Project Racing Services, who's
owner MARK (forgot last mane) @ 707-996-7719 will talk to you about all
the testing they do...).  He has said that these tires are great and wear
very well, with little effect of heat cycling (they are using Goodyear
GS-CS's now for dry, because they are about .5 seconds faster, per lap,
than the RE71R's, but are only good for ***** 2 ****** heat cycles!!!, then
they go away BIG TIME (which is VERY $$$$$$) ).

ALSO, remeber that there are **2** tread patterns with the RE71R's:
there is a regular thread block pattern and a 'slick' pattern (can't
for the life of me remember the designation's.. Bill Buetow could tell
us all about the Bridgestones...he used to be a contract driver for
them for SCCA AX'ing...).  The open tread is a great RAIN (hence 'street'
tire) and the 'slick' pattern (even more of a slick as it wears or is shaved
down to 3/32's) is a great dry, track tire.


Please understand they are NOT AX tires, like the YOKO RSII's, so you will
not get the quick heat up & stick like the II's, but they are very easy to
drive and are a great compromise!!


Personally, I realy like these tires and the are fantastic in the RAIN!!

Here are my humble opinions on tires:

Autocross:     YOKOHAMA RSII's (if you run SCCA & can win $$$$)
               BFG 230's (new compound for RR'ing, with new AX
                          compound out by December '93- great
                          tire: easy to drive at the limit; lot's
                          of feedback at the limit and great wear
                          [the new AX compound should wear much better
                           than the old BGF 204/206 compounded tires]

Track:
    Dry with unlimited budget:  GOODYEAR GS-CS's

    Dry or wet with a budget:   RE71R's-   1st
                                008 RS's-  2nd
Both world's-
    Wet Track/Street:           RE71R's-   1st
                                Goodyear
                                 GS-C's-   2nd
                                 (a great rain/street tire- got a ride with
                                  a guy in a 928 at Las Vegas & was REALLY
                                  impressed with the wet performance of
                                  these tires- but they do wear reeeeal
                                  fast if you run lot's of toe out
                                  &/or camber... beware since they are
                                  VERY EXPENSIVE....)

These are my 2c worth and as usual, are IMHO only....

Date: Thu, 4 Nov 93 13:00:44 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 356b mods what do you think

The "big bore" kit is a new set of pistons and cylinders from NPR
(Nippon Piston Ring) and is, as of August, NO LONGER AVAILABLE. Any
kits you find have "appreciated" significantly since then, and many
people (including Tweeks) advertise them but can't deliver. Near as I
can tell, Harry Pellow bought up most of the stock (damn it -- I hate
doing business with Harry!).

Otherwise, it's the right thing to do.

Weber carbs are also good to do -- but buy them from PMO (Porsche Mail
Order -- ads in the usual Porsche mags) instead of anyone else. You'll
save several hundred dollars.

The stock carbs, even Solexes, have the problem of many parts not being
available. NLA in Nevada has a Solex rebuild service that puts your carb 
back to just about perfect condition for $200 each. Got mine back a week
or so ago, and they *look* GREAT. Haven't put them on an engine yet, though.
But for the Weber, I can reach into my race gear and re-jet or re-venturi
as needed to get it to work. And I can get these parts all day long. Solex
parts, yeah, right. You can make some of them, you can adapt some Weber
parts, but youcan't buy lots of them any longer...

Stan

Date: Thu, 4 Nov 93 10:51:58 CST
From: James F. Young <young@teco.rice.edu>
Subject: Re: 356b mods

Wow! A 356 question; I finally get to say something on the net.

I, like many others, have used the NPR "big bore" 1720 cc pistons and
cylinders on a 356.  There is, of course, no boring out of the engine:
the new pistons and cylinders just replace the originals and you use
the old heads (assuming they are in good shape, which is often an
unjustified assumption).  The main reason for doing this is not really
the "big bore" part, but rather the "low price" compared to Porsche
replacement parts.  I am not familiar with the Tweeks kit, I just
bought the NPR set at a local supplier; nothing else is needed except
some gaskets.

BUT, those gaskets can be very critical, because some, the copper rings
used between the block and the cylinders, determine the compression
ratio, and in some cases, lack of same will cause pistons to make
intimate contact with head & valves, which is bad news.  The good news
is that you can choose the spacers to get pretty much whatever
compression ratio you want.  You need to know the displacement of your
heads--especially if they have been flycut--and then make some
calculations, pick spacers, assemble, and CHECK BY MEASUREMENT before
firing up.  The best guide for this is the Maestro's books, worth every
$ in both guidance and entertainment.

By the way, don't wait too long to get the NPR set; word has it (see
the Maestro's recent column in the 356 Registry) that NPR is ceasing
production of this item, so it will be back to the expensive Porsche
part.  The Maestro is stocking up, and sells BALANCED sets, which is
a nice advantage.

Also, don't expect this to be a terrificly "hot setup". I guess some
performance improvement is likely, depending on how you set up the
compression ratio, and if you get the cam reground, but it is not going
to be astounding; perhaps not even noticible to the casual observer.
For a street car, and with today's gas, it is probably not wise to go
above a compression ratio of 9.1, which if I remember correctly, is
more than a 356 C normal engine, less than a SC engine, but about the
same as 912s.  Others on the net may have different experience.

If you are doing all this, it is a good time to magnaflux/regrind the
cam, magnaflux & balance the rods, new rod bearings, of course.  And on
no account use the old rod bolts/nuts, only new.  But then, you probably
know all that; if not, consult THE BOOK.

Can't help you regarding Webers.  Once I learned how to rebuild,
adjust, and balance the Zeniths, I had too much invested to switch!

Good luck,  Jim Young, young@rice.edu, Houston, TX.

Date: Thu, 4 Nov 1993 13:41:00 -0800 (PST)
From: LAFORCE@XENON.ARC.NASA.GOV
Subject: re: 911sc window fogger

Ken writes:

No one seems to be saying anything about the '78/'79 vintage 911 SC defrosting
problems.  I have a '78 SC Targa and I'm constantly fighting fog-up when I
first turn on my defrost in the morning.  As the system heats up, it appears
that there is water inside that begins to turn to steam and proceeds to fog up
the windows.

*********************************************************************

Ken,

You didn't list symptoms in the original post.

I think I know what's wrong (not that I know the symptoms).

There is a hose/duct between the heat exchanger and the ducting comming 
from the fan in the engine compartment.  When this hose tears, you 
get water/condensation into the heat exchanger/flapper box area. 
Turning on the heat generally gets this water into the heat exchanger
and it turns to vapor and steams your windows (you will never guess
how I know this...).

Inspect (and then replace) the hoses that connect to the heat exchangers.

One each side under the car near the back.

Automotion has some nice durable rubber/metal ones.  They look like they
will last, and only make the car smell like rubber for a few weeks.



- --Soren            laforce@xenon.arc.nasa.gov

Bob, I just ran into a similar problem with my '84 Carrera. I do not know if 
your car works the same way. Hope this helps.

I noticed that when I pulled on the red levers that are next to the hand brake
only the passanger side was activated by the pass. lever. The driver's lever
produced no output of air (the fan would not turn on). I believe that in my
car I have separate L/R blowers. Don't know about yours...

I proceded to look at the wiring diagrams. Then I remember that when I installed
a Shortschifter, there were a couple of cables comming out of the heat levers
by the hand brake. I was able to trace the problem to this switch. I did not
even had to change it. I used a little sandpaper, some contact cleaner and a
little coaxing with a screw driver. I actually pushed in the contacts a bit. 
This was just enough to make it work.

Oh, BTW, I used an ohmeter to check the operation of the switch. And make sure
that the lower,red lever in the dash is all the way to the right. This shoot all
heated air to the windshield.

You might want to check this out in your car. I hope they work the same way.

Good Luck!

Lou Nicotra

'87 Metallic Black Mercedes 300D Turbo

Bob> REAL sports car :)

It must be the REAL sports car syndrome...my '84 VW Scirocco has it too.
Maybe I ought to replace it with a 944 (nah!).  The steps listed below
have mitigated - but not necessarily eliminated - the steam problem for me:


* Check to see if your heat box's drain is plugged up (water can't escape)
* Run heat the day before to try to keep the system as dry as possible
* See if the slant the car is parked on results in pooling that can't escape
* Wait for the heat to come up a little bit before trying to apply heat(steam).
* Run AC to keep the window defogged (Brr!)
* Open the windows and go to full blast with the steamy stuff.

These are pretty much in order of preference, and they've helped on my
VW.  I don't know the 911 heating system to determine how relevant some
of these may be.  I'm going to try Bob's suggestion of "warm the air and
then move to defrost" next time its nasty here in NJ such that I have to
take the VW.

 -hh

Date: Thu, 4 Nov 93 08:24:42 EST
From: sos!len1@uunet.UU.NET (L.E.Nicotra-WH-77122H(WH5672)W214)
Subject: Cruise controls...

>  
> 
> HEY Lou Nicotra,
> 
> I tried twice to send a message to you but got bounced. So I am doing it this 
> way.
> 
> My car does the exact same thing yours does with the cruise control. Can you 
> (or any one) give me more details about the CPU that controls the cruise 
> control and the connector that needs to be cleaned?? Thanks
> 
> Minh Le
 
Minh, here's what I've done:

1. Open trunk and peel back rug half way. There are two button snaps by the
firewall? (Dashboard)

2. Using a 10mm wrench, remove 4 nuts that hold a curved cardboard cover right
in front of dash. You will see heater cables and blower. Right in front of the
driver, there is a little silver box, about 1.5 x 5 x 8 inches. It has a 
multiple connector that attaches to its bottom.

3. Using a screw driver, remove it. I think it uses 2 screws. Disconnect it
from the connector and you are ready to clean/re-assembly. No need to open it,
since "there are no user serviceable parts".

4. If this does not work, you might have a vacuum leak in your servo motor.
This can be tested with a vacuum pump e.g. Mighty-Vac.

Hope this helps...

Let us know!

Lou Nicotra
att!sos1!len1
'84 Guards Red Carrera Targa
'87 Metallic Black Mercedes 300D Turbo

Date: Thu, 4 Nov 1993 17:35:14 -0500
From: Robert B Mohan <rmohan@afit.af.mil>
Subject: Forged/Cast/Repro Wheels

My 2 Cents worth to the net,

Having gone full circle on wheels on my '86 951, I'll let
you benefit from my mistakes/experiences.

Fortunately, an '86 951 will accept 930 wheels, unlike later
951's, so if I want forged wheels, I have the choice of:

Fuchs: 7x16, 8x16, 9x16

BBS RS: 7,7.5,8,8.5,9,9.5x16,x17
(these are 3-piece w/forged centers, and spun alum. rim halves)

BBS also makes the RG, a single-piece forged wheel, but not avail.
in US, only Europe and Japan

OZ and Gotti/Etoile also make 3-piece wheels with forged centers
and spun aluminum rims.

Robert Wood and BBS Motorsport (Atlanta) both agree that forged
is the best for STREET.  The unbeatable strength can handle 
curbs, potholes, speedbumps, etc. without catastrophic failure.
They may still bend, but will not shatter.

Cost is still a premium for any make of forged wheel.

BBS Motorsport makes race wheels for all applications, including
962's, etc. which are not streetable because they are NOT STRONG
ENOUGH.  They have magnesium centers and thinner aluminum rims.
They are designed for light weight and take advantage of generally
more friendly conditions found on road race tracks.

As for cast repro's, they're as good as cast originals of equal
quality.  My brother-in-law has a set of shattered original
cookie cutter's to prove it.

Bob Mohan

rmohan@afit.af.mil

Date: 04 Nov 1993 15:59:50 -0500 (EST)
From: Tim Donahue <tdonahue@maelstrom.timeplex.com>
Subject: Re: Porsche 911 (993)


Re: The 993

A friend of mine recently traveled to Germany and brought back an
official factory brochure for the 993.  The bodywork looks great,
as does the interior. The door panels and ends of the dash have
been resculped to flow together smoothly.  The dash gauges have
the familiar 911 organization, with a new improved clock (complete
with high-speed advance controlled by a button on the dash).  I
don't recall what the seats look like.  Steering wheel is one of
the now-generic things with four spokes and an airbag in the
middle.

The trick bits of note in this particular book are the headlights,
which seem to combine a hyperellipsoidal (sp?) beam and a more
traditional sealed (halogen) beam in the same lens.  It isn't
clear which is the main beam and which focuses the brights.  The
rear suspension is carried in a cast aluminum ()-shaped gizmo, the
castings having clearly-visible reinforcing webbing; dampers are
coil-overs I believe; location by 4 or 5 links (I don't recall
they looked like A-arms).

The rear view is sweet, the front view very 968-ish but much more
squat, aformentioned headlights raked back somewhat.  The
windshield and roof are familiar 911.  My friend considers the
C-pillar/roof-into-rear fenders-wheel well the one awkward piece
of styling.  Cup-like wheels and the usual mondo brakes complete
the side view.

Twin coolers are located behind a largish chin duct - one for A/C,
one for oil.  Targa is gone - coupe and cabrio only.  Pricing in
DM was equivalent to about US$70000.

I'm like to see one (hopefully up close and stationary) before
passing judgement.  My friend tells me that there are many more
oddly-colored cars driving about in Germany than here (where it
seems 99% of mondern cars are black, white, or arrest-me red),
with lots of pastels and multi-hued metallics.

Cheers,
Tim

9117610055

------------------------------

Date: Thu, 4 Nov 93 13:03:47 PST
From: John Wright <jpw@hpsadlb.sr.hp.com>
Subject: reproduction alloys

Around 1976 an auto parts saleman showed me the 5 spoke center 
that had snapped out of an Italian made "fake Fuchs".  

He said it was from his 912 and happened under hard cornering.  

According to him, it was not caused by hitting a curb, and was not caused by
a tire failure, since the rim with tubed tire was recovered still inflated.

The car was totaled in the wreck.  

He was one "unhappy camper", since the wheel distributor denied any liability.

I have NEVER heard similar stories about the USA made reproduction wheels,
they may be fine.

I had the option to buy reproductions or used forged and I opted for used.

If you decide to buy used:

  911 forged alloy wheels are commonly available in the 15 X 6 size.  
  $500 +/- $100 will get you a usable set of 4.

  Some of the 15 X 6 wheels take the 3-prong center cap, some take the ring 
  center cap.  Not all of mine are the same, so I have both types of caps.

  There may be other offset/appearance differences in factory 15 X 6 wheels.
  Robert Wood in LA should be a good source of information.

  After visually inspecting the wheel, if memory serves, there are three
  measurements to make: radial runout, axial runout and wear of the radius 
  of the rim where it contacts the tire.  

  So the factory has found that an alloy wheel can wear out.

  The spec's are in the 72+ 911 factory shop manual and in some of the 
  later spec books.  

  It will be impossible to measure the wheel with a tire mounted.

  The contact radius spec is checked with a radius gauge, the other two 
  with a dial indicator.


John Wright

Date: Fri, 5 Nov 93 08:36:17 EST
From: fwk@hogpa.ho.att.com
Subject: Re: wheels

>You wrote:
>Steve Timmins
>> Porsche has only ever made 3 popular forged wheel styles - Fuchs, 928
>> flat style wheels, and the 951S forged wheels.
>
>Well, really four.  That "Fuchs" in your list should include both the
>traditional 911 Fuchs 5-spoke, as well as the 914's optional Fuchs 4-spoke
>(commonly known as the "914 2.0L wheel").

Of course, over the years Fuchs has made a wide variety of forged wheels
for Porsche, beginning with the 4 1/2 x 15 wheels for the early 911S,
but the 4 bolt 914 Fuchs wheels were enough different than the rest that
perhaps they deserve special mention. While on that subject, those
of you who use the 914 Fuchs wheels on the track should be aware that
they can crack. I cracked 2 of them over the course of a few hundred
PCA track events, as well as significant street driving. Yes, they
were factory wheels, not repro's. No, I didn't slide them into a curb
or some such. They crack between the openings, perhaps 2 inches toward
the center from the rim. In both cases the wheel stayed intact.
I wouldn't do anything drastic, just look at them carefully after each
event.

Also, on the more general topic of "fake" cast Fuchs look-alike wheels,
most (all?) regions of the PCA in the Northeast will not allow these
wheels to run at PCA track events.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@hogpa.att.com

Date: Fri, 5 Nov 1993 10:29:24 -0500
From: John N Berry <jberry@afit.af.mil>
Subject: 911 Oil Tank Questions

Hello!

I've got a few questions about some oil tank work and I could use the benefit
of the net's experiences...

My oil level sending unit went out on my '75 911S.  Symptom:  The gauge 
alternated between *very full* and *very empty* at idle and w/ a warm motor.
After I assured myself that the motor was not in the process of self-destructing
I unhooked the sending unit hot wire, taped it off and secured it, and continued
my trip (I was many miles from home, at the Grand Canyon - excellent road trip
area if you get the chance!)

To replace it, I cleaned the area well, took out the old, put in the new, fixed
an *iffy* wiring harness connection, and it all worked well.  This was several
months ago.  Recently I noticed that the '75 sending unit is NLA and what I
actually installed was an '81-'89 unit (page 65 of the *Performance Products*
catalog).  It also mentions that a new dipstick is required.  This makes sense
to me:  I've checked the oil a few times and heard/felt a soft *click* as I
withdrew the stick - no doubt the dipstick is interfering with the sending
unit float.

Here are my questions:

1.  What's the geometry like in the tank?  Am I poking a hole in the float?

2.  Can I just shorten my old dipstick?  How much?


On a related subject...  I've noticed that the tank is showing a little copper.
Since the car has just entered storage for the winter [:-(] I'll be changing 
the oil REAL SOON NOW and intend to remove the tank and refinish it while it's 
empty.  I'd like to hear some stories/get some advice on this topic.  

Specifically,

1.  How do you prep the tank properly?  I know the copper coating is thin so
sandpaper is a no-no but how do you get it all cleaned up and ready?

2.  What's a good chip resistant paint for this application?  I have access to
a good paint gun so that's an option, but is there an *out of the can* solution?

3.  Any experience with the Wurth underbody spray? (on page 96 of the 
*Performance* catalog, for instance)  Is this what I should use?  What's the
best source for one or two cans of the stuff?


Thanks for your thoughts on this subject!!

John Berry
jberry@afit.af.mil
'75 Peru Red 911S Sunroof Coupe

Hello!

Here's what happened to me...

WHAM!! from behind!  I thought the car was totaled...  But, then I got out,
looked, and realized that I'd been hit square on the right side ruuber
absorber of my 911.  A few paint scrapes and a pushed in back bumper.  The
body shop that did the work (Bavarian Coach Werks, south of Dayton, OH) said
that the bumper shock didn't need replaced because it was only pushed in a
little and wasn't leaking oil.  It was an insurance job (thank goodness!) so
they could have replaced it with no hassle but said it didn't need to be done.
They just pulled it out about 3/8" or so and put it back on.  No sweat.

Good luck!

I read in an old body shop book (testbook) from the early 80's how 
they used to actually take these things apart and repair them.  Of 
course this was on old detroit steel not porsches.  

Anyway you should be able to basically pull the sukers out again. 
They are basically gas charged shocks but the thing is, they never 
move.  So, when they are finally eventually pushed in they somtimes 
stick.  I had this happen to me on a superbeetle and what i did was 
stick bar between the body and the bumper and just give it a little 
coax and it popped out on its own.  

I knwo a 914 has no space between the body and the bumper but if 
you can figure out a way to get a grip on it just yank it out

I hate sayin' stuff like this cuz i know theres just somone out there 
whose gonna disagree....

John

Date: Fri, 5 Nov 93 16:00:00 +0000
From: INGRAM_GLENN/HPBOI1_03@hpdmd48.boi.hp.com
Subject: Fake Fuchs....

I had a set of fake (replica) Fuchs made by American.  They were
heavier than the forged originals, but the cost was right ($200/4)!

Well, I was skeptical about their strength until I wrecked my 911 into a
rock wall at well !! over the national speed limit (my poor judgement here
but the cars was handling sooooo great...).
Anyway, the front replica took a BIG hit (I mean really big) and , to my
surprise, was NOT BENT!!! The  outer lip broke off, due to it being
cast, but the wheel was STRAIGHT & was repaired (I DID NOT & WILL NOT EVER
RECOMMEND THIS ON A CAST WHEEL- the 'new' owner of my old car was a REAL
tight *ss and didn't want to fork up the 125 clams for a new one....he
is very stupid & I told him that to his face, but he bought the car...).

The rear wheel took a slightly slower hit, since the car rotated around
after hitting the mountain, and was only scratched...not even bent!

A WORD OF CAUTION HERE  (engineer geek speaking):  THese cast replicas
will NOT BENT-THEY FAIL (BREAK) UNDER EXCESSIVE LOAD!! The factory forged
factory wheel, under the same loading, will bend and stay together...

Choose your posion, I would only recommend them for Highway use only
and not for track events, since failure at REALLY high speed could be
REAL BAD.  Don't comprimise on safety- whether it's your wheels, fire
suite, or any other safety equip- IT IS NOT WORTH IT!!! SPEND MONEY
ON THE THINGS THAT WILL 1) KEEP YOU SAFE 2) KEEP YOU ALIVE & 3) KEEP
YOU AROUND FOR THE NEXT TIME you have fun....  I could have been killed
in this accident and I will do things differently if I ever drive
in this kind of situation again..

G'day,

very lucky 'mr wizard'

Date: Fri, 5 Nov 93 12:20:30 -0500
From: Jeffrey A. Shorey <js@ee.duke.edu>
Subject: Re: 911 Oil Tank Questions

John N Berry writes:
>My oil level sending unit went out on my '75 911S.  Symptom:  The gauge 
>alternated between *very full* and *very empty* at idle and w/ a warm motor.
Shortly after getting my '73 (and after changing the oil) the level
gauge starting reading *real full*.  Fortunately I was still in to checking
the oil level manually and found that the dipstick did not correlate with
the gauge reading.  Turned out to be dirty connections at the spade connectors
on the oil level sensor.  This seems like really stupid engineering: an open
circuit left the gauge indicating full!

Sounds like you were a little low on oil and you had a flakey connection -
probably didn't need to replace the level sensor.  I basically recrimped
the female spade connector, cleaned off the male connector, and made sure
they fit together firmly.  Haven't had any problems in 3 years.

 - Jeff Shorey
'73 911S Coupe
