Date: 5 Nov 1993 09:47:06 U
From: "Lance Keigwin" <lance_keigwin@engtwomac.synoptics.com>
Subject: 911 brake noise

                       Subject:                               Time:9:32 AM
  OFFICE MEMO          911 brake noise                        Date:11/5/93
I noticed a similar "ticking noise" in my high-mileage '88
turbo-look and had it checked out.  After some difficulty
isolating it I found the left rear rotor had a 2" hairline
crack in it(!) along the inside cup edge.  It was very hard
to see unless you were looking for it.  Replacing both rear
rotors fixed the problem (yikes -- don't ask how much 930
brake rotors cost).  I hope this is not your problem but I
thought I'd pass it along.

Lance (lkeigwin@synoptics.com)
P.S. These old vented, drilled rotors make handsome
planters.

To: porschephiles@tta.com
Subject: Thinking about buying a Porsche (FAQ?)

(Is this a FAQ? Is there a FAQ list for Porschephiles?)

After several years without a car, I'm thinking of buying a used 944.

Is there anything special I should watch out for? What kind risks would
I be taking if I bought a high-mileage car? Any good references on buying
a used Porsche? Any good mechanics/dealers in the Boston (Ma) area I could 
take a car to, to get a second opinion.

 thanks,

  benji

    A few thoughts:

    - Porsche made a number of improvements in the 944 in the second half
    of the 1985 model year.  I would look for an 85 1/2 or newer
    model...these are easily recognized by the newer style dash and
    instrument cluster.

    - Maintenance is every bit as important as mileage.  Without proper
    care and feeding, even a low mileage car  can be trash.  On the other
    hand, a well maintained Porsche will run a long time.  Insist on
    service history!

    - Water pumps and motor mounts have been weak points of early models.
    Check to see if these have been replaced/upgraded.

    - Timing belts MUST be adjusted every 15K and replaced every 30K.  If
    you can't determine ABSOLUTELY when this was done, factor it into your
    purchase price.  A failed belt can turn your pride and joy into a parts
    car real quick.

    - Finally, you are right, the price of a second opinion is well worth
    the investment.

    good luck

    Steve Carlson   '84 944   Chicago Region PCA

Subject:  914 buying

Greetings gang,
I was so depressed from work yesterday that I went out and bought a Porsche.
Another Porsche I should say...for le$$ than a grand, and drove it home.

A shop downtown had it out in the back lot (they werent proud of it).
It's a '76 with a 2.0 and dual carbs (single barrel =:< ) and monza exh.
At one time it had air, but the pump and underdash stuff has been ripped
out.  The battery was shot and the engine lid hinges were broken off.
The color was earl scheib black over L80E (light Ivory) with black vinyl
seats, and the body is rusty in the usual places, but not as bad as my
recently rustored 914.  The rear deck lid hinges were both broken off
and the front trunk lid didnt fit because the tub was distorted in a way
I still havent figured out how. 
The original wheels were gone and in their place were four american mags
with michelin mxl's all 'round -no spare.  The front trunk is missing
the carpet and spare covering, and the fuel tank seems to leak.
The interior is pretty torn up and the carpet is missing on the driver
side.  The steering wheel is missing the centerpiece, the center console
pad is loose from it's hinge and the radio has been ripped out.
The windshield has a thin crack down the center and the passenger side door
wont open from inside or out.  The engine tin is missing all its fasteners
and a  mysterious zip cord passes thru to below... where the 'j' tubes
and heat valves WERE.

The shop wanted 995 and i offered ahem, less.  So they tried to start it
 -it ran poorly on less than 4 cylinders and spewed water out the monza's.
A couple weeks later i went back and they still had it and wanted to
sell it to me 'as is'.  My offer was considered, countered and I said OK.

So I put a battery in it and a couple gallons of premium and:
The clock showed 90 something under cloudy glass, and wires hung out from
under the dash (along with the ignition switch).  I put a screwdriver into
the switch and (hail Mary) twisted: The speedo and odo dont work, but 5  
miles later we get home, smoking and sputtering on sometimes less than
3 cylinders...and the oil light came on just as i drove up the driveway,
leaving a trail of oil.  A pushrod tube leak, i thought.

- --Begin Diagnostic and Detection Routine--

On cleaning the interior, I find over $7 in change, three cassette tapes
(prince, sade, and some funky rap) and about 25 pens and pencils, all
crammed under the seats.  Studying some of the receipts (trash) I see the
most recent date of transaction: Jan. 11 1993 (western auto) items: wiring, 
fog-off pads, muffler patch, and -binaca.  
Preliminary conclusion; the previous owner was a student with friends at
royal body shop (pen logo) who knew little about 914's.  The former
owner liked music and Porsche's, but could afford little of either.
Poor devil had bad breath but not bad enough to de-fog the windows because
of NO heat.  The zipcord went to a 'belly pan heater' Ah Ha! no choke!

***TO BE CONTINUED***
***END OF PART ONE***

N'yuk, N'yuk, Ow!  I'm a victim of (my own) coicumstance!

Date: Fri, 5 Nov 93 16:53:41 -0500
From: kennedy@admin.njit.edu (William R. Kennedy, NJIT CIAT, 201-596-5648)
Subject: 914/4 to 6

Nicholas Cole inquires about 914 upgrade from 4 to 6 cyl.; I reply from per-
spective of somewhat similar conversion of Vanagon.  First, I would only
consider Porsche 6, especially for a daily driver.  Some enthusiastic comments
on the net about a month ago (?) clarify why.  Second, you need to think
carefully about what you want to put in and what you want to get out.  Conver-
sion will keep the car off the road for a surprisingly long time, and will
use money that could have gone into the engine if you stayed with the 4.

My list of areas that needed solutions was: accelerator, heat, electrical,
gauges, flywheel/trans, engine support, oil tank, vacuum for brakes, shrouding,
muffler, fuel supply, and headroom for carbs and fanhousing.  Your list would
be similar.  Some of the solutions were measured in hours, some in weeks.  In
the 914 case, Porsche's brutally expensive solutions are an option, and there
are aftermarket solutions as well, so you would be able to trade money for 
time.  

I haven't toted up my bills yet, but I am certainly over $1000 excluding the
cost of the engine, and I have done all the work myself.  My work is mech-
anically sound, but cosmetically awful; if you wanted better, you'd need to 
budget even more dollars and/or time.  By the time you add in the price of a
good Porsche engine, you will be real close to affording a nice 914/6 instead
of a mutant.

In terms of $ per hp -- raw, raw estimates:
brandie new 2.4 liter Type 4 engine with carbs, $4000/130hp=30.8
serviceable 3.0 liter 6, including installation, $9000/200hp=45.0

Would love comments from others, and to hear what you decide.  You can't lose;
end result will be fun either way, but the two paths are real different.

B.

Date: Fri, 05 Nov 93 17:17:00 PST
From: Sherri Seybold <Sherri@asymetrix.com>
Subject: RE: 911 Brakes


If you're running aftermarket brake pads, like Cool Carbons, you'll find 
that they're not manufactured to the same exacting tolerances (size-wise) as 
the OEM pads, and what you may be feeling is the slightly smaller backing 
plate seating and unseating.  If this is the case, it's not really anything 
to worry about.  Both my car and Darryl's do it too.  Drop by Chris's or 
Squire's.  They should be able to tell you if that's what it really is in a 
couple of minutes.

Date: Fri, 5 Nov 1993 20:44:31 -0700
From: Bob Greschke <bgreschk@aoc.nrao.edu>
Subject: 914 fan for heat/defrost

Hi Pam!

I just ran out and checked the heater fan on my 1975 914.

Nothing appears to happen when the fan lever is in the blue "crescendo"
portion. But there are 3 different speeds: low - I, med - II, high - III.
The light behind the fan icon comes on anytime the fan is running.

You can wrestle the whole switch unit out through the front of the
dash. I think there is only one fuse for the motor (one of the regular
fuses in the fuse block).

Haynes (page 138 in mine) shows the switch assembly pulled out. After
checking to make sure that the moving "fingers" make proper contact, I
would check the resistance of each of the contact points (B, C, and D
in the switch) to ground. If points B and C are not "open" then you have
a gremlin. If they are open then you either have broken wires or bad
windings. Point D should read some resistance since the high speed works.

If they read open I'd try to do the same reading along the same wires as
close to the motor as possible.

Actually, I'd try doing the above with the switches in place if possible.
That way you don't have to undo the control cables. I think there's
enough room.

Hey! At least my car is good for answering those "stationary car"
questions. 8-)

Bob
1975 Fiesta Orange 1.8L 914

------------------------------

Date: Sat, 6 Nov 93 04:55:30 GMT
From: Richard Cole <colers@prl.philips.co.uk>
Subject: RE: 914 fan for heat/defrost


Pam asked about 914 fan control - well if it is the same as that on a '72 911
there are three fan speeds selected from copper tracks on the base of the fan
control.  The blue light should come on when the fan runs (ie at position I).
My fan gave the same symptoms as Pam described and as it ran at all the fuse
was deemed ok and the tracks mentioned above were clean.  So I took the fan
housing out (removal is the reverse of installation :-) ) to see what was
what.

The power to the fan goes through a four pin plug on the fan housing (you
could check whether you had volts here - one pin is ground and the other
three get 12 volts depending on which speed setting is selected.  It is
possible to take the fan housing to bits by removing the spring clips all
round the join.  The plug socket can now be examined.  On the back there are
two large resistors which give the slower fan speeds for settings I and II.

Each resistor is "protected" by what I think is a bi-metalic strip switch.
Now this is where my knowledge of what this thing is designed to do ends as
the strips on my unit were very rusty and stuc closed - so the resistors were
shorted and the fan always ran at full speed (I, II or III) or was off.  As I
was not able to get the plug unit as a replacement (I think you have to buy
the whole fan unit $$$) I just bent the bi-metalic strips open and everything
works ok.  However they must have been there for a purpose - perhaps to
protect the resistors from overheating and burning out (anyone have any more
info on this).  I did this a couple of years ago and the fan has worked fine
since then.

Richard
<colers@prl.philips.co.uk>
'72 911 E 2.4 Bahia Red

Porschephiles #561                         Sun Nov 7 02:18:51 CST 1993
Date: Sat, 6 Nov 1993 00:23:44 -0600
From: jmb@wuchem.wustl.edu
Subject: 914/4 to 6

	Not owning a 914 myself but having friends who do, I thought I
might give you a couple of opinions. The most bang for the buck can be
had with the VW based engine. Not only the up front costs but especially 
thge maintance costs will be MUCH less. It also takes a much greater level
of owner knowledge to deal with the 6 as opposed to the 4 if you intend
to ever do much of the maintance yourself.  The conversion to the 6 is
actually rather straightforeward since Porsche did it and all the parts
are readily available. I'm sure others on the net have probably done this
themselves.  My suggestion: if you wish to basically leave the rest of the
car alone and drive it build the 4 to the 130hp level and enjoy.  If you 
intend to fully improve the chassis (welding in bracing required) and convert
to a 911 front suspension, overhaul the rear suspension, convert to 911
brakes all around (of course they come with the 911 front suspension) and
also convert to a 916 type transaxle, go with an older carburated 6 as
reccomended by the individual with the vanagon. Your conversion would
definitely be easier than his.  Of course you now have quite a different
car than you started with and a much bigger bill. The VW tranny will take
the lower torque 4 at 130hp with a stronger clutch and judicious sue of 
first gear on take off. The 6 will destroy it if you aren't verry careful.
From an economic only standpoint the 914 will not be worth the money which
is spent to fully convert it to a 916 (that transaxle is very expensive).
But then if that is what you want, and you really love that particular
car, it may be well worth it to you. My 356 isn't worth what I've spent on
it, but then, I don't care, thank you very much.  My personal feeling would
be to stay with the 4, but as everything else I've said this is only an
opinion.  At any rate, good luck in whatever you decide. Jonathan

Upgrading your factory alarm                            Kevin Gross

With the '85= 944, Porsche introduced a factory alarm system which 
remained largely unchanged until the '89 model year.  When triggered, 
the alarm beeps a horn.  In '89, an additional option became available.  
Designated M484, cars equipped with this option strobe their fog lamps 
and stop lamps along with the horn.

Car alarms are often ignored these days because of the frequency of false 
triggers in motion-sensing types of alarms.  Strobing the lamps might 
make the difference between your car's alarm being ignored, and having 
it draw people's attention.  Fortunately, upgrading the 944-series alarms 
from pre-'89 with the M484 option is straightforward.


How it works
- ------------

The accompanying figure shows the part of the wiring diagrams we're 
interested in.  The key part is the block on the right, showing the new 
relay, G18, and its wiring.  Notice that it takes its input signal from the 
output of the Alarm Control Unit (ACU).  The same strobed 12-volt signal 
sent to the alarm horn is now also used to open and close the relay.

Operating the relay adds very little additional electrical load on the ACU. 

The real power to the fog lamps and stop lamps comes from a new fused 
circuit we'll add, using fuse #12.  The relay has two output circuits, one 
for the fog lamps and one for the stop lamps.


Wiring it
- ---------

Most of the work is simply running the new wires.  If you haven't read 
my article in the March Nor'easter describing the Central Electric Board, 
now would be a good time to do so.

Disconnect the negative lead from the battery, then lift the CEB out of its 
case.  The map inside the top of the case shows where relay G18, 
currently unused, will go.  Remove the punch-out from the CEB at this 
location.  We'll next run a number of wires to this location.  As I 
mentioned in the March article, be sure to run the new wires long the 
existing harness so that there is enough slack.

Start with a black 0.5 mm (20 AWG) from pin 1 of the ACU connector 
back to the CEB.  You'll find the alarm control unit clipped to the back of 
the Bosch DME computer.  Which is behind a wood panel in the 
passenger footwell.  Pull the connector off the ACU, then use a jeweller's 
screwdriver to depress the pin 1 connector's locking barb so that you can 
pull it from the connector block.  Clip off the old flat connector, and 
crimp a new one (N.017.491.8) to both the original black/red wire and to 
your new wire.

You will also have to add a red 1.0 mm. (16 AWG) wire to the stop light 
circuit.  There are a couple of places you could do this in the harness.  I 
found it simplest to run it to the stop light switch's plug on the brake 
pedal assembly.  Again, remove the red/white wire's connector from the 
two-pin plug, clip off the existing connector, and crimp a new one 
(N.017.491.8) onto both the red/white wire and the new wire.  Be careful 
here - the stop light is an important thing!  Consider both crimping and 
soldering.

Two down, three to go.  The next wire is a fat, 2.5 mm. (12 AWG) one 
which brings power from the CEB to the relay.  As you can see in the 
wiring diagram, it goes to plug location K31 in the CEB.  Use a 2.5 mm 
CEB connector (999.652.475.22).  Loop the wire down into the harness, 
through the first cable tie, and back up to where the relay will go.

Now the fog lamp wire.  This should be a green 1.5 mm wire (14 AWG).  It 
plugs into CEB location J11.  Finally, you need a brown 0.5 mm (20 
AWG) ground wire.  Crimp a 1/4" ring terminal onto it, and fasten the 
ring terminal to one of the ground blocks located next to the CEB, visible 
looking up from inside the driver-side footwell.


Final assembly
- --------------

You now have a bundle of five wires which need to have connectors put 
on their end, then be inserted into the relay socket.  Notice that the 
socket is in fact two pieces.  The top of it unlocks from the bottom by 
unlatching it on two sides.  Take it apart now and set aside the smaller 
top part.

You'll need a short length of red 1.5 mm (14 AWG) wire to bridge power 
to the stop lamps.  Crimp the large 4-6 mm. female flat connector 
(N.017.494.3) onto both this wire and the red 2.5 mm wire, then insert it 
into pin 4 of the socket.  Make sure the barb locks it in properly.

Crimp the rest of the female flat connectors onto the red, black, and 
green wires.  Crimp the funny-looking 2.8 mm connector (N.903.352.03) 
onto the brown wire.  Solder them as well, if you'd like.  Insert them all 
into the relay socket in the pin locations shown in the wiring diagram.  It 
matters which way the flat connectors are inserted: do it so that the 
locking barb engages.  If in doubt, check out an existing relay's socket.  
Reattach the top piece of the socket, then insert it into the CEB.  Add the 
15 amp fuse to circuit 12.  Plug in the relay.  You're done.

Almost.  Before plugging in the relay, I would test the signals at each of 
the socket pins.  With a multi-meter, look for zero ohms between pin 7 
and ground.  Look for 12 volts at pins 4 and 5.  Look for 12 volts at pin 7 
when you turn the fog lights on (ignition on and headlamps on).  Depress 
the brake pedal (ignition on) and look for that to bring 12 volts to pin 3. 

Finally, trigger the alarm and look for strobed 12 volts at pin 2.

My thanks once again to Mack Mackenzie at Clair Parts Express for his 
help in chasing down the parts and their numbers.


Bill of materials
- -----------------

quantity        item                                    part number
1               relay socket                            928.610.511.00
1               alarm relay                             944.615.113.01
6               female flat connector,                  N.017.491.8
                 barbed, for up to 2.5 mm. wire
1               female flat connector,                  N.017.494.3
                 barbed, for 4 to 6 mm. wire
1               female CEB-style connector,             N.903.352.03
                 for 2.8 mm. pin, up to 1.5 mm. wire
1               female CEB connector,                   999.652.474.22
                 for 6.3 mm. pin, up to 1.5 mm. wire
1               female CEB connector,                   999.652.475.22
                 for 6.3 mm. pin, 1.5 to 2.5 mm. wire
1               1/4" ring terminal
1               15 amp fuse
 -               wire in various sizes and colors

Subject: Carbon brakes

Speaking of articles, here's one I wrote which will appear in the Dec.
issue of my region's newsletter.  As ever, editors of other region's
newsletters are welcome and invited to make use.  I would ask only
that you indulge me by sending along a copy of your newsletter -- I love
reading them!

Carbon brake pads                                       Kevin Gross

Anyone who's been near motorsports in the last five years has no 
doubt heard the term "carbon fibre" bandied about a great deal.  There 
are in fact two different technologies being discussed.  I'll bring you 
up to speed on one of them.  But before getting to it, let me first talk 
briefly about the other one.

Which is carbon fibre as used in composite materials.  Composites 
combine some sort of fibre with a resin to form structural objects, 
such as a race car monocoque.  Generally, the fibre is first woven into 
a cloth.  Layers of this cloth are immersed in and joined with an 
adhesive, binding the fibres together and producing a material that is 
both light and at the same time strong.  The fibres are usually man-
made ones such as carbon, Kevlar, fibreglass, graphite, and recently 
ceramics.  The adhesives are typically phenolic, acrylic, polyurethane, 
or epoxy.

One of the first big users of carbon fibre composites was the Space 
Shuttle program, and indeed much of the development of current 
composite technology came from the Hercules company.  The state-
of-the-art was probably defined earlier this year by NPTI, Nissan's 
racing arm.  They revealed that they had developed and certified a 
sportscar monocoque plus roll cage made entirely from composites.  
No steel at all.  It withstood 1.5 times the static load test to which 
FISA subjects WSC cars, and did so without any structural failure.  
On top of that, the design weighed 40% less than a similar unit made 
the old fashioned way.

But enough of that.  What I really want to write about is the use of 
carbon in high performance braking systems.  Terms like "carbon 
fibre brakes" and "carbon-carbon" or "carbon-Kevlar" get tossed 
about.  What's it all mean?

Until a few years ago, pads were commonly made from a mixture of 
asbestos fibres, metals, other friction material, binders, curing agents, 
and a bunch of other black magic.  Like soft drinks, a manufacturer's 
recipe for brake pad material is a closely kept secret.  The asbestos 
fibres had the same sort of role as straw does in adobe, or steel in 
concrete: they give a degree of mechanical strength to the pad.  They 
also provide some of the pad's frictive characteristic.

Racers have two sometimes conflicting needs of their brakes.  They 
need to stop quickly, and they need to stop reliably.  The older 
"organic" and "metallic" brake pad compounds would produce a 
coefficient of friction of only about 0.28 when used with a cast iron 
rotor.  Moreover, when worked very hard at the track, they would fade 
or disintegrate as heat became excessive.  "Harder" compounds 
were developed, such as the venerable Ferodo DS-11, but they had 
their thermal limits as well, and worked very poorly until they got 
good and hot.

The aerospace industry again came to the rescue.  Gordon Murray 
adapted the carbon-carbon technology used by Dunlop in brake rotors 
for the Concorde aircraft, to producing pads and rotors for the 
Brabham Formula 1 team.  They found themselves with brakes that 
were light, strong, able to withstand tremendous temperatures, and on 
top of that, with a coefficient of friction that could exceed 0.5.  
Moreover, this coefficient of friction was available over a wide range 
of component temperatures.  Another benefit they found was that the 
carbon pad material conducts heat less than older compounds, 
meaning less heat delivered to the callipers and reduced problems 
with the boiling of brake fluid.

The term "carbon-carbon" tends to be used by people in two different 
ways.  To some people, it describes the combination of carbon brake 
rotors with carbon pads.  To others, it refers to the way these brakes 
and rotors are made: a carbon matrix produced by vacuum cracking 
hydrocarbons at great temperature, reinforced by carbon fibres.  
Substitute Kevlar fibres for carbon ones, and you have a carbon-
Kevlar material such as is used by Cool Carbon in their line of 
products.

Like many things in the aerospace industry, this technology was far 
from cheap.  Which is probably why the Continental Circus was its 
first outside application.  Two companies, Hitco and Carbone 
Industrie, have been responsible for virtually all the carbon-carbon 
brake technology used in Formula 1.  More recently, they and other 
companies have begun supplying brake pads based on this technology 
to more and more motorsport series, as well as street cars.
Today there are at least two companies making carbon brake pads for 
street Porsches: Cool Carbon and Performance Friction.  Both 
companies manufacture pads in several different compounds, and 
selecting the right one depends on the weight of your vehicle and 
driving style.  These pads are priced competitively with OEM brake 
pads.

My personal experience has been with Cool Carbon pads over the 
course of the last two years.  It's been a very positive one, both driving 
on the street, at autocrosses, and at the track.  Their carbon-Kevlar 
pads stop very well both cold (that first autocross run of the day) and 
hot (that last run group at the track).  

Cool Carbon pads include a unique ceramic insulating layer between 
the steel backing plate and the carbon-Kevlar pad, allowing even less 
heat to conduct into the calliper.  We calibrated this on my car at a 
few track events this season, using thermal paint on the front rotors, 
pads, pad backing plates, and callipers.  We saw temperatures in 
excess of 560 degrees C (1040 degrees F) in the rotors and on the pad 
at the pad/rotor interface.  Despite these temperatures, and using AP 
550 brake fluid with its dry boiling point of 550 degrees F, I have 
experienced no problems with boiling.

Ok, any disadvantages?  Two minor ones.  They do squeal at times, a 
problem shared with most brake pads since asbestos has been fazed 
out for health reasons.  If this bothers you, Porsche makes anti-squeal 
shims for most models' brakes.  There is also a variety of paint-on 
rubbery liquids available that work fairly well.

The other disadvantage is that the brake dust produced by carbon pads 
seems to bond quite aggressively to both plain alloy and painted 
wheels.  Weekly cleaning with, for example, P21S should keep your 
wheels looking good.  Where I've been negligent in my ablutions, a 
little polishing compound has done the trick.

There are two widely held myths about carbon pads which I'd like to 
dispel.  The first is that the pads do not require any bedding-in or heat 
cycling to avoid "green fade" at the track.  Not true.  My experience 
has been that these pads require at least as much heat cycling as 
asbestos pads did, and perhaps more.

The other myth is that carbon pads aggressively wear rotors.  I haven't 
had this experience.  However, bear in mind that with these pads, you 
can generate more heat in shorter periods of time, and an overheated 
rotor will wear or degrade more quickly.  Make sure that your braking 
system is adequately cooled.



Braking Tidbits

A number of PCA members lucky enough to own new 911's have had 
problems with their first brake pad change.  These cars have come 
from the factory with anti-squeal liners (dampers) glued to the back of 
their pads.  Unfortunately, the liners have protrusions which insert 
into the calliper piston bores, making removal of the pads impossible 
without first breaking the glue seal between the pad backing plate and 
the liner.  I've found that using a 1" putty knife helps.  Make sure that 
it has a fairly sharp edge.  Shim it between the liner and the pad's 
backing plate, cutting the glue seal.  You will then be able to first 
slide out the pad, then work out the liner.

AP Racing has issued a technical bulletin warning customers against 
mixing their AP600 high temperature brake fluid with other glycol-
based fluids, including their own AP550.  The problem is apparently 
one of crystals precipitating out of the mixture and potentially 
interfering with the operation and sealing of the brake system.

Date: Sun, 7 Nov 93 22:22:10 -0600
From: pfile (Porschephiles Mailer)
Subject: Monthly Update

November 1993

Monthly Notices for the Porschephiles mailing list. 
PLEASE READ THIS WHOLE MESSAGE!!

There are five sections: UPDATE, COPYRIGHT, FTP-INFO, CLUB-INFO, RULES. Be sure
that you are current on RULES...

UPDATE

Still mired deep in the swamps of Jersey, as one famous Jersean (Jerseyite?)
put it long ago... Again, your tolerance is appreciated as I take far too long
to process routine requests.  One energetic volunteer is about to go on-line
as a "substitute me", which will be an interesting experiment. See, first I 
have to get all this info out of my head...

Oh, my "normal" e-mail and phone continue to work (you'll get my voice mail
on the phone). New e-mail (stan@netsmiths.com) also works, but for now the 
bits still go to Houston so it's no faster/better/whatever.  My phone number 
in New Jersey is (908) 287-8070 -- you may get voice mail there too.

Am I moving? Let's just say "it depends". But nothing will happen until
at least Feb '94 at the earliest.

COPYRIGHT AND FAIR USAGE STATEMENT

The messages sent to this list constitute private correspondence by and between
members of a closed community. As such, the copyright for EVERYTHING that 
ANYONE posts lies with them. PERIOD. This is *NOT* a bulletin board system,
where consent for further redistribution is implied by nature of the the
system; it's PRIVATE.

If you wish to use something written by someone on the list, you must first
seek their permission unless they specifically grant the right in that
specific posting to reprint it at will. And, in general, it is a VERY GOOD
idea to *CREDIT* someone if you use something by permission "copyright xxxx
Joe Gearhead, used by permission" to keep *SOMEONE ELSE* from screwing up
down the line.

You may also solicit articles, comments, notes and expansions of previous
notes. Sometimes it works, sometimes it doesn't.

Remember, when you read something on this list: what you are reading belongs
to whoever wrote it. They may have plans to publish it further themselves.
They may want to keep it "private" (i.e. just for list member use). They 
may not care what gets done with it. It's still theirs, and before re-use,
permission must be obtained.

Please respect people's rights, even if they don't make a big deal about it.
I mean it.

FTP-INFO

Archives are available via FTP, compressed, since 4/90. There are no
older archives available. Remember, while it seems like a good idea when 
you're first starting out on the list to know what has gone on before, 
there are OVER 7 MEG of archives. DO NOT TRY TO FTP THEM ALL AT ONE TIME. 
Period!!! And PLEASE DO NOT FTP between 8:00 AM and 7:00 PM CST, or I'll 
have to take access away. We do have to get *SOME* work done around here, 
you know...  Also DO NOT ask me to mail you archives, since it's just too 
much work. Thank you.

Images from a variety of sources are available as well, in /pub/images.
There is an UPLOAD directory there as well, if you have images that you'd
like to upload. Please include a file called "from.<username>", where
<username> is your logon ID that describes the files if possible.

NOTE: To FTP the compressed archives, you will have to use BINARY mode in FTP. 
Then on receipt, you need to use the UNIX "uncompress" program, or equivalent,
to restore to textual format.

SECOND NOTE: if you really, really need to get a full set of archives, 
we have a business services group which could write you a tape for a fee.
Let me know if you really, really need that.


CLUB-INFO

Some Porsche Club info follows. IF you're not a member, PLEASE join!! 
This info is also on-line in /pub/pca/info, in case you forget to save
this message!

Alexandria VA 22310

+1 703 922-9300, roughly 9:00 to 5:00 EST (GMT - 6).  Dues are $36/year.
Have your Visa/MC number *AND* your VIN number(s) handy, if you own a car.
Or they'll send you an application which you can mail in with your check.

National membership chairman is Nann Weissenberger who is reachable via
e-mail at n_weissenberger@harvard.edu.   You can ask her for info or 
get her to send you an application as well.

RULES

This list is still being run as a mail reflector, which sends incoming
mail to the whole list at once. The participants include people with a 
variety of backgrounds, some technical, some race/autocross/performance 
drivers, some owners, some just admirers.  

To send a message to the whole list, mail to

		porschephiles@tta.com

To send mail regarding distribution problems, or other administrative issues,
send mail to

		porschephiles-request@tta.com

If you have a friend who would like to get on this list, or if you feel
moved to tell other people about it, ONLY GIVE OUT THE "-request" address.

The list is also available as a digest, where you get a compilataion of
the day's messages once per day, rather than getting all of the individual
messages. This seems to be a win for people with slow mail links or who
are just too busy to stay current. If you want to convert to (or from)
the digest, send mail to the porschephiles-request address above.

PLEASE NOTE A FEW ITEMS:

1) There are a lot of  members on the list. It's small enough to be effective,
   and large enough to have a number of diverse opinions and sets of
   experience. Remember that before you decide to not ask a question, or
   if you get flamed for asking an "obvious" question. Diversity -- it's
   a *feature*....

2) The list is maintained irregularly. If I'm in town or can reach the
   host system from wherever I am and I have the time to deal with it,
   I *try* to handle changes on a real-time basis. However, as a consultant
   I travel extensively and from time to time, changes will be batched
   on a weekly basis.

3) ONLY send administrative mail to the "porschephiles-request"
   address, or to me directly. And, you can also reach me by phone 
   at (800) 582-6892.

4) In the same vein, if you use a "pure" System V mail system, please pay
   attention to your replies. The default will send the reply to 
   the "porschephiles-request" address, as it is the UUCP "From "
   address (first actual line of the mail header). 

   PLEASE BE AWARE THAT I ROUTINELY TRASH SUCH MIS-ROUTED MAIL!!!

5) If your mailer starts screwing up *you will be dropped from the list*
   Should you notice that you aren't getting the list any more, send me
   mail and I'll figure out why not...

Enjoy the list! If I can be of assistance, please don't hesitate to drop
me a note.

Regards From Your Moderator,

Stanley P. Hanks
Principal Scientist,                            Technology Transfer Associates
U.S. Snail:        P.O. Box 2087,   Bellaire    TX    77402-2087
FedEx/Shipping:    5303-F Glenmont, Houston     TX    77081

Subject: misc

Cool Carbons
- -------------

Thanks Kevin for the interesting article about composite
material brake pads. Someone recommended me the Cool carbons
earlier but I have not seen anyone selling them.

Any advice where to look?

Bumper shocks
- ---------------

My car had a minor impact on the front bumper on the ferry ride
from germany to sweden (private import). This was repaired by
a Porsche repair shop but when I took the bumper off last winter
I saw how the "repair" was done. The deformation zone of the shock
had been deformated some 5 mm and also partially cracked. 

The fix they made was to have a thicker rubber pad between the
bumper and the shock. This worked estetically but today 8 - 9
years after the repair the shock has started to corrode why
it will probably not do the job next time...


Brushes
- ------------

Sorry for the "Swenglich" word coals.

Magnus
911SC '82

Date: Mon, 8 Nov 1993 10:14:15 -0500
From: John N Berry <jberry@afit.af.mil>
Subject: Really Broken Oil Tank Sender

Howydy!

Jeff Shorey wrote...

>Turned out to be dirty connections at the spade connectors
>on the oil level sensor.  This seems like really stupid engineering: an open
>circuit left the gauge indicating full!

>Sounds like you were a little low on oil and you had a flakey connection -
>probably didn't need to replace the level sensor

I really did have a broken sending unit.  I took everything apart first just
to make sure (I had already tried the *put the wire back on, check all the
connections* route).  Take one apart and you'll find out that the sending unit
works by sliding a metal arm over an exposed wire-wound resistor: a simple and
effective way to do it!  Anyway, as the arm slides over the exposed wire it,
of course, *eats* part of it.  After many years of fluctuating oil levels this
can wear thru the wire of the wire-wound resistor, causing an open circuit.
This is what happened to my unit.  As to the *open circuit equals full gauge*,
that has to do with the sensing of a small change in resistance with an 
unsteady voltage source (like the battery/alternator set-up in cars).  Being the
*Curious George* type of electrical engineer I drew everything out and figured
out how the system works.  Looked to be a pretty good way to handle the total
system to me.

BTW, I'm still looking for the answers to my questions:  What's going on w/
my dipstick/float combination and can I just cut off the old dipstick? 
Thoughts, anyone?

Thanks to Kyle Kehler for his thoughts on refinishing my oil tank.  I'm going
to use the Wurth stuff to coat the outside of the tank - it had some undercoat 
on it  orginally! ;-)


Thanks.
John Berry
jberrry@afit.af.mil

------------------------------

Date: Mon, 8 Nov 1993 10:43:38 -0500
From: John N Berry <jberry@afit.af.mil>
Subject: Re: Winter Storage


Paul,

Hello!  I've just done the same thing to my '75 911.  I acted a little too
early, though - we had some great driving weather the weekend after I took
the insurance off!

Well, here's all I'm going to do for storage:

1.  Charge the battery once a week.  I'll turn on an interior light for three
or four hours to drain it a little and then charge it up.  Lots of different
*trickle* type chargers around, I'm just going to use the low setting on my
manual job.

2.  Change the oil before too much longer ;-)  I'm short on free-time at the
moment.  I'll run it for a week or so this spring and then change the oil
again - gets all the water/gunk out.  I always do a filter change.  Even at
$10 a pop for a filter it's the cheapest 911 motor part around!

3.  Fill the tank up all the way before storage.  This keeps water for conden-
sating in the tank.

4.  Start the thing and let it run for about 20 minutes every two weeks or
so.  DON'T let it just idle though, keep the revs up so you have good oil
flow and everything comes up to temp.  Me, I'm going to open up all the garage
windows/doors, sit behind the wheel and pretend I'm racing down some Alpine
mountain pass in my Porsche.... ;-)

5.  Lots of different opinions on paint car.  I'd wash it down real good,
outside, underside, inside and wax it up.  Use a car cover if you have one
but I'm not going to sweat it.  Just wash it again this spring!

6.  Don't put it on stands for extended periods.  It will expose the shock
absorbers to corrosion.  I'm going to have my on stands for a while for some
work but then I'll take it down.  Just air up the tires to 55 psi or so and
roll it back and forth once a week.  Keeps from flat-spotting the tires.

This is by no means a complete list of what to do but it is the obvious.  Look
in the archieves or in Up-Fixin' for a more complete description.  Anyone
have any other ideas/tips?

Good luck and keep dreaming of spring!

John Berry
jberry@afit.af.mil
'75 Peru Red 911S Sunroof Coupe

Date: Mon, 8 Nov 93 08:40:18 PST
From: bart@research.att.com
Subject: carbon fiber brakes

re kevin's article on carbon brakes, i have some comments/questions regarding
bedding in procedures.

i got some cool carbons and a new set of front rotors this summer.  unfortunately
i missed a track event in the process and had to bed them in on the street (better
anyway, you say?  tell me why if you think so!).  per instructions, i warmed the
brakes up a little bit by dragging them a bit, then ran the car up to 70-80 and
braked hard to 40 or so, repeating until the brakes faded, then proceeded on my
way (across the country, as it turned out).

some time later i noticed that hard braking resulted in big-time shudder.  this
was 2000 miles later (nearly home).  later at a track event i encountered the
same shuddering until about the 3rd run session, when everything was fine.

happened again yesterday, only it got better faster.  actually it seems like they
shudder when cold, then are fine after about 5 laps.

are my rotors warped?  do these pads deposit goo on them while squealing in street
use?  why does it get better late in the track day?

Date: Mon, 08 Nov 1993 08:55:53 -0800
From: Mike Lee - Team Banana Racing <mikel@ichips.intel.com>
Subject: Re: It was bound to happen... 



>Here's the article.  There a drawing which goes along with it, and I
>would be happy to US-mail you (or anyone) a copy given and address.  Alas,
>I deleted my own fog lights recently, to use their space in the air dam to
>vent air to a new oil radiator.  Back to the drawing board...


There's one thing the article forgot that is generally done when alarms are
used to flash lights; add a diode between the alarm output and relay.  If a
thief should happen upon upon your car and set off the alarm, they could pop
off a light, short the leads, and cause enough current to be drawn to fry the
alarm control unit.  Now, I don't know how common it is for thieves to do this,
or whether or not it would actually work (doesn't seem to likely to me; I
think the fuses for the lights would pop first), but given that a diode is only
$.80 at Radio Shack, why take a chance?

Mike

record - so much for insurance statistical inferences based on tickets ;-).

Date: Mon, 8 Nov 93 15:23:23 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: Jacob's Electronic's Ignitions


Jacobs Electronics makes claims which cannot be substantiated and
which defy various laws of thermodynamics. They have a HIGH "fleece
the rubes" factor, and a BAD track record on giving hard info. Particularly
once they figure out that you have some idea how to herd electrons through
a capacitive discharge system...

My vote is for an MSD-7AL, the *heavy-duty* racing grade unit, plus
an Allison points-replacement module if you like. I use a 6AL myself,
because I got a very good deal on it, but will replace with a 7AL
when it fails.

Stan

Date: Mon, 8 Nov 93 20:16 EST
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: carbon fiber brakes

Bart asks about shuddering.

Did it start at or right after a track event?  If so, I would suspect
material transfer from the pads to the rotors.  This normally will clear
up after a few hundred miles of street driving.  If it doesn't, you may
have warped rotors.

The solution, in either case, would be to cool your rotors with something
like the Holbert Cool Brake set-up.

Kevin

Porschephiles #564                         Wed Nov 10 02:12:04 CST 1993
Date: Tue, 9 Nov 93 08:02:26 +0100
From: Magnus Ottenstrom <magot@weald.air.saab.se>
Subject: gearbox oil change

>The shop I hang around uses Mobil 1 synthetic in all Porsches, as far
>as I know.  Works fine.  This is in New England.  I found that Swepco
>made winter shifting too difficult.

>Kevin


I don't have this fresh in mind but the 911 (at least '77 - '82) transmission
oil has to meet the GL5 norm (hypoid additative). The Mobil 1, if I recall it
correctly, was therefore not recommended (I can be wrong). Mobilube 
SHC 75/90W (synthetic) meets this requirement and was therefore used in 
my gearbox. Works fine although I have not driven in in winter temperatures.

P.S. The filling is quite messy. I took a long hose, taped it to the filling
hole so the filling could be done above the RH rear wheel.

Magnus

Subject: What's a Porsche VIN worth?
Date: 8 Nov 1993 20:29:34 GMT
Organization: The Avant-Garde of the Now, Ltd.

I had a 1971 Porsche 914-6 incinerated in one of the recent fires in  
LA.  It's a complete loss--engine and wheels and windows all melted.   
While I was poking around at it yesterday, it occurred to me to wonder  
if the Vehicle Identification Number (VIN) and associated pink slip  
have any value separate from the scrap value of the car.  I heard a  
story about a guy who tried to convert 914-4 into -6, and I've heard  
rumors about people putting together cars around a particular VIN.  I'd  
be interested in hearing opinions about the value of a VIN for a  
relatively rare car (they made ~3200 914-6's), and especially  
interested in any suggestions regarding how one might identify an  
potential buyer for such a thing.

Date: Tue, 9 Nov 93 09:26:07 EST  
From: "Bruce Wm. Pigozzi"  <22107bwp@ibm.msu.edu>
Subject: Winter Storage

Paul and anyone else

John's advise will work but some argue that running for 20 minutes with no
load on the engine is not good, I'm not sure why but P-cars seem to thrive
on stress.  More important, if you're in a cold climate, just remove the
battery and put it on a trickle charger outside the car.  The worst thing,
maybe even worse than rodents nesting in your air cleaner, is to have your
battery freeze, break and run all over everything as you spend the winter by
the fire dreaming of summer driving!

I recommend the fresh oil change and fillup; I'd add that if you don't run
premium octane gas that you make this fillup with premium.  I've read/heard
that the gas can degrade as it just sits there (anyone care to enlighten
us?).

Avoid the stands or blocks but DO pump up the tires.  Rolling back and
forth is supposed to be good for both the tires and the bearings.

I like a product made in my neighborhood called OMNI BAG (approx $95).  It
is a heavy gage plastic bag that you roll your car into, toss in a couple of
desicant tubes (which come with the bag), fold up the end, and clamp it with
some heavy clamps (which also come with it).  The car comes out just like it
goes in.  Even in an unheated Michigan garage for the winter brake disks
come out with no rust at all.  (I've seen a reddish rust film form overnight
in some climates.)  I'm not affiliated with the company in anyway except I
bought the bag!  The owners are Porsche folks (and Alfa's) anyway.
I'll post the details if anyone is interested.  Granted, there is a problem -
it's kind of difficult to go out and sit in the car pretending winter's
over.
Bruce Pigozzi '84 944 (never seen snow)

Date: Tue, 9 Nov 93 10:03 EST
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Questions

>1. What size of wrenches are needed to remove both, the filling plug and
>   the drain plug on an '84 Carrera? Where can they be purchased?

17 mm. hex key.  Two possible sources.  First is to go to your generic
auto parts store and ask for a VW transmission wrench, same size and
often sold as such.  Second source is Snap-On, who sell a nice 17 mm
key socket for a 3/8" ratchet.

Kevin

Just to add my $.02...

I'd opt for the key socket and ratchet setup.  I purchased the
big hex wrench and found it to be a pain in the butt.  It was hard
to get it seated correctly (in part due to the incredible precision
used in the manufacturing of this tool ;-)).  Don't get me wrong, it
worked, but, the key socket with a short extension would be much 

better IMHO.

It may be possible to find the key socket from Sears/Craftsman if 

you're not inclined to chasing a Snap-On truck...

> The shop I hang around uses Mobil 1 synthetic in all Porsches, as far
> as I know.  Works fine.  This is in New England.  I found that Swepco
> made winter shifting too difficult.
> 

> Kevin
> 

> 


 During the past year of autocrossing and such, I encountered binding
of the shifter on the G50 in the RS America after the car had been
running continuously under load and temps up.  I called the dealership
about this phenomenon and was told that I was not the only one who 

had experienced the problem.  I asked whether changing tranny fluid
to synthetic would help and was told NOT to use synthetic in the G50.
The reasoning was that the synthetic is TOO slippery and causes problems
with the synchros.  As it turns out, the binding problem is apparently
the result of heat expansion of the linkage components.  


FWIW: I did use synthetic in the 915 of my '82 SC with no ill effects...

Drew

Date: Tue, 9 Nov 1993 13:06:28 -0500 (EST)
From: "Alexander J. Denner" <xdenner@minerva.cis.yale.edu>
Subject: Re: Questions

On Tue, 9 Nov 1993, L.E.Nicotra-WH-77122H(WH5672)W214 wrote:
> 2. There has been a lot of talk on the net about cast vs. forged wheels.
>    Can one of you mechanical engineering types out there explain what is the
>    actual forging process? I've heard of of forged pistons, tools, etc. and
>    it has always been a mistery subject to me.

Casting is when molten steel is poured into a mold and allowed
to cool.  The molten steel forms in the shape of the mold.  There
are different types of casting, for instance you can use sand
molds which are just "cracked" off the steel molding once has
hardened.

Forging is when hot steel is forced into a new shape.  For instance,
if you took a yellow hot steel rod and hit it with a hammer until
it was in the shape of a bar, you would have "forged" the bar.
Forgings are generally much stronger than cast parts.

A typical way to make, say a crankshaft, would be to cast 
a shape close to the desired shape, forge it, then machine
the surfaces that need close tolerances (bearing surfaces, etc.)
Something like a engine block will usually be cast (with a
sand mold) then the cylinders will be machined for higher
tolerances.


Alexander J. Denner			    denner-alexander@yale.edu
118 Mason Laboratory, Yale University       (203) 432-4339
9 Hillhouse Avenue
New Haven, CT  06520

Date: Tue,  9 Nov 93 12:07:30 PST
From: Mike Conte <mikecon@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE71....and the RE71R's


Just wanted to add some positive notes on the RE71Rs, since I have seen 
so much bad news about them here. I drove on them for a year or so 
before switching to Yoko 008Rs, like many did here in Seattle. The 
RE71Rs are great tires in the rain, very forgiving, and easy to drive. 
They give lots of warning and you don't have to be very precise while 
driving them. They don't last very long (shaving them to 2/32's helps) 
, and you need to keep the pressures up or they will roll over and 
trash the sidewall. The Yokes are faster, stiffer, and last much 
longer, partly because they have a harder compound and stiffer sidewall 
but also because the outside tread is molded as almost a solid block. 
They aren't as good in the rain as an RE71 with tread. The Yoke's 
require a tidier driving style since they won't slide as much, but I 
think they are a better 911 tire than the RE71 (the old Yoke RTUs of 
the same era were worse). So the RE71 is a great tire to learn on and 
you will have lots of fun. Use em up and then you can use the Yokes at 
the track. Or, do what some do and leave the RE71 at full depth and 
drive them on the street (expect about 10k miles and lots of rock 
chips)...but they are so soft it rides like a caddy!

Mike

914 gearbox...

theory, re-engineer the whole back half of the car to install a 901
thouh... (I have seen a 356 with a 2.4 liter 911 engine, where the
owner did just that...)

Date: Tue,  9 Nov 93 21:44:55 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: Quality of Automotion water pumps?

> I can call Automtion in the morning to find out what brand these
> are. (My mechanic had planned to order a rebuilt one directly from
> PCNA.) Assuming that it's the same brand some of you have gotten
> from them before, what's your opinion? I'm looking at $112 from
> Automotion vs. $300 from PCNA, so if the ones on sale are reliable,
> I'm very interested.

Well, the PCNA part comes with a warranty and is rebuilt as an OEM 
replacement, i.e., we/they hope it's within original specs.  If the 
thing fails, PCNA will help you fix the problem again.

Unless Automotion sells EXACTLY the same part, I'd be leary - I've seen 
too many "original" parts with identical part numbers from the original 
manufacturer, that are out of spec or otherwise inferior.  Through the 
PCNA channel, you're guaranteed highest quality.

You might consider the extra $182 as insurance.  What's the labor to 
replace the new pump if it goes bad?

Anyway, Patty, this is just a critical opinion from someone who demands 
high quality parts and performance!  Hope this helps,

		JBS

>     Don't know why, but experience (anecdotal) seemed to bear him out, 
>     particularly with marine batteries. I've always stored batteries up on 
>     a shelf, even in a garage, and they seem to "survive" the winter much 
>     better. Don't know if this is an old wives tale or what, but forwarded 
>     for what its' worth.

 ! sorry..deleted the poster's .sig from above para..
     
   My brother worked on Ferrari's, Bentley's, Rolls, Saab, etc.
   He never said anything like that.

   ** The battery-on-the-floor routine **IS** an old wive's tale. **
   ** Many years ago when batteries where constructed of tar and ockum(sp?)
   this may have been true, not with today's plastics tho'.

   Since your locale has winter, perhaps the battery being on the shelf
   actually kept it from freezing (heat rises) solid in the garage.
   When a battery loses it's charge, it can freeze. <bad>
   An unused battery will lose ~10% or more of its charge each month.
   As temperature drops, a battery's efficiency also drops.
   At 0'c a battery in good condition may have only 50% of its rated CCA
   (cold cranking amps).  CCA is usually rated at ~20'c (room temp)

   ** The best thing for a car battery is USE.  If you have to store a 
   battery for a season, the best thing is to give it a jolt of use
   at intervals (your choice < 30 days) in between charges.
   A low rate draw would be OK if the cycle does not go too deep.
   (car radio/tape deck).
 
   **  As cheap as batteries are these days,
   blow off maintenance and buy a new one (two) every 2-3 years to
   avoid a surprise and prevent damage to your car's electricals.

   ** I have seen sears offering an intelligent battery charger
   and maintenance device...~$60.  Seems to handle deep cycle
   batteries as well as car batteries.  I will get one if they
   have one in stock (they must be good, cuz they're always out).
   Schumacher (chicago) makes it.  on sale occasionaly too.
  -kyle
  <kehler@daisy.wichitaks.ncr.com>   <black14>
  73 914 2.0 rust guard black (Phoenix red L32K)
  76 914 2.0 spray can black (light Ivory L80E)

   UUuuhhH?  -Tim Allen

Date: Wed, 10 Nov 1993 12:52:07 -0600 (CST)
From: David Harris Stewart <stew@owlnet.rice.edu>
Subject: Bosch ABS

Does anyone out there know some of the technical details of the Bosch ABS
systems Porsche uses on their later model 2wd cars?  I'm hoping to adapt
the 928S4 computer to a 4wd Porsche based prototype being built at Rice
University in Houston.  We're using Torsen style worm and spur gear 
limited slip diffs front, rear, and center.  They are free on overrun and in
braking, so they won't interfere with the 4 wheel slip sensor system used
in the Bosch ABS as badly as in a Carrera 4.  I believe that model uses
two different sensitivity settings based on an additional acceleration 
sensor for slick and normal road conditions.  The 2wd computers don't need
this because the front wheels are always free to rotate and can be used 
as references.

Any info on the Bosch systems would be of interest.  Anyone know of a 
good book (short of the Bosch manuals which I have on order)?

Dave Stewart
'82 928
1160 HP GTS twin turbo in the lab

Date: Wed, 10 Nov 1993 16:30:14 -0500
From: John N Berry <jberry@afit.af.mil>
Subject: Winter is NOT Wunderbar

Here's some discussion/responses on this subject:

1.  Move to Arizona

In a heart beat!  Actually, I'd rather go to New Mexico but...  I'm stuck
here for awhile.

2.  Bruce Anderson Disagreeing... With Himself! (I don't think so...)

I looked up my source.  It was an old article in Up-Fixin' (Vol VII)
and in it he agreed with an earlier Up-fixin' article by someone else.
Run the car every once in awhile to keep water/corrosion out of the motor.
The earlier article is in Vol III.  Don't have page numbers with me.

In any event, I would under no circumstances:  a) let a motor sit for six
months without being started (unless you did some preservation - oil down
the cylinders, put in water-absorbing plugs, etc. as outline in the Vol III
article) or b) start a motor w/o letting it come up to temp.  That's the idea
behind letting it run at above idle (see my original post) for 20 min or so.
It drives away the moisture and splashes lots of oil around.

I agree w/ Bruce - rotate all parts that normally rotate.  Good idea.  Since
I'll have stands and a floor jack handy, that could be accomplished rather
easily.  Put it in gear and hope to god that it doesn't fall off the stands!

Is he changing tune?  I don't really think so.  It comes down to, as usual:
Use it or lose it!  It's bad to let it just sit.  I couldn't find any more
recent articles in the Up-Fixin' Index and haven't looked thru my un-indexed
issues recently.  Anyone remember any more recent (>85 or so) articles?

In any event, I think it's a must start the car everyonce in awhile and
exercise it.  Not everyday but more than zero times in six months!

3.  Batteries on concrete...

I usually keep a board or piece of insulation between the battery and the floor
if they are sitting out in the cold.  I guess don't really know why except that
it seems that a concrete floor is nice way to conduct heat from an object and
freeze it!  So, a little insulation allows the battery to warm up some as it
is charged.  Of course, if you go to the extreme and take it in the house, put
LOTS of stuff between it and your carpet or you'll have this battery-shaped
hole where the carpet used to be...

I concure w/ Kyle - put a load on the battery before you charge it.  Drain it
a little w/ the interior lights and your favorite cassette tape.  Then charge
it up.  I've done battery work for the Air Force so I've had to read up on
all these sorts of things.  Don't tell my boss but I'd rather be reading up
on Porsche stuff...

Well, I'm sure there's more to be said on the subject.  Thoughts?

John
jberry@afit.af.mil
'75 Peru Red 911S (that I wish I were driving right now!!!)

Date: Wed, 10 Nov 1993 16:26:21 -0600
From: jmb@wuchem.wustl.edu
Subject: Batter storage

	Not suprising the battery has a shorter life if on concrete during
the winter. More efficient thermal conductivity to the battery from the
concrete than from whatever shelving material you have (most likely).
Cold will lower the solubility of the electrolyte (depending on state of
charge) and can lead to your plates more rapidly ending up as salts on 
the bottom of the case instead of where they belong. No, I didn't go into
all the details here (such as why concrete never reaches ambient temp in 
the winter, at least the concrete in your garage or the details of the ion
exchange process occuring in the cells). Point is try to keep a battery as
warm as possible, below the vapor point of the electrolyte of course, during
the winter. Charging it will help it last longer if it must be exposed to
cold.    good luck JB

change a thing!

Since my first Porsche in 1969 (a '66 912) I've noticed that familiar
smell in every 911 I've owned.  It is distinctive, and not at all unpleasant
(to me, anyway).  In fact, it brings back so many memories that I
intentionally breathe deeply when I get in for the first time each
morning.  Next I pull on my driving gloves.  And then Walter Mitty comes
to mind...

I've often wondered what causes it since some of my cars had vinyl,
cloth, and leather interiors and yet all smelled alike.  But I've never once
considered anything to mask it.

I always thought every other owner felt the same.  Now I'm wondering if
something's wrong with me.

Lance.

Date: 11 Nov 1993 06:48:36 -0400 (EDT)
From: "Andrew J. Toth" <TOTHAND@hsdwl.utc.com>
Subject: Re: 914 restoration

Lins Kerr writes...
| 
| [snip]
| 
| A few questions......
| 
|     1. Other than the hoses are there any other items that need
| checking?, eg injectors sensors etc.  I will change oil and filter,
| put some oil down the bores, turn the engine over by hand and put
| fresh petrol (gas) in the tank.
| 
    It's a very good idea to check the elbow hoses that connect the
    injectors to the fuel "T".  These hoses were known to crack
    causing fuel to spray the engine compartment.  (Can you say fire?)
    Sometime last year, PCNA told Pano that a recall is still in effect 
    to replace these hoses.  You'll need to bring your VIN to the dealer 
    to see if this service has been performed on your car.  If it has, 
    and the hoses look cracked, *replace them*.  It's worth the effort.
    
    You might just want to see if it turns over before you do too
    much work.  The less you change, the less new problems you have
    to contend with.


|     2. The 914 is fuel injected. I have heard conflicting comments
| about the relative merits of fuel injection versus carbs....any
| comments?
| 
    See the net for the ongoing survey of FI vs carbs.  I have FI
    which is working just fine, but when I put a 2.0l in the car
    (1970 914) next year, I'll change to carbs.  By the way...anyone 
    have a 2.0 for sale?  I have a complete 1.8 for sale.


|     3. My 914 has some rust in front of the windscreen, and around the
| battery. Are any other areas likely rust spots?
| 
    Unfortunately, if it's quiet, you can hear a 914 rust.  I wouldn't
    worry too much about the rust in front of the windscreen, but the
    battery rust may be a major problem.  (Take it fromn someone who
    did a *complete* rebuild of the car for this reason.)  The problem
    is this: water hitting the battery would mix with the sulfuric acid
    present on the outside of the battery and drain down into the structure
    of the car.  The acid would corrode the metal around the battery tray
    and the area from the trailing arm inside mounting point to the right
    rear floor.  (I've seen even worse.)  As you might imagine, this makes
    a "Flexible Flyer" even more so.  Check this area from all angles
    as well as possible.  Tapping lightly on the underside with a hammer
    will give you audible cues as to the integrity of the structure.
    If the hammer goes through...well...

    A recall to add a cover to the battery is also still in effect.


| It's great to be able to get answers to my questions over the net as
| I am rather remote down here in New Zealand.  I hope you understand
| my message...I had trouble figuring out that 'coals' were
| electric motor brushes!
| 

    I'm glad you could participate.  It's always nice to help a fellow
    Porsche owner.  Remember, the answers you get are opinions, and you 
    should always do your homework before taking on the problem: ask the 
    net, read publications, and talk to trained service technicians...then 
    take the average.  Some people on the net like to consider themselves 
    a "guru" and will attack others who have differing opinions.  This act 
    is called "flaming".  Be cautious of the flames!

    Andrew J. Toth                             Katana Pilot
    tothand@hsdwl.utc.com                                  ===o&o>

    Speed costs money...how fast do you want to go?

Date: Thu, 11 Nov 1993 11:08:47 -0500 (EST)
From: hjdpci%borg.cis.rit.edu@ariel.unm.edu (H.J. Deeg )
Subject: Re: 914 restoration (KERRL)

Lins Kerr writes:
> 
>     3. My 914 has some rust in front of the windscreen, and around the
> battery. Are any other areas likely rust spots?

Yeah, there are some. When I bought my 914 I thought it was rust free - that
opinion got changed once i had a closer look into the engine bay with the
engine out. There is a "sink" just behind the firewall where water running
down over the battery can collect. It should have a hole in it for drainage
which easily plugs up. There might be rust down into the frame, and forward
into the firewall. This area is also rust prone on the left side of the car.
Then, there might be rust inside the lower firewall (i.e. the part of the
firewall if you look from the underside of the car; this is hollow, the
upper firewall isn't). That will most likely show up as rust in the left and
right rear corners of the floor pan and rust from the lower firewall into the
passenger compartment. The area behind and below the seats is also in danger
if the rear window's seals are bad: while driving in rain, water gets sucked
in and stays forever between the metal and the sound proofing rubber there.
You'd have to rip out most of the interior in the rear to see into these
places, not a great fun. Inside rust in the front floor pans and around the
pedals is also likely from leaky windows, wet shoes, spilled coffee etc; i had
enough of it and didn't dare to look too closely (and removing the sound deadener
on the floor pans is a real pain.).
Enough of that unpleasant stuff... 
- -------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Hans-Joerg Deeg                                              Tel (716)-475-7061
Center for Imaging Science                                   Fax (716)-475-5988
Rochester Institute of Technology                 email hjdpci@borg.cis.rit.edu
Rochester, NY 14623, USA                               or hdeeg@perseus.unm.edu

Date: Thu, 11 Nov 1993 11:26:53 -0500
From: Robert B Mohan <rmohan@afit.af.mil>
Subject: Phosphate-Free Coolant

FYI:

As I posted earlier, Prestone now makes a phosphate-free coolant
called "4/60."  I requested learned opinions on the product but 
got no response.  So I applied my golden rule:  If you want it 
done right, do it your damn self.

Contents of VW Autobahn coolant (blue): 

ethylene glycol (107-21-1)
diethylene glycol (11-46-6)
sodium tetraborate (1303-98-4)
sodium benzoate (532-32-1)

contents of Prestone 4/60 (yellow):

ethylene glycol (107-21-1)
diethylene glycol (11-46-6)
disodium sebacate (17265-14-4)

contents of "normal" coolant (green) include dipotassium phosphate
(7758-11-4) which is the aluminum corrosion culprit.  It is absent
in both above-mentioned coolants.

Performance-wise, all three coolants advertise freezing points of
 -34 degrees F for 50/50 mix with water.

Seems to me this makes Prestone 4/60 suitable for Porsches (water-
haulers only, of course).

A note about "Sierra" and other propylene glycol-based coolants.
There was a write-up in European Car (you should be skeptical
already) about "Mecca" cooling systems, using this type of
coolant.  With a few mods, such as a new thermostat and pressure
relief valve, cooling systems can be adapted to run with pure
coolant (ie. no water added).  This is a zero-pressure system,
with a higher boiling point, lower freezing point, but less heat
capacity (water has very high specific heat).  Big selling
points are corrosion resistance, 100,000 mile lifespan, and
environmental friendliness.

Bob Mohan

rmohan@afit.af.mil

Date: Thu, 11 Nov 1993 12:46:24 -0500
From: Tom Haapanen <tomh@metrics.com>
Subject: Re: BBS Wheels fall apart

> My friend has a 911 turbo and was out yesterday abusing the speed
> limit. He was taking a corner pretty hard when he started to get a
> violent shimmy in the steering wheel so he started slowing down as
> quick as possible and at 50mph the car spun out on him. It appears
> that his BBS three piece wheels decided they wanted to be three
> individual pieces. Did quit a bit of damage to the suspension The
> wheels have never been hit hard or in any way damaged. The question is
> has anyone heard of these wheels coming apart before?

Not those, or on a 911 Turbo, but ... way back in '83 or so, I had a
set of (original) EMPI 2-piece wheels (with 195/60-15 P6s) on my '72
Super Beetle. One night I was taking a fairly rapid corner, when all of
a sudden the there was a jarring sensation and a huge clunking noise
from the outside rear wheel. I thought a wheel had fallen off, but in
fact it had split in two; several of the bolts had sheared off. The tire
was fine, but I had to buy some EMPI clone wheels as replacements... :(

The explanation I believed at the time was that originally ('69 or so)
the EMPI wheels were made for racing use, and this used a different
aluminum alloy.  This alloy was supposed to be stronger, but it was
more suspect to metal fatigue as it aged.

I don't know whether there's any truth to that theory, but...

> } #$%*  Is that still true? Performance Products now has a
> } higher grade of "lookalike" that is pressure cast - still
> } not forged (at least not in the mettalurgical sense) but
> } supposedly stronger and straighter than gravity cast.
> } 
> 
> The reason for the problems with FUCHS replicas is that forged metal si
> SO much stronger than cast (pressure or not).  When companies made the
> fuchs replicas they simply copies the fuchs design without reinforcing
> for lower strength material.
> 
> The wheels that are pressure cast replicas of other pressure cast wheels
> are generally OK because they were engineered with that material in mind
> and are appropriately reinforced.
> 
> Imagine taking a '60s steel wheel from a US passenger car, only when you
> made it you just took a cast of the steel wheel and poured in some moten
> Aluminum.  You could prob. bend it by stepping on the rim.  The problem
> with the copying of fuchs is just that.
> 
> Porsche has only ever made 3 popular forged wheel styles - Fuchs, 928
> flat style wheels, and the 951S forged wheels.
> 
> If you are still not convinced that this is the case compare a 944 turbo
> cup (magnesium) wheel to a 1987 phone dial of the same width (8"). If you do so
> you'll notice that the 8" wheel actually LOOKS like a 7" wheel from the
> outside because the material in the webbing is about 3/8" thicker and
> the whole center sticks out that much farther.  We noticed this when we
> put on wheel fans. The front 8" magnesium wheel really doesn't accept a
> fan since the center sticks out farther than the rim.  The rear 9" mag
> wheel is nearly identical to the 951 (87) 8" wheel and accepts the same
> wheel fan.
> 
> FYI,
> 	Steve

> put on wheel fans. The front 8" magnesium wheel really doesn't accept a
> fan since the center sticks out farther than the rim.  The rear 9" mag
> wheel is nearly identical to the 951 (87) 8" wheel and accepts the same
> wheel fan

Date: Thu, 11 Nov 93 15:25:54 EST
From: Jeffrey A. Shorey <js@ee.duke.edu>
Subject: Re: Re: Batteries

Vol writes:
> Has anyone done this?  Any leads on a cheaper source than Automotion?

I just go down to my local foreign/domestic/but-not-porsche auto parts
place and buy a standard size battery (I believe it's a size 58 or something
like that).  It fits into the left battery box sideways, so the ground
terminal is inside the box and the positive terminal is outside.  The vertical
dimension is the critical one - it just barely fits into the box.  In fact,
I put one of those acid neutralizer felt washers on the ground terminal last
time I got a new battery and it didn't have enough clearance for the terminal
to fit.  There's about an inch free play in the horizontal dimension - I have
a couple of wooden blocks to keep it from moving around (in additional to
the stock metal strap).  Costs about $50.

I've seen a pair of gel batteries specially made to fit into the boxes of
the pre-74 911's advertised in the back of on of the relevant magazines -
does anyone know if the Optima 800 battery (a stand-alone gel battery advertised
elsewhere, e.g.  Automechanika) will fit in the box in a similar fashion to
the above?  The price per battery it the same (around $100), so I'd rather go
with the single if it fits.

 - Jeff Shorey
'73 911S Coupe (black on purple)

Date: 11 Nov 93 19:22:40 EST
From: ED SCRIPPS <73200.2362@CompuServe.COM>
Subject: Fact or Fiction?

Here something I'd like some opinions about.

An article in "EuroSport Car" magazine entitled "Cross 
Culture - Tuning your European car for American use" 
suggests that European cars ignition settings are designed 
for wetter-colder climates and slower stop and go driving. 
It proposes increasing the spark plug cap to get better 
performance. 

Quoting here:
"While suitable for the conditions they were originally 
designed for, they may not be correct for the different 
driving styles common in America. Usually when driving in 
the U.S., poor throttle response, rough idle and decreased 
mileage can be reduced by increasing your spark plug caps 
over factory settings."

"These larger gaps will enable your engine to run much 
smoother with better mileage and noticeably more power."

Here are some examples given in the article:
  "If you tune up your vehicle often, i.e., change plugs: 
  Change spark plug gap (+) .006; Four percent improved gas 
  mileage, noticeable smoother operation. No change in power."

  "If you only tune up your vehicle when it runs badly: Change 
  spark plug gap (-) .004; Allows you an additional 5,000 
  miles between tune ups. Increase in full throttle power by 
  two-percent. Lose three-to-five percent mileage."

  "If you drive to achieve maximum power (full throttle): 
  Change spark plug cap (-) .010; Six percent improvement in 
  power at 4,000 rpm. Better starting with fouled plugs. Seven 
  percent loss in mileage when driving slowly. Decrease in 
  tune up frequency by 25 percent."

The article also discusses spark plug "indexing". In a nut 
shell it says you should install the plugs so that the open 
side of the electrode is facing the incoming fuel/air 
mixture. 

This all sounds a little suspicious to me, but then that's 
not saying much.

For the record the article was written by Dr. Christopher A. 
Jacobs, PH.D., E.E. who has written "The Doctor's Step-by-
Step Guide to Optimizing Your Ignition".

Any thoughts on these "tricks"(my term) or the book.

 -Ed-

Date: Thu, 11 Nov 93 21:48:32 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914 head problem

>I've got a problem with a 914 2.0 4 cylinder head off my '76.
>#1 cylinder had an intake valve seat fall out of the bore.
>No apparent damage was suffered other than the seat hanging in the head.

You need to *carefully* check that connecting rod, piston, and wrist pin
for deformity and cracks. Magnaflux all ferrous parts, dye check the
others. Seriously.

>I took the head to a shop for repair and when I got it back the 
>valve with the repair was set into the head ~0.50" deeper than the
>other valve.  This makes the valve stems uneven in length, visually.

Man, what a turkey repair job! 

>It was claimed that the head was serviced by a reputable VW head repair
>shop.  Seems that an experienced shop would recognize the consequence
>of altering the combustion chamber volume and installed spring height
>this has caused.

Reputable. Uh-huh. Sure. Bet they sell waterfront property in FL too...

>I'm sure the head can be fixed, but the costs are mounting and i want
>to do it RIGHT.  I'm no turkey..but im sure there are some in this town now.

Send said head to H.G. Bieker Engineering. He'll fix it up *RIGHT*. There
are two (and only two) ways to do it: (1) Gene Berg's way - cut your own
valve seat from a length of chromoly tubing to the correct length, 
press into the head, do a valve job (hey, this worked *several* times for
VW dragster engines I've built...) *or* (2) remove seat from head,
weld up seat area, re-machine to correct depth, re-install seat. The
(2) way is about $100, the (1) way is about $40 if you can get Gene or
Gary Berg to do it for you and too damned expensive if anyone else anywhere
else does it, if you know what I'm saying.... Needless to say, Bieker does (2).

>Here's the question since I cant reach my mechanic friend till next week:
>Is an unequal valve stem length bad?

Yes. It has two problems. First, you now have interesting deformities in
your combustoin chamber which change the displacement hence compression for
that piston *and* give you edges that may retain heat and cause 
detonation/preignition/dieseling depening on how bad it is, not to
mention ruining the head in the process. Second, you have way hosed
up valve train geometry now, which will put extra strain on your rocker arm
and potentially cause galling of the shaft, *plus* will "shorten" the
available space for the pushrod, making it necessary to either cut the 
pushrod down or making it impossible to adjust the valves. Note that none
of these eventualities are very pleasant.

>Implications?

See above. 

BTW, only 2.0 heads are worth fixing. There are enough good 1.7/1.8 heads
out there that you can just chuck them and get another used one. 2.0 heads
new, minus all the hardware, are over $800 and used are over $200 most of 
the time. And most of the used ones need *some* repair work -- typically
cracked near the spark plug hole (another fix Bieker does really well)
or having stripped stud holes.

Stan

Date: Fri, 12 Nov 93 10:19:24 GMT
From: smc@ingres.com (Steve Chapman)
Subject: Re: BBS wheels

> My friend has a 911 turbo and was out yesterday abusing the speed
> limit.  He was taking a corner pretty hard when he started to get a
> violent shimmy in the steering wheel so he started slowing down as
> quick as possible and at 50mph the car spun out on him. It appears
> that his BBS three piece wheels decided they wanted to be three
> individual pieces. Did quit a bit of damage to the suspension The
> wheels have never been hit hard or in any way damaged. The question is
> has anyone heard of these wheels coming apart before?
> 

Hmm, this is going to be a bit contentions but here goes. IMHO
Split-Rims as these type of wheels are known in the UK are plain bad
news as a road wheel. They are pandering to the desire of looking like
a racer, where having a number of different sizes of centre section
allows you to play with offset/width et all. It also allows you to
only replace the outer rim edge if you kerb the thing. I don't think
anyone on the really uses the ability to disassemble the wheel to fit a
tyre, 'cos it takes longer than doing a 930 plug chop in the middle
of winter.

Friends of mine who have used these wheels (yes BBS), have had all
sorts of problems. Ranging from leakage around the 'O' ring where the
edge butts to the centre section to catastrophic failure when a number
of the bolts have sheared. Road salt is a real killer here, esp. when
it gets to work on the threads of the Allen bolts. They have all given
up now and gone back to one piece rims. I would guess that if you want
the look on a road car, you should go for the Audi approach and have a
purpose built dress one piece casting with the 'bolts' doing nothing
but looking pretty tapped into the edge of the rim.

My advice would be to cut one meter diameter circles out of plate
glass, for the remaining rims, mount it on top, and use as coffee
tables.

Steve Chapman - 
'77 911 Carrera 3 Sport Coupe - black everywhere

Kyle and Brendin,
