Those little Porsche Spec. books available from
Performance Products and other sources for a few
bucks have a wealth of information as far as 
specifications and measurements. Its like having a 
pocket sized shop manual. They show virtually all engine
measurements and wear limits.

The book probably gives acceptible limits for valve stem
height and would be excellent ammunition to either get
your money back or have the shop do the job right (I don't
think I would give them another chance)

There is also a really neat little tool that you can
get that fits down over the valve stem and spring and
has a scribed line for the correct assembled valve spring
height for your car, to help you determine the correct 
number of shims. I'm not sure if they come in sizes for
914's but they are well worth the $35 or $40 if you can 
find one. 

Good luck,

Reid

Date: Fri, 12 Nov 1993 09:14:54 -0500
From: Steve Timmins <timmins@chopin.udel.edu>
Subject: Re: wheels

Cast fuchs:

These wheels have been known to fail due to CORNERING loads alone in
AutoCross.  The cornering loads creade shear stresses in the webbing
which initiate mode III crack growth leading to catestrophic failure,
i.e. the centers HAVE been known to rip out of the center.

Remember one thing:  Cast aluminum (especially gravity cast) is
non-ductile, i.e. brittle and generally fails via crack growth and
consolidation initiated from surface imprefections.  Cast materials
have almost NO ductility, which is why engine blocks crack and not
bend.  Blah Blah Blah......

Forged materials start out as cast then have their surfaced deformed
by hot working so that work hardening occurs on the surface and
throughout the structure.  In this way cracks are closed durring
deformation and surface toughness AND hardness are both improved.
Fatigue, which is a crack-initited failure mode is also nipped in the
bud as surfaces on forged materials are in a state of compression due to
deformation, which acts to close cracks.  Also, lattice structures are
irregular surpressing crack growth.

Pressure cast wheels have an inherently smooth surface and
fall somewhere in between.

Date: Fri, 12 Nov 93 09:25:51 EST
From: fhp@unipress.com (Fred Pack)
Subject: Valentine radar

12 NOV

Yes, I have had one a year.

I ordered it sight-unseen as soon as I saw the 1st ad in C&D. (I knew of his
reputation and have a G-analyst, so I expected the Valentine 1 would be very
good.)

It didn't come for about 5 months  -- immediately the orders got all
backlogged.  (When they told me there was a 3 month wait, I asked if it was
vaporware and was assurred that it wasn't -- simply that they were receiving
many, many orders more than could be filled.)

After the 3 months dragged on to 4, I even called them and spoke to Valentine
himself about the delay. He told me that QA on these microparts is VERY hard.

Finally it arrived.

It definitely works.  There are 2 antennas builtin (forward and behind) so it
finds the badguys whereever they are.  Of course, I can't attest to the
X/K/Xa, etc stuff which C&D tested, but I assume that everything functions
properly. The arrows are neat: they light forward, backwards, or sideways to
indicate where the badguy is. The device is appreciably larger than my old
Passport, so it is more intrusive. It only runs from the car's power supply --
no built-in battery.

Recent reviews rave about it. I hear that there are still very long leadtimes.


Complaints: none. I love it.

Comment: 

	It has 3 modes of operation (increasing intelligence of radar
	filtering out).  It doesn't allow you to change the default mode, and
	since the best one is the 3rd choice, you have to reset it each time
	you bootup.

Date: Thu, 11 Nov 1993 22:16:08 -0600 (CST)
From: David Harris Stewart <stew@owlnet.rice.edu>
Subject: 928 GTS

Stefan-

>What's a GTS and how do you get 1160 HP out of it?
>How do you control it on the street.

Porsche inroduced the first major engine upgrade for the 928 since 1987
with the 1993 928 GTS.  The stroke was increased to give it about 5.4 L
displacement and the main bearing placement is different.  The engine I
have is actually a factory new casting, but of the older S4 design.  I
then modified it so that it is more similar to the GTS engine than the old
S4.  I designed a new crankshaft that has 1 cm more stroke than the S4 did,
for about 5.698 L, and machined it out of vacuum degassed 4340 billet and
nitrided it.  New conrods were machined out of a similar material and 
shorter forged pistons lower compression ratio to 9.2:1.  Stronger head
and crankcase studs were added and I'll o-ring the heads before running
at full power.  A dry sump system takes care of lubrication.  Add
ceramic, low friction and oil shedding coatings to the various components,
do a little detail work on some stress raisers in the castings, plumb a 
reverse flow cooling system in and, simple as that, you have a fantastically
strong race-ready block that can handle the stress of over 1200 HP.

Then I add two Garrett turbos that can boost 30 psi each, massive intercoolers,
a custom neural net to detect detonation, a fully sequential fuel injection
system, two massive fuel pumps, custom rails and injectors, and about a
zillion hours of development.  The result is the power source for my senior
mechanical engineering project at Rice University.  

Getting this to the ground is a task, so my group has designed a 3 diff
4WD system with Torsen style worm and spur gear limited slip units to 
apportion torque where it is needed.  A fly by wire throttle with built in 
traction control helps, as will ABS in stopping it.

If you have any specific questions, me or one of my group would be happy
to field them.  We would also welcome any comments from the super-high 
performance junkies out there.  If you have entered the realm of truly 
serious performance, or wish to, I'd like to hear what you would like to 
see in a no-limits automobile.

David Stewart
stew@owlnet.rice.edu

Date: Fri, 12 Nov 93 08:57:13 -0800
From: kwatson@wv.MENTORG.COM (Keith Watson)
Subject: Re: Fact or Fiction?

> An article in "EuroSport Car" magazine entitled "Cross 
> Culture - Tuning your European car for American use" 
> suggests that European cars ignition settings are designed 
> for wetter-colder climates and slower stop and go driving. 
> It proposes increasing the spark plug cap to get better 
> performance. 

I also read this article. I was pretty disappointed with this new
magazine in general but that is a different topic.  As I was
reading the article I was wondering if the author might have
something.  Then I decided to go read the name of the author...

> For the record the article was written by Dr. Christopher A. 
> Jacobs, PH.D., E.E. who has written "The Doctor's Step-by-
> Step Guide to Optimizing Your Ignition".

One of the things he implied in the article is none of this will
do you any good unless you get a different ignition system first.
Look on the inside of the front cover and see who is trying to
sell what.  Why, it's Dr. Jacobs' company Jacobs Engineering
selling ingnition systems.  And making claims of huge HP gains
(over factory figures).

Something else just clicked as I was writing this.  In our local
PCA newsletter one of the local guys wrote an article about visiting
the Porsche factory.  He was describing watching an engineer do a
dyno test of a new 911 engine.  I don't remember the exact numbers but
the factory figure is 250hp and this engine was 264hp.  The tour guide
just smiled and said the published figure is a little conservative.

Keith Watson
81 911SC Targa (for sale)

Date: Fri, 12 Nov 1993 12:08:14 -0600 (CST)
From: David Harris Stewart <stew@owlnet.rice.edu>
Subject: Rice Project

In response to Bob Mohan's comprehensive list of questions...

>Sure, I have a few academic questions about your senior project.

>Is this Grad or Undergrad work?
I'm an undergraduate senior and will graduate in May.

>How long of a project is it? A group Project? Like how many?
The work I am doing in conjunction with Rice and five other rice
students will end in May.

>Who pays?  You or the school?
I have a small company that does automotive design work on a
contract basis.  Composites, computers, high perf stuff. My
partner and I are financing the entire project.

>Is money a constraint on your design?
We have been moderately successful, so when I set out to design
the best performing car in the world, I didn't have to worry 
about expense.  It ended up costing a small fortune, though
much less than what a major design house would spend on a
prototype of this quality.

>What are you doing to the drivetrain to handle 1200 HP?
>(or the chassis for that matter?)  Titanium Indycar 
>driveshafts, etc?
The drivetrain is a freshly designed 4WD system with a
variable torque split to all four wheels.  The main driveshafts
(there are two) are 3 inch diameter steel or 3.5 inch aluminum
with U-joints (angles are fixed with a max of 3 degrees).  The
halfshafts use Porsche CV joints and stub axles with custom
shafts (300ksi yield).  The chassis is a composite design that
I can't discuss in public.

>Is this going to be a dragster or will it handle turns, etc.?
The car has a mid-engined layout with room for four full size 
passengers (thanks to a 120 in. wheelbase and a forward design
where the driver's feet are just over the steering rack).  The
suspension has active spring and damper rates, as well as 
variable ride height.  It's a GT car with a top speed as high
as the driver is brave or the road is long.  Should pull a high
9 on street rubber, or run with the best at Le Mans.

>Do you have an aerodynamics team?
You're talking to him.  Two years of development with the bulk
of the analysis done on a radical program run on a YM-P.  If 
you want to know more you'll have to call me voice and I'll
have to fax you a non-disclosure agreement...
The results are a .17 Cd. Great care was taken to properly
locate the air intakes so as not to increase the drag of the
computer design.  I have seen so many low drag prototypes that
have no provisions for cooling without destroying the flow 
over the front of the car.  Just melted looking blobs or
impractical designs like the ford probe IV (the front wheels
do NOT turn and there is room for a radiator for a 8 horse
engine at best).  I've added an extensive active-aero system
as well.  Under braking the brake ducts open full, the Cd
goes up to .4 or so and the Cp moves rearward.

>How about a weight reduction tiger team?
The target was 3200 lbs with glass and a full interior, but the
chassis was so light, I haven't worried about it in the least.

>Are you doing appropriate brake and suspension upgrades?
See above on suspension.  The brakes are a new AP six-pot 
design with 14 in. rotors.  I'm working on ABS.

>Who gets the car when it's done?
ME.  I'll be selling it off (sob) after I make the rounds of 
the free press.  The entire car was designed with limited
production in mind, so I may build a few more depending on 
the market.  I have more interest than I can cope with now,
so maybe that won't be a problem.

>I'm a grad student at the Air Force Institute of Technology,
>School of Engineering, and we always enjoy a high-tech,
>high-performance discussion.  If you have any questions
>and would like opinions with an aviation/military slant,
>then fire away.  We specialize in aero, astro, electrical
>engineering, ops. research, and physics, with a little
>mech. materials, optics/lasers thrown in.

I would like the opinion of the Porschephiles on quite a few
topics.  All the attributes of this car are tunable.  The 
throttle is fly-by-wire and the suspension can be set up
to mimic any kind of handling characteristics.  I'll have
to set all of these eventually and I want to give it the 
most driver-friendly persona possible.  As I get to 
specifics, I'll post.  Otherwise fire away in general.

One thing.  The mechanicals will be finished in May, and
the body, aero, interior and programming the May after that.
I've always hated advance hype about new automotive designs, 
so if we could keep this away from the almighty Press, my
life would be a little easier.

Thanks for the interest, and sorry for the long message-

David Stewart
stew@owlnet.rice.edu

Date: Fri, 12 Nov 93 13:13:40 PST

Subject: Summary - Paint Shops in Bay Area



Thanks to all those who responded to my request for recommendation 
of quality paint shops in the Bay Area for my 911.  For the benefit 
of others, I have provide a summary of information I have compiled 
from mail sent either directly to me or posted on the internet.  

In addition, I received mail recommending excellent shops in Los 
Angeles for $3500 and Houston for under $3000 (Thanks Stan and Scott).  
I found a shop locally so I did not pursue the Houston or Los Angeles 
alternatives.   

I chose L&M Body Shop.  I found their price to be the lowest and the 
quality the same as the other more expensive shops.  Given, 
I'm not an expert in determining quality paint jobs.  But the following 
criteria is what I used to judge a good paint job:  1) No ripples or 
waves in the body.  2) No orange peel in the paint.  3) No paint on 
any of the seals or other parts that are not supposed to be painted. 
4) Evidence that the car was taken apart to properly paint the car.
ie. doors, windows etc.  Please enlighten me if my criteria are 
incredibly naive.


Moal's Body Shop - Oakland
- --------------------------
Does not do complete paint jobs.
       

California Classics - Alemeda
- -----------------------------
Quoted me $4300 parts not included.  Very good quality.  The price  
also includes extra stuff like an engine steam clean and refinishing 
the rubber on the tail, mirrors etc.  I was able to talk him down 
from $5500 to $4300 providing I have him paint the doors and hood 
on my other 911 as well.  


Cal-West - Sunnyvale
- --------------------
Heard it was taken over by a new owner.  As a result, I did not want to
take a chance on a shop whose reputation was built upon a previous 
owner. 
    
   
BodyStyle - San Jose
- --------------------
Can't quite remember the quote.  I think it was around $8000 to $10000
all parts included and virtually all seals replaced.  Very good 
quality.   


L&M Body Shop - Campbell 
- ------------------------
Quoted $3500 including seals for front and rear windshields, doors, 
fender to cowl and windshield squirters.  Will install other seals
as well if you provide the parts.  Very good quality.   


C&C Body Shop - Mountain View
- -----------------------------
Doesn't do full paint jobs.


An enthusiast who works from home
- -------------------------------------------- 
Did not want to paint my car in the winter.   
    

Stephen Chan

Date: Fri, 12 Nov 1993 16:57:31 EST
From: James J Dempsey <jjd@spserv.bbn.com>
Subject: Re: "truly serious performance" 

> >Seems to me that somewhere around 1000 hp we have achieved truly frivolous
> >performance.  But then I'm the guy that thinks racing formulas should be de-
> 
> Actually, this is not even up to Mark Donahue's definition of "adequate
> power", if I remember correctly. I believe that he defined it as being
> able to spin the tires at any speed.

The story Mark Donohue told in "The Unfair Advantage" was that when he
first went to look at the 917(/10?) at Weissach, he went out for a
test on the test track and afterwards the Porsche engineers asked him
if the car had enough power.  He said "No".  Weeks later after
improving the engine and another test they again asked him if there
was enough power and he said "No".  After some number of iterations of
this, they finally asked when there would be enough power and he said
that "there would be enough power when I can spin the tires at the end
of the main straight."  Evidently the engineers were amazed and
couldn't believe that he could possibly want that much power.

This led directly to the 917/30, I imagine.

		--Jim Dempsey--
		jjd@bbn.com

Date: Fri, 12 Nov 93 15:32:36 -0800
From: kolner@kirchhoff.EE.UCLA.EDU
Subject: Repainting a 911

Regarding Steve Chan's search for a good paint shop in the Bay
Area, I offered him the following opinion:

Dear Steve,

I was reading your synopsis of Bay Area paint shops
and it was all very interesting until I got to the
end. Where you said that you were going to paint
your Turbo black while it is currently (and originally, I
presume) Metallic Blue.

I can't emphasize enough to you what a GREAT MISTAKE you
will make if you alter the original color of your 911.
I have owned 911's for 10 years and I have looked at hundreds
for potential purchase. Nothing short of an obvious blown 
engine (or bad rust) brings down the price of a used 911 faster than
non-original paint. It suggests two things: (1) There was collision 
damage or it is hiding rust. (2) The owner does not respect the value
of the car and therefore probably doesn't take good care of it.
This last point may seem frivolous but people who change the color
of a 911 for aesthetic reasons without going to BARE METAL everywhere
and replacing the paint code plate inside the door jamb, are
not typically viewed as enthusiasts who really take good
care of their cars. I know this sounds judgemental but I have 
seen a strong correlation in my travels. There will, of course,
be the odd exception (they usually seem to be racers bound for the
track and don't care about market value).

If, on the other hand, you are returning the car to original color,
bravo for you. You can find all of its original attributes from 
Porsche Cars of North America in Reno as pointed out several
net-grams ago.

Again, please reconsider. A repainted 911 with one color outside and
another one tucked away somewhere looks like s**t and scares away
a fair number of potential buyers.  You are prepared to pay a lot
of money for this job and you want it done right. You will loose
what you are putting into it (and perhaps more) if you change the
color.

These are my opinions. Do what you like.

 -Brian Kolner   kolner@ee.ucla.edu

Date:         Fri, 12 Nov 93 18:52:04 EST
From: Jim Foster <MADIF@rohvm1.rohmhaas.com>
Subject:      Turnsignal problem on 87 944S

Posted for my better half:

HELP!!!
I have a 1987 944S that I think has a Gremlin in the electrical
system.  Yesterday as I left for work, I flipped my turnsignal
indicator on and instead of the usual rhythmic click I got one
flash then nothing.  I figured I had a burned out bulb and checked
that after I got to work.  The bulb wasn't burnt!  I fiddled around
with the light assembly for a couple minutes and tried again...it
now blinked!  I started the car to go home...no signal again.  I
messed around with it at home and it started to work again!!!.
This morning it didn't work again...however about half way to work
it started blinking erratically.  This afternoon it did the same
thing.  I tried contact cleaner on the wiring harness, the bulb and
the contact point.  To sum up...the bulb blinks correctly when the
engine is turned to accessary, blinks slower than it should when
the engine is running...and doesn't blink when the car is cold. I
hope someone has an answer other than many hours at the shop! :-)

Date: Fri, 12 Nov 93 18:51:43 CST
From: tonye@weissach.eng.hou.compaq.com (Tony Eggers)
Subject: Re: RePainting?

DavidOB writes:

  Well, I've been looking at lots of 911s over the past few months
(23 cars, I have a log book) and personally, I wouldn't buy a 911 if
I could tell it had been painted. So, the paint job should DEFINITELY
not change color, and should be detailed enough so that even the
inside of the door jambs (and other such places) have been finished
so as not to reveal painting (either by color shade or paint ridge).
For a complete repaint, the cars I've seen have knocked $2000 to
$3000 off of what the car would have sold for. Apparently, the 911
buying public feels the same way, just ask any pre-owned Porsche
dealer. 

  So, Stephan, I know you didn't ask for this opinion, but I'll give
it anyway for the sake of this discussion. If the car NEEDS repainted
due to rock chips, fading, vandalism, damage repair, etc. I would say
go ahead and have the car repainted THE SAME COLOR. That way it won't 
cost you the amount of the paint job PLUS lost resale value.

  Personally, I think black is a good color for concours but it shows
dirt and scratches for too easily for street or track use. Maybe that's
why it's a good color for concours, flaws are easy to spot with the
naked eye.

Think about it. Ask some other people for their opinions. You might
just change your mind.

Tony

Date: Sat, 13 Nov 1993 11:12:28 -0600 (CST)
From: David Harris Stewart <stew@owlnet.rice.edu>
Subject: Frivolity


>Seems to me that somewhere around 1000 hp we have achieved truly frivolous
>performance.  But then I'm the guy that thinks racing formulas should be de-
>fined by limiting the amount of fuel.  Give each team a fixed quantity, let
>them use whatever shape size induction displacement etc. car they want.  You'd
>get great racing, interesting strategies, and quicker technology spin-offs for
>real-world cars.

I can't totally disagree with this.  But, then again, there is something 
frivolous about a Porsche and I love them none the less.  This prototype
was meant as an exercise in every leading edge automotive field possible.
It turned out that if I applied the latest technology in engine management,
I could get over 1000 HP with relative ease and tractability.  Keeping it
under safe control has turned out to be a worthy challenge.

You might be interested to know that this engine was not only designed with
maximum power in mind.  At light part throttle conditions it will go into a
lean burn mode (though not as radical as the Honda system), and runs in 
closed loop using info from two lambda sensors.  It also uses a new quick
light catalytic converter (a non-heated system).  We had two constraints on
the maximum output of the car, 1) we wanted it to come in under the gas
guzzler limits, and 2) we want it to be able to pass 1995 CA emissions 
regulations.  With the level of control that our computer system gives us, 
it looks like this will be possible.  The low low low Cd helps on the 
highway cycle and brings us in well under the wire.

Environmental responsibility aside, there is something undeniably exciting,
even addictive, about being traction limited all the way up to 140 MPH.

I, too, would love to see the racing formulas get more relevant to the
passenger car.  Spending millions on optimizing the aerodynamics of a 
car that runs 1-2" off the ground really does seem frivolous.
I guess that did bring us the moving ground plane wind tunnel, but I have
yet to see a production design that put any real emphasis on aerodynamics.

David Stewart
stew@owlnet.rice.edu

------------------------------

Date: Sat, 13 Nov 93 11:38:18 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: RePainting?

>Now, given the above information, will the resale value of my car be 
>affected by a different color paint job?  I'm tending to think not.
>But please let me know if I am wrong.

Give the above, it probably won't matter. *BUT* I **HIGHLY** recomment 
that you "do it right" and strip the body (i.e. take everything off the car, 
not spray with Paint-Be-Gone) so that a *good* job can be done.
This means engine compartment, front storage compartment, all the
doors/lids/whatever, under the carpet inside the car, etc.

If there's anything still showing original color that's casually visible, you 
won't be happy, and you *won't* be able to sell it very easily. 
Cars with that sort of history are best sold to people who want to
tinker with them, which already means you won't get top dollar. But
then, if it's a theft recovery, presumably you didn't *pay* top
dollar so it doesn't matter...

Just be sure you're painting it because you *want it to be that color*
not because you think it will help the value, and spray away!

Stan

Date: Sat, 13 Nov 93 21:55:23 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: State-Farm

I find all this State Farm bashing to be quite interesting. In all of
my dealings with them, I have had *zero* problems, including about a
$14k repair to my 911 after I was rear-ended. Factory parts, some
serious time on an alignment rack, weight checking the car, all
the stuff you need to do to make a 911 *perfect* in collision repair.
They paid, didn't ask questions, didn't complain.

And I didn't have to threaten, call my lawyer, or anything. I just explained
that this was how it was going to be, took the car to the shop I knew
had the 911 heavy collision experience needed, and left town for about
six weeks. Lots of FAXes went back and forth, some photos got FedExed, 
and I had a few people stop by to check progress for me, but when I
returned, I was completely satisfied except for the way they had tinted
my back glass (since it popped off the Targa bar, I figured this was a great 
time to get it done *RIGHT*, and someone blew that. Sigh.).

State Farm came by, inspected the car, inspected the receipts of the stuff
used to fix it, and droppd of the check. No further problems.

Stan

Date: Sun, 14 Nov 93 21:51:12 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: Turnsignal problem on 87 944S

I had an erratic light like this on my 951 for a while.  Turned out to 
be an intermittent front lens.  The gasket doesn't fit correctly, and 
moisture gets in easily.  It was quite a feat to get a darned little 
light to work...

When the main blinker bulb doesn't work, the running/parking light bulb 
lights in its place and the blinker acts a little funny.

Things to check are: light sockets & correct filament lighting, blinker 
relay, ignition switch.

Good luck,

		JBS

Porschephiles #570                         Tue Nov 16 02:11:19 CST 1993
Date: Mon, 15 Nov 93 08:54:25 GMT
From: malc@ncc.co.uk (Malcolm Smith)
Subject: Re: Metal finishing


...> 
...> There was a number of articles in different magazines (UK) that interviewed
...> a wheel re-builder, the only one in the UK the re-anodises the two tone anodised
...> finish for Carrera RS wheels for example. He said 1) Porsche wheels are the
...> absolute "dogs bollocks", the best wheel *anybody* makes and 2) BBS are useless 
...> - they actually have to be sent back to BBS in germant to be re-built properly 
...> (obviously the split-rim type) because of the sealing between sections etc.
...> 



...> Folks,
...> 
...> If anyone on the list has had experience with restoring alloy wheels'
...> appearance, or with Eastwood's metal cutting and polishing products,
...> please drop me a line.  I've been lent a set of basket-case forged alloy
...> rims which I'm cleaning up.
...> 
...> (Yes, I know about Rob't Wood.  He won't come to my house.)
...> 
...> Kevin
...> 


The answer's above. Basically the guy based in the London (UK) area refinshes wheels. 
He actually does work for Porsche G.B. The setup is basically this.

1. He removes the old protective finishes e.g laquer in a chemical bath.

2. He sets the wheel on a CNC lathe and takes a few microns of metal off
    ( and all of the corrosion with it).

3. He repaints as per original or customer instructions.

The net result is basically amazing. I would say the wheels
are actually BETTER than the originals (Having seen 2 931s at
the UK Concours  one with orignal "pampered, only used at concours"
wheels and second with knackered and then re-finished wheels
the re-finished had a noticably better polish to them).

He charges about 40-50 GBP per wheel (rim only, more if you want tyres
taking off and putting back on).

There must be somewhere in the states that does this.

 -malc-

Date: Mon, 15 Nov 93 08:47:49 EST
From: bart@research.att.com

>But it seems to me that your car might well have a top speed of 350mph.
>On the other hand, at that speed, gas mileage is probably in the range of 1-2mpg
>With a tank capacity of 20 gallons, that gives you a range of 30 miles, or a
>time between refueling stops of 5 minutes. Am I approximately right with these
>numbers?

slightly off.  power required goes as velocity cubed.  mileage goes down as
velocity squared.

assume you get 30 mpg at 60 mph in your 928gts, and that it uses 300 hp to go
170 mph.

top speed is now 170*(1000/300)^(1/3) -> 254.

mileage is 30*(60/254)^2 -> 1.7 mpg.

on the other hand, at the more practical speed of 120 mph (remember the highways
of the future we kept hearing about in the 60's?), mileage would be a cool 7.5 mpg,
or 1.25 hours worth.

the obvious optimization problem is left as an exercise to the reader..

Date:     Mon, 15 Nov 93 8:51:36 EST
From: "Hugh A. Huntzinger" (CCAC-LAD) <huntzing@PICA.ARMY.MIL>
Subject:  Re: Radar detectors


Gary Hoffman:
> Well .. it's time for a new one.  I promised someone my Bel 635.
> Any experience with the Valentine One?  Interesting concept in a

Eric Typpo:

|>The Valentine One is wonderful.  Fantastic range and the directional
|>arrows are very useful.  Current V1s do detect the latest wideband, and
|>according to my conversation with them about a month ago, owners of older
|>V1s will be getting a letter offering an upgrade for $30.  You do
|>have to use a power cord however, and I believe that there is still a
|>backlog, although not as bad as when it first came out.  Overall, an
|>excellent product.

Ditto.  I've had mine for ~6 months now (backorder was 5 months), and am
waiting for my wideband upgrade letter...the offer to upgrade previous
owners for little more than shipping costs is a sign of good customer-focus.

In addition to the directional arrows, it counts "engagements" (up to 9),
which is needed, given its sensitivity.  I get a false positive rate of
80%+ in "All Bogies" mode, but I'm in NJ...home to tons of X-band security
systems and cars using radar detetors to keep their Insurance rates down.  
Detection of other radar detectors shows up as either X or K, sometimes both.

On the power cord, I have the optional remote display, which can be
mounted anywhere between the unit and its power.  I intended to mount it
near the speedo, but the "stock" configuration (tip of the cigarette
lighter power plug) works well and serves the primary purpose of getting
the display off the detector where "someone following you" can see it.
Custom cabling is made easy - it uses standard telephone wire & jacks.

On the down side, a friend who had one last winter says that the suction
cups don't hold onto his windshield as well as his Escort did when it's
"cold" (He's in the Midwest: "cold" = <10F??).  The price shouldn't be too
much of a downer for this group - roughly $300, plus $50 for the remote kit.

Date: Mon, 15 Nov 1993 09:05:26 -0600 (CST)
From: David Harris Stewart <stew@owlnet.rice.edu>
Subject: Re: 928GTS project

Chris

>I don't know enough about the level of performance you're talking about.
>But it seems to me that your car might well have a top speed of 350mph.

The goal is to reach 300 mph on the short course at Bonneville.  We should
have an extra 175 HP leaft over at that speed to burn in downforce and 
acceleration.

>On the other hand, at that speed, gas mileage is probably in the range of 
>1-2mi. With a tank capacity of 20 gallons, that gives you a range of 30 miles

We have two fuel cells each holding over 16 gallons.  The economy at top 
speed should be over 2.4 mi/g thanks to the low Cd.

>In a more serious vein, with all your computer power in the design of this car,
>have you figured out at what speed the car is going to be most fuel efficient?

Maximum efficiency is achieved at a cruising speed of 75 mph.  For fear of
being laughed off Porschephiles, I won't print our estimates.  But the 
low Cd again helps in that only 60% as much HP will be needed to propel our
prototype to this speed as compared to the 928.  This figure also accounts for
the friction in the full time 4WD system.

Now wouldn't it be nice to say "but, officer, that WAS the responsible speed
to be driving at!"

Seriously, if Detroit ever gets its act together and produces low drag, low
consumption autos, the average maximumally efficient cruising speed will rise
from 50 to maybe 70 mph.  Sounds like a great reason to raise speed limits 
to me.

David Stewart
stew@owlnet.rice.edu

Date: Mon, 15 Nov 1993 09:25:31 -0600
From: smaardyk@rs6000.houston.wireline.SLB.COM
Subject: 944 belt info solicited


Porschephiles,

I have been searching the archives and reading recent posts concerning the
cam and balance shaft belts on 944's.  There have been a couple of tantalizing
bits if information which lead me to the following questions concerning the
issue of 'replacing your own:'

1.  '87 and later models have a built-in tensioning device for the cam belt
which makes it possible to replace this belt without the Porsche tensioning
gage.  This should have two additional benefits.  First, since the tensioning
device should make up for at least some of the thermal expansion of the engine
block shoudn't the belt last longer?  And second, it seems that it should be
unnecessary to retension the belt following a break-in period (T or F?).

2.  The balance shaft belt still requires some method for achieving the
correct tension.  However, it looks like the geometry of this belt should
make for a reduced effect from the block's thermal expansion.  Also, the
function of this belt is less critical than the cam belt (barring a failure
which could induce a subsequent problem with the cam belt).  Therefore, it
seems to me that it ought to be possible to use some 'eye-balling' method
to adjust the balance shaft belt.  In fact, some of the posts have
suggested such a possibility.

I am particularly interested in the second question and would appreciate
any comments, suggestions, and (particularly) techniques from anyone out
there with appropriate knowledge.

Many thanks..

John		smaardyk@rs6000.houston.wireline.slb.com

Date: Mon, 15 Nov 93 11:30:34 EST
From: lmcanro@LMC.ericsson.se (Andre Rotchel)
Subject: Re: Turnsignal problem on 87 944S


Hi, 

I've had the same problem.

The answer: Change the turn-signal RELAY (about 12$Cdn).

The explanation: The soldering cracks after about 5 years.
                 -When it gets cold the cracks expand as the 
                 soldered material shrinks.
                 -When it gets hot the cracks close up as the 
                 soldered material expands.

Warning: This problem is an early indicator of
         similar problems with other relays
         in that beautiful box of "Bosch" components...
         But that's another story!

Regards, Andre Rotchel
Ericsson Communications,
Montreal, Canada.

Date: Mon, 15 Nov 93 13:19:51 EST
From: fwk@hogpa.ho.att.com
Subject: Re: 944 belt info

>1.  '87 and later models have a built-in tensioning device for the cam belt
>which makes it possible to replace this belt without the Porsche tensioning
>gage.  This should have two additional benefits.  First, since the tensioning
>device should make up for at least some of the thermal expansion of the engine
>block shoudn't the belt last longer?  And second, it seems that it should be
>unnecessary to retension the belt following a break-in period (T or F?).

False. The built-in tensioner replaces the special tool in the tensioning
process, but the tensioning process is still manual, not automatic.
A pair of nuts are loosened, and a spring which is internal to the
tensioner then establishes the proper tension, and the nuts are retightened.
This will not alter the effect of thermal expansion, nor does it
eliminate the need for periodic retensioning, including the one after
the break-in period.

>2.  The balance shaft belt still requires some method for achieving the
>correct tension.  However, it looks like the geometry of this belt should
>make for a reduced effect from the block's thermal expansion.  Also, the
>function of this belt is less critical than the cam belt (barring a failure
>which could induce a subsequent problem with the cam belt).  Therefore, it
>seems to me that it ought to be possible to use some 'eye-balling' method
>to adjust the balance shaft belt.  In fact, some of the posts have
>suggested such a possibility.

In concept this is true. The balance belt is less highly stressed, and 
the consequences of breakage are less, unless the broken balance belt
gets caught in the cam belt and causes it to break. However, you are
placing a rather large wager on the table (several $K), based on a
rather limited set of data. The fact that one or a few balance belts
have failed without taking the cam belt along for the ride isn't much
to go on, in my opinion. I would suggest that you let the cam belt
adjust itself, and perhaps use a heuristic method for the balance belt
to cover a few hundred miles until you can get the balance belt
adjusted with the tool (borrowed, done by dealer, etc.), but don't
bet the farm on a heuristic method alone.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@hogpa.att.com

Date: Mon, 15 Nov 93 13:00:51 PST
From: John Wright <jpw@hpsadlb.sr.hp.com>
Subject: Re galvanizing

> In this part of the world  the galvanized bodies of newer 
>Porsches is an important consideration.  Were the '73 911S models
> galvanized or did that come a little later?  Opinions would be 
> appreciated.
> 
> Jim Fry	'82 931, black
> 
> 

All Porsche cars were galvanized starting with 1976 model year.

For the 73S, the answer is it may be galvanized.

My 1972 911T Targa was completely galvanized.  I know because I personally
stripped the car down to bare metal.  Doors, doorposts, lockposts,  fenders, 
rear quarter sections, rocker panels and front hood, were galvanized.  

Even the gas cap lid and the headlight buckets were galvanized.  

The rear lid had been replaced with a factory non-galvanized version,
so I don't know if the car shipped with a galvanized one.

From various sheet metal purchases, I acquired a normal 72 front bumper that
was galvanized but a front "S" spoiler that was not galvanized.
Again verified by stripping to bare metal.

I understand there was a limit on the quantity of Thyssen (sp?) galvanized 
steel that Porsche could buy.  So galvanized steel was selectively used until
1976 when Porsche could get enough for all cars.

For example, the 911 floor pan should be galvanized on all non Karmann 911's 
after mid - 1970.

Here's some speculation, based on my very limited data.

  The best candidate for a galvanized car pre-1976 would be a 
  Targa assembled in the Porsche factory.

  Targas, because any structural rust weakens an initially weaker body
  than the coupe, and I know that galvanized Targa rear quarter panels existed
  in 1972.

  "Porsche assembled" bodies because the Porsche factory would not
  burden the Karmann factory with any production problems that resulted from 
  the gradual changeover to galvanized metal.

  The Porsche/Karmann origin can be determined by the paint tag on the
  door hinge post or the VIN number.

Date: Mon, 15 Nov 93 16:53:23 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: '74 - '77 911's

>After reading a number of old reviews on the 2.7l 911S over the weekend, I'm
>wondering if purchasing one wouldn't be such a bad idea.  Several key benefits
>were discussed in the reviews...

Every Porsche engine was a significant improvement over it's predecessor --
without exception. So comparing a 2.7 to a 2.4 (particularly around
the time the 2.7 was introduced) would only lead to glowing reviews.
*BUT* with the 2.7, there were some significant problems as well.

>I have read several discussions on this net regarding the low life expectancy
>of the 2.7l engine (rebuild at 75k?), I am wondering how valid this figure is.

Actually, I'd say it may be optimistic. *BELIEVE IT!!!* We're not
making this stuff up. Several of us (myself included) have even
been bit by this sucker.

>Also, I've heard about the problems with overheating and the cylinder head 
>studs pulling out.  How common were these problems on the 2.7l engine.

That's *WHY* you get the rebuild at 50-70K. It's not that the nuts fall
off the con rods, or the main crank journals get square -- it's that
2.7 was more engine that you can reliably put on that wonderful
old magnesium case. It was overkill for a 2.0 or 2.2; it was still
measurably over-engineered for a 2.4. But just NOT up to the task for
the 2.7. 

Oh, for a race engine, it's still great -- but you get to rebuild them all 
the time anyway. But for the street, don't plan on getting more than 70K
miles. In fact, if you haven't sold it by 50K miles, start counting your pennies 
for that rebuild... You may need it any day.

Once it's rebuilt, you can relax for a good while. If you use a full set of 
Dilavar studs, new pistons and cylinders, and do everything else "right"
you'll probably get 100k miles out of it. Maybe more.

But not on a stock 2.7. And a rebuilt 2.7? Yeah, right. Who did the rebuild.
When. Using what for parts. If you didn't do it, and don't intimately
know who did, don't trust it. It's just another core engine waiting to 
be rebuilt...

Stan

Date: Mon, 15 Nov 1993 16:37:54 -0800 (PST)
From: LAFORCE@XENON.ARC.NASA.GOV
Subject: re: 2.7 liter query

Steve writes:



>I have read several discussions on this net regarding the low life expectancy
>of the 2.7l engine (rebuild at 75k?), I am wondering how valid this figure is.

From **everything** I've read or heard, it's pretty accurate.


>Also, I've heard about the problems with overheating and the cylinder head 
>studs pulling out.  How common were these problems on the 2.7l engine.  The
>reviews I read said the max temp recorded was 210defF and that was after
>a 1 hour run at over 100mph.

Did the review mention the outside air temp?  It makes a big difference
on my engine temp at an auto-x.  Also, a new engine will run cooler,
less dirt to impead heat transfer.

The overheating may be reduced by going to an 11 (instead of 5(?)) blade
fan and removing the thermal reactors ('75-'77).

From what I've read/heard, the best of the lot is the '74 (no smog
pump, thermal reactors, etc.).  Unfortunately, it's the best of a 
bad lot.

Before buying a 911, read Anderson's book: "The 911 Performance Handbook."

It's the best $20 you will ever spend on a 911.

I think you're much better off spending another $5k and getting an 'sc.

I have heard that the 2.7 is a nice engine to drive, when they are running.

------------------------------

Date: Mon, 15 Nov 93 23:28 EST
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Turnsignal problem on 87 944S


Andre suggests:

>The explanation: The soldering cracks after about 5 years.
>                 -When it gets cold the cracks expand as the 
>                 soldered material shrinks.
>                 -When it gets hot the cracks close up as the 
>                 soldered material expands.

Good call, could indeed be the culprit.  If so, relays and switches are
often repairable.  If it's a cracked solder joint, reflowing the solder
may be all that's required.  Pull the cover off the relay and hunt.

I had a similar problem last winter with my rear window defroster switch.
A real pain to get out, and then to add insult to injury, the replacement
cost was in the $30 range.  So I opened it up, got a little clever with
the soldering iron (as much as that might be possible) and saved myself
some money.

With respect to the original problem, another thing I would check is
battery voltage.  Make sure you have no less than 12.6 volts when running
the car.  You'll obviously need a digital meter for same, but the accuracy
can be important.

Kevin

Date: Tue, 16 Nov 1993 00:20:47 -0400 (EDT)
From: DELTAONE@delphi.com
Subject: Performance upgrade - '87 Carrera

I have a 1987 Carrera cab (fact. turbo option). The motor has 44k miles
but is strong as confirmed by recent leak-down and compression test.
I would like to get more performance at a reasonable price. Since the
car is not used on the track (I would need  roll bar installed for PCA
events) it would be great to get some added power in the low-end of
the rpm range.
   My local shop, Exclusive Motorcars in Montcair,NJ, showed me a product
by AutoThority which seems very good. It is an intake and chip replacement
which developes significant torque improvements in the 2200-4200rpm range.
I'll skip the full spreadsheet of test results (which I frankly don't
understand anyway) and summarize below:
            
           @2300 rpm: torque 154.9lbs(w/o mod)-173.7lbs(with)
           
                      hp 69.9 (w/o mod) - 76.1(with)

           @3300 rpm: torque 169.8lbs(w/o mod)-189.5lbs(with)

                      hp 106.7 (w/o mod) - 119.1 (with)

           @4300 rpm: torque 189.7lbs(w/o mod)-197.2 (with)

                      hp 155.3(w/o mod)- 161.5 (with)

PEAK PERF  @6100 rpm: torgue 186.8lbs(w/o mod) - 202.3 (with)

                      hp 217.o (w/o mod)- 235.0 (with)

The motor which was used was a 1987 carrera (mileage unknown). All results
conform to Standard Corrected Data for 29.92 inches Hg. 60 degrees F 
dry air.
   The cost for all of this was around $2000 installed. Exclusive is very
confident about the results. They were very negative on chip replacements
before this particular set-up.
   I would really like to hear what you think of the above. I'm new to
Porschepiles and am not sure if this is an old topic so please bear
with me. Thanks. 
Don Tydeman (deltaone@delphi.com)

Stoddards has an update oil plate for around $50 (at least it was 3 years ago
when I ordered one due to stripped threads).  You'll need a new magnetic
drain plug, too.  The update comes with a new sump screen with a slightyly
different scavanging arrangement.  Fit fine on my '73.

Subject: Re: Turnsignal problem on 87 944S

Hi, 

I've had the same problem.

The answer: Change the turn-signal RELAY (about 12$Cdn).

The explanation: The soldering cracks after about 5 years.
                 -When it gets cold the cracks expand as the 
                 soldered material shrinks.
                 -When it gets hot the cracks close up as the 
                 soldered material expands.

Warning: This problem is an early indicator of
         similar problems with other relays
         in that beautiful box of "Bosch" components...
         But that's another story!

>>>>  Any hints on how to extract those #!*#! relays...obviously not a task
    designed for normal sized fingers?

    Steve Carlson  '84 944  Chicago Region PCA

The rear right factory sway bar mount on my 1986 911 has sufford metal fatigue.

attached. I was told by my local Porsche dealer that this is a common problem.
Since I need to get this repaired, I was considering using the Weltmeister mount
kit that is made for cars that did not have a factory sway bar. This kit mounts
to the torsion bar housing. The alternative is to weld an OEM sway bar mount
on the floor pan (as it came from the factory).I would like to prevent this
problem in the future. The mount from Porsche is $60 plus welding cost. The
Weltmeister kit is about $75.

Would it be better to stay with the factory design?
Would it be better to use the Weltmeister kit?

I would appreciate comments from others that have delt with this problem.

Date: Tue, 16 Nov 93 13:21:18 EST
From: Paul Moruzzi <pmoruzzi@hpwarhw.wal.hp.com>
Subject: Re: small dent removal


> There are a couple of "franchise" dent removal places
> that have cropped up in our area this past year and one
> is advertising in our NNJR-PCA newsletter - something
> like "Mr. Dent"
> 
> They promise painless and inexpensive dent removal
> (parking lot variety) without affecting the paint 
> on your car. They say that they can do most jobs while
> you wait!! "Painless" and "inexpensive" haven't
> been part of my Porsche vocabulary, so I'm curious
> to hear if anyone has any experience with these guys.
> 
> Is it really possible to remove small dents without 
> affecting paint? It sounds too good to be true.

Yes its really possible.  However I do not know about these
franchise operations.  I have had several dings removed
painlessly this way.  The person who did the work was German. He
had worked in the BMW factory for years (so I was told), his job
there was to remove dings that happened in production without
the need to repaint.  I watched him work, there was no magic
involved, he'd just get behind the ding with a metal bar (he'd
bend up a bar to get in inaccessible areas) and slowly work it
out, he also tapped at the painted side with a HAMMER while he
was working the ding.  It was pretty amazing too watch, you
absolutly  can't tell where the ding was, and my car is black
(seemed like anyone could do it if you took the time to practice
a lot). This guy lived in Minnisota I think, and a couple of the
local dealerships would get him out here a couple times a year
(Boston area).  If you need it I can try to dig up the info at
home. Cost was $70 - $120 pretty cheap in my book.

Hope this helps
Paul Moruzzi
pmoruzzi@wal.hp.com

Date: 16 Nov 93 17:43:20 PST
From: "WJOB" <wjob@chevron.com>
Subject: 911 2.7ltr


 From: Wilkinson, Joe (WJOB)
 To:  OPEN ADDRESSING SERVI-OPENADDR
 Subject:  911 2.7ltr
 Date: 1993-11-16 17:27
 Priority:

 ------------------------------------------------------------------------------


 I think 2.7ltr engines are being given a bad rap..again. Maybe I am just
 lucky, but I have had very good luck with 2 different 2.7 ltr engines. My
 first was a 77 model and I sold it running strong with 110k miles. I
 currently own a 74 Carrera with over 120k miles and it is still has good
 compression and leak down. Neither engine was ever rebuilt, however both
 were routinely maintained throughout their life.

 Before you start hitting me with reply notes let me say I don't disagree
 with any of the technical things heard about the 2.7ltr engines, mag case
 problems, pulled studs etc. All engines have their quirks and the 2.7 is no
 exception. I think it is more a matter of numbers. Porsche made more 2.7's
 than any previous generation 911 engines and it is not surprising mechanics
 began to see them more often, there simply were more around. (another
 example of this phenomena has occurred with the 944). Had Porsche perceived
 the 2.7 as weaklings wouldn't they have made the switch to the 3.0 sooner.
  Remember the 3.0 case was available the entire time the 2.7 was in
 production. One could argue that Porsche produced the 2.7 for 4 years, twice
 as long as the production of either the 2.2 or the 2.4, which was an
 indication Porsche actually favored the engine.

 Let me conclude by saying the 2.7 is a great motor, worthy of the Porsche
 name. Certainly comparisons to other manufacturers and its racing success
 prove this point. I agree it may not be as capable as the king of longevity,
 the SC's 3.0, but my experience has proven they are good for over 100k. They
 simply are not the 50k mile time bombs some people like to describe them as.


 Joe Wilkinson

Date: Tue, 16 Nov 93 14:35:59 EST
From: tommywmson@aol.com
Subject: RE:Porsche Odor

Kyle,
Last week you wrote complaining about the smell and mentioned
a strong fuel odor.

Have you check that the fuel tank breather hoses and charcoal filter
canaster are hooked up properly.  This can really stink up the inside
of the car if the fuel tank air is not routed back to engine or vented to
the outside of the car.  

A good way to check for leaks is to use a match or lighter.

Subject:  NLA part needed.

Hey gang, before i engage the distant salvage yards or pano part'ers
i'm asking here first.  My '73 914-4 needs this part: 111.953.535D
it is a 'contact ring' (made out of unobtainium) found on the upper
steering shaft.  It appears on fiche plate 4/3/2 pg. 2 item 23.
Uh, fiche # WET 613 420.  Just got off the phone with Stoddard; NLA!
With exception to the 1.8L 914, all 914-4 and 6 use this part and the
bearing ring that fits over it.

This bearing will cause a lot of noise in the steering when turning the
wheel (a kind of shiiiish sound)[no pun intended] and both 914's i have
suffer(ed) this.  I suspect other 914's do this too because the bearing
is of poor quality  marked with a star.  The lower bearing is
a fafnir of good quality and durability. IMHO!

I am willing to purchase the entire steering head including wheel and
centerpiece if the price is fair.  Just the part i need would be ok
but i also need the steering wheel centerpiece too, anyone know if
these are interchangeable across years?

I also am in need of a couple engine tin pieces for a 1976 914 2.0.
These are peculiar to the '75 and '76 years, but I might rather be
wanting a complete HX for '73 or so...its so tempting to stray from OEM
for that year (76).

Date: Wed, 17 Nov 93 13:47:10 GMT

Subject: If they do this with a Range Rover what would it be like with a 911?



I guess this is Blues Brothers UK style

> I don't know if anyone read a story in their Saturday morning tabloid about a
> 3 hour chase around High Wycombe and surrounding towns which resulted 9
> [NINE!] police cars being totalled by three joyriders in a Range Rover. What
> was printed in the papers was pretty astonishing (for many reasons); however,
> it's not quite the full and unexpurgated story. I live in Wycombe, and about
> 0200 on Friday morning I was woken up by a jellywopper hovering low over my
> house and waving a searchlight about in my general direction; obviously the
> police, and it worried me enough that I went and dug out my old FM airband
> radio to see if I might discover what kind of gun toting psycho they were
> looking for in close proximity to my front garden. As a result, I listened
> into the whole incident in it's un-glossed form; the police don't come out of
> it smelling of roses.
> 
> The lunatic driving the Range Rover was obviously an excellent driver; bloody
> good job, too, because if he hadn't been he would surely have been through
> the side of someones house the speeds he was being chased at. As it was, the
> only thing he hit all night were police vehicles, all of them deliberately.
> After three hours every high-speed traffic jam sandwich for miles around,
> including the local armed response vehicle, was a pile of twisted metal. The
> police were reduced to following in their bottom-of-range Astra panda cars,
> or more accurately hiding from the Range Rover which had gone from pursued to
> pursuer; I heard one car calling in an asking if it was safe for him to drive
> up the A40. As soon as the helicopter ran out of juice, they lost the Range
> Rover... the Metropolitan plod come out of this really well (not); they
> wouldn't lend Thames Valley another helicopter to continue the pursuit. At no
> time during those three hours did they apparently think of calling up Range
> Rovers, or Landy Discoveries from adjacent areas to ram or box in the Range
> Rover; instead they just aimlessly chased it around the area at breakneck
> speed. If they couldn't catch it, why on earth were they chasing it and
> risking their, the thieves and many other peoples lives in the process?
> 
> Anyway, the newspapers DIDN'T mention that not all of the police vehicles
> were trashed by hitting the Range Rover; at least two of them hit each other
> in severely embarrassing circumstances, and a third very expensive traffic
> car, provided like the others by the polltax payers of this fair county,
> disappeared down a bunker on the 18th fairway at Flackwell Heath Golf Course
> while pursuing the Range Rover thereupon. What really concerns me, though, is
> the police justification for the whole incident. The car sped through a speed
> trap in Wycombe; fair enough, I wasn't listening to the chase then. The
> police also allege, in the paper, that 'The three occupants of the car could
> be seen taking drugs while they were driving along'. I presume that this is
> designed to lend weight to the police argument that apprehending these people
> was worth the risk to life and limb, not to mention the entire new vehicle
> budget for Bucks this year and next. But all I can say to that is 'B*ll*cks'!
> The police couldn't even see their faces well enough to describe them, let
> alone 'see them taking drugs'. Unless they were shooting up with their arms
> out the window, I don't know how they could have. A Range Rover is four feet
> above a cop car anyway, so they'd have a job seeing anything going on
> outside, and the way this guy was driving, it was very unlikely he was on
> anything except, just conceivably, speed. Besides, I was listening to the
> whole thing and I didn't hear drugs mentioned once in all of the commentary
> or discussion from the police in the two hours I was listening. My
> conclusion: They're making that bit up. Inspires confidence doesn't it?
> 
> You couldn't have made a film about this; this is High Wycombe, not Hazzard
> County. What exactly do we pay our police for? Is it enough that they risk
> their lives, as they clearly were here, or do we require that they do it
> competently, and to a purpose, and cost effectively, without risking our
> lives as well? Or perhaps it is that they behave like the Keystone Kops, as
> they did on Friday morning... And what, dear reader, would have happened if

>

> Range Rover is going to be a real hero among his friends. After all, Smokey
> and the Bandit has nothing on him... even I can't help but have a sneaking
> admiration for the little toe rag, so his peer group will have no trouble. We
> will now be invaded by hoardes of his pals, probably less able than him, all
> keen to be a hero like him. People will be injured, or even die as a result
> of all this. No doubt the police will then bleat about how they need more

Date: Wed, 17 Nov 93 10:43:10 EST
From: Jeffrey A. Shorey <js@ee.duke.edu>
Subject: Re: Broken rear factory sway bar mount on a 911

W.P. writes:
>The rear right factory sway bar mount on my 1986 911 has suffered metal
>fatigue.

Same thing on my car - evidently it's gone through 2 sway bars this way.
I can't say which fix is better - but a year or so ago in European Car
there was an article about 911 suspensions that used a reinforced replacement
rear sway bar mount bracket.  These sold for $100 a pair.  I can go look up
the place that sells them for you if you want.

The primary cause of fatigue in these things is that people use them as
jack points to jack up the car...I always use the lower bolt on the torsion
bar mounting fixture.  Is there any net wisdom on the correct jack points on
an older 911?

I have a 1986 944 Turbo with "Phone Dials", 7*16 front and 8*16 rear.Through
investigation I have come to the conclusion that I have 87' "Phone Dials". The
wheels are set deeper in the wheel well, because of the offset. The offset was
changed on the 87' year to accomodate ABS.So different phone dials were
manufactured because of this ABS update. I purchased the car used 2 months ago,
so whoever had the car before me changed them, or maybe the dealer did at time
of sale. What was standard equipment on this car? I ask this question because I
get different answers,some people say "phone dials" (with the right offset,not
the ones I have).Tom hiser at Porsche Cars Of North America, customer service
department in Reno states that 1986 944 Turbo's came across the assembly line
with 911 type forged wheels as standard equipment,a 951 prefix part # (Sorry ,I
don't know the proper name of these wheels).He also states that the wheels that
are on the car now (87 "Phone Dials") will not work properly on the car because
of the negative scrub radius, and it changes the wheelbase specs. What does this
term mean,and is it a problem using these wheels? Other people say that the
correct phone dials were standard .Did it change during the production year? Can
anyone help me on this? I have option code # 395, which is a forged wheels
option.(I guess the car once had the proper wheels).This is confusing.I have
heard that even "standard" equipment is listed on the option code list due to
assembly procedures and point of destination (different countries).Any help
would be great.

Date: 16 Nov 1993 11:27:12 U
From: "Lance Keigwin" <lance_keigwin@engtwomac.Synoptics.COM>
Subject: Hood Shocks

                       Subject:                               Time:11:00 AM
  OFFICE MEMO          Hood Shocks                            Date:11/16/93
It took 45 minutes, but I finally managed to replace the driver's
side hood shock on my 88 911, clip and all!  If you've never had
this experience you're missing one of the highlights of Porsche
maintenance.  Human fingers are almost entirely useless.
(Thankfully I installed the heavy duty shock Automotion sells
and it should hold up for a while.)

I am now faced with doing the same on my wife's 89.  Aarrgghh!
Anyone found an easier way of doing this, some special tool,
etc.?  I've used the rachet-like mechanical "shock" that is often
advertised in Excellence in place of both hydraulic shocks and
was actually pretty happy with it.  The only problem was that
others who didn't know how it operated tended to force it and
it finally broke.  (You have to listen for the rachet "clicks" as
you raise and lower the hood and stop at the right moment.)

I really like the hydraulic shocks but *hate* having to change
the front left.  Ideas anyone?

Lance. (lkeigwin@synoptics.com)
88 factory turbo body targa, diamond blue (just try to find blue in it)
89 coupe, metallic black (25th anniv.)
85 535i, that medium grey everyone has

Date: 16 Nov 1993 14:23:10 U
From: "Lance Keigwin" <lance_keigwin@engtwomac.Synoptics.COM>

        Reply to:   RE>BBS WHEEL UPDATE
Date: 11/16/93 11:39 AM
From: sidak_ok@mike.lrc.edu
WELL MY FRIEND CALLED BBS AND TOLD THEM WHAT HAPPENED. THEY DENIED
ANY WRONG DOING ON THEIR PART AND REFUSED TO HAVE ANYTHING TO DO
WITH HIM. HE CALLED HIS LAWYER AND NOW EVERY THING IS BEING PAID FOR
BY BBS. THEY ARE EVEN BUYING HIM ANY SET OF WHEELS HE LIKES. 

This is another (unfortunate) example of why it pays to be a pain in the
ass.  Several years ago I bought a low mileage 84 cabriolet from a local
Porsche dealer.  I later discovered a loud "clunking" noise in the rear
upon a sudden weight-shift (hard corner, tight turn up and into driveway,
etc.).  The dealer was reluctant to work on it, but because I had an extended
warranty they tried to find things they could repair and claim (and even
went so far as rebuilding the limited slip, as silly as it seemed to everyone
I talked to).  Eventually they gave up and told me to drive it and enjoy it! 
No one could pinpoint the problem.  I wrote letters to Bruce Anderson, sent
complaints to all the trade rags, and finally heard from PCNA who offered
financial help to convince the dealer to redouble efforts.  In all, it took
2 years to resolve.  A new trailing arm mysteriously fixed it (and no
one ever understood why).

Lance.

Date: Wed, 17 Nov 93 16:36:45 PST
From: tilden@bethe.Data-IO.COM (John Tilden)
Subject: Re: Hood Shocks ie Gas Springs


	I found that the left side hood spring on 911 to be a real 
	problem as well!
	I made a clip out of a large paper clip and taped it to a long
	screw driver. This held the pin at a right angle to the screwdriver
	against the flat, enableing me to insert the pin through the bracket
	and spring end with ease and retrieve the screwdriver/clip assembly 
	mereley by pulling it away from the headed pin!
 	Once the pin was in the proper position the fastening clip could 
	easily be snapped in place.
	Most of the tedious jobs can be dealt with if you take a few minutes 
	and fabricate a tool for the purpose. I bet the factory has a tool
	to do this job, as it has to be done repeatedly and there is no easy
	way to do it with out one.
	Other hints from other mechanics on their favorite labor saving tool
	appreciated!

John Tilden
tilden@data-io.com

Date:     Wed, 17 Nov 93 18:10:01 EST
From: "Hugh A. Huntzinger" (CCAC-LAD) <huntzing@PICA.ARMY.MIL>
Subject:  RE: Hood Shocks

Lance writes:
>I am now faced with doing the same on my wife's 89.  Aarrgghh!
>Anyone found an easier way of doing this, some special tool,
>etc.?  I've used the rachet-like mechanical "shock"...

Replaced my hood shocks in January...cold = double torture.  
Two suggestions, however, for replacing the retainer clip on the left side:

i) Tie a piece of thread to the clip.  It makes retrieval for WHEN you
drop it easier, and helps (a little) in manouvering it around.

ii) Ask your wife?  My SO has very small hands, which should make the job
easier, and it is HER car after all.  This is only a theory...I knew
better to ask mine (she's cold on 80F days in July...forget January!).

iii) How much does Porsche actually want to do the job?  Yeah, its a
rediculously "easy" job, but so, so frustrating.  Desn't hurt to ask...

Next time, I would plan to try designing a special tool.  Maybe some sort
of flat stock with a few zigs & zags, with a good pushing surface that
you glue (secure) the piece onto...might be able to cut one from
something like a putty knife?

Date: Wed, 17 Nov 1993 15:20:40 -0800
From: Mike Lee - Team Banana Racing <mikel@ichips.intel.com>
Subject: rebuilding an engine



I have some generic questions about engine rebuilding I hope you folks can
help with.  A friend and I will soon be rebuilding a VW engine which will be
put into a 356 while the original motor gets "unseized"

The VW for the transplant has been hot-rod'ed with a bigger bore (1835cc, I
think) and some head and cam work.  I'm not sure how it was treated by the
previous owner.  While the engine runs now (barely), we're re-building it
primarily to get some experience before tackling "the real thing"

My questions are:

1)  What components need to be replaced during a rebuild?  I know things
like rings, gaskets, and bearing should be replaced, as well as things that
don't measure to spec, but what about things like cranks and rods?  If the
tolerances on the crnak and rod measure okay, and there aren't any cracks
when xray'ed, does that mean they are okay?  How do you determine if it's
too fatigued, especially with a component that's been subjected to more
stress than designed for?

2)  Should valve guides be replaced?  How are they reamed after installation?

3)  How do you check a valve seat for wear?  The head is basically toast if 
they are worn, right?

4)  What sort of allowances do you need for the expansion of different
materials when assembling the engine?  For example, chro-mo case studs and
aluminum cylinder block?

5)  Any other gotchas we should think about?

Thanks,
Mike

Date: Wed, 17 Nov 93 21:38:57 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: 87' "phone Dial" wheel confusion

> I have a 1986 944 Turbo with "Phone Dials", 7*16 front and 8*16 rear.Through
> investigation I have come to the conclusion that I have 87' "Phone Dials".
[stuff deleted]

Michael,

The '86 951s *did* have positive offset wheels, which were not to 
appear on any further cars.  As of 1987 many (all?) Porsche models had 
negative offset wheels for ABS.

'86 phone dials are all cast aluminum colored; the forged wheel option 
had black-centered Fuchs in the more classic pattern.  Phone dials were 
standard.  In fact the wheels were shared with the 911 series.

The information you posted, which you obtained from several sources, is 
substantially correct.  If your car truly has negative offset wheels, 
then your track (not wheelbase) is wrong and the car's handling is 
compromised.  Also, if the option sticker shows forged wheels, then it 
also sounds like the previous owner sold them (they're worth $$$) and 
replaced them with *wrong* equipment.

Good luck on getting this resolved!


		JBS

Date: Wed, 17 Nov 93 21:46 EST
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: 911 Wheel Bearing Dust Covers

>What is the proper tool/method for the removal of the wheel bearing dust
>cover on a 911?  I've gotten them off before but let's just say I'm fairly
>certain the factory had a better way of removing them!

I assume this is a grease caps, which pushes into a bore in the end of
the spindle.  That being the case, the official factory method recommends
something like tire irons.  What I do is tap around the cap with a large
chisel, pushing it out.  Avoid scoring or otherwise marring the spindle's
bore.

Kevin



------------------------------

Date: Thu, 18 Nov 93 08:12:47 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: rebuilding an engine

First off, I'm only answering this because it's a VW engine going
into a 356. There *IS* a VW-specific mailing list, which I unfortunately
seem to have lost the address for... So: no more VW questions to
the list, OK?

Get a copy of two books: the HP Books volume called "How to Rebuild
Your Aircooled VW Engine" (which also covers 914s, BTW) and John
Muir's classic "Idiot Book", which is really called something like 
"How to Keep your VW Alive for the Complete Idiot". Also, if you
want to *really* do it right, get the Robert Bently service manual
for Type I. Your total investment at this point should be about $60,
which is really cheap compared to slagging your engine by doing 
this wrong. If you had been talking about any Porsche, I would have
said get the factory service manuals and the "spec book" for your 
particular model. Total investment would have varied from $200 for
a late model 356 to over $600 for a late '70s 911 Turbo. Again,
cheap relative to the cost of the engine...

Count on replacing *all* of the "consumables" -- gaskets, seals, 
bearings, etc. For *EVERYTHING* else, there is listed in the
factory specs a wear tolerance. If anything else is worn beyond
tolerance, reapir or replace it.

I routinely send cranks and rods out to be magnafluxed, and if good,
checked for tolerance then re-done if necessary (including balancing).
It's just a good habit. On heads, you *will* need a valve job. You
may need valve guides. I always replace 356 guides, and measure 9xx
heads to see if it's needed.

Clean everything *SUPERFINE*. I use about 5 gallons of Varsol and
a case and a half of spray carb cleaner (which may be why the Tree
Nazis don't drive Porsches...) on a 914 or 356 engine, much more on
a 911/9444. Anything you miss when cleaning may kill your engine
later on. This includes the *outside* of the engine, too.

Other than that, it really helps to have the right tools. Muir claims
(and he's right) that you can rebuild a VW engine with two cresent
wrenches, a pair of screwdrivers, and an empty beer can.  You just
can't do a job that lasts very long or runs very well.o

Good luck! BTW, I routinely recommend that people contemplating
a do-it-yourself Porsche engine rebuild do a VW motor first. It's
a cheap way to get the hang of it...

Stan

WRONG! 944 fuchs wheels were NOT shared with the 911 series. they have their own part numbers and the offsets are clos, but not identical. the 8" fuchs is 951-1362117 and had a 23.3.mm offset. the backspace, i.e. the distance from the inside edge o' the

Date: Thu, 18 Nov 93 08:48:42 EST
From: fwk@hogpa.ho.att.com
Subject: RE: 87' "phone Dial" wheel confusion

>Why don't you measure the offset ? 1986 had 10mm something, similar to the 911. 
>1987 and later have the 20mm something offset, and are incompatible with 911s.
>I hope I got this right..

I believe that the correct numbers are 23.5 mm for the 1986 and earlier 
944 series, and 52.3 mm for the later ones. I believe that some of the
Turbo S cars may have used something in the low 60 mm range like some
928's, but I'm not sure. 52.3 mm is definitely the correct offset for
1987 944's.

In any case, you don't need to measure the offset. It is embossed on
the inside of each wheel, along with the size(i.e. 7x15x52.3)

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@hogpa.att.com

Date: Thu, 18 Nov 1993 10:45:00 -0500
From: Robert B Mohan <rmohan@afit.af.mil>
Subject: RE: 928 GTS

RE:  Humidity and its effect on engine performance:

I dug up some old textbooks and found this:

Engineering Thermodynamics
by William C. Reynolds and Henry C. Perkins
McGraw Hill, 1977

They discuss humidity and its effect on air in detail,
including useful tables and equations.  I ran some
numbers as an example, under the conditions previously
discussed:

100% relative humidity
20 degrees C
1 atm pressure

And came up with this:

0.01469 kg water per 1 kg air

This relationship is linear, given constant press/temp, 
so at 50% humidity:

0.00734 kg water per 1 kg air

How this affects the partial mass/pressure of oxygen in the
air is: (@100% humidity)

mass of O2 = 0.9855 of dry air

Normally, this is insignificant, but in your case:

0.9855 x (1160 HP) = 1143 HP

You are giving up 17 HP and running rich for lack of
oxygen.  But, the good news is you have turbochargers
to pump up the boost, restore the partial pressure of
oxygen (absolute) and gain the added benefit of water
injection.  It sounds like your engine management system
could handle that variable.  The tables in the book I
mentioned provide enough info for you to construct
an array of partial pressures as a function of RH, temp,
and press.  A humidity sensor may be a trick, because
it typically involves a source of water.

Bob Mohan

rmohan@afit.af.mil

------------------------------

Date: Thu, 18 Nov 93 11:04:13 PST
From: dorr@hyperion.llnl.gov (Milo Dorr)
Subject: 87' "phone Dial" wheel confusion


As Jim Selders has said, I believe that the 5-star forged Fuchs were
an optional upgrade.  If you want to keep the phone dials, one option
you might consider is using spacers and longer studs to make up the
difference.  I don't recall the exact dimensions, but the difference
in offsets between the newer and older wheels is within a couple
millimeters of the width of a standard 930 wheel spacer (27mm).  On my
'86 951, I have these spacers on all four wheels with 72mm studs.  If
I had it to do over, I would try 80mm studs on the rear with an
additional thin spacer to increase the rear track.  Nevertheless, my
current setup looks and works fine.  I bought the spacers and studs
from Automotion.  These aren't cheap, but are cheaper than 4 new
wheels.  My goal in using the spacers was to allow me to use "Design
90" wheels (as used, e.g., on the 944 S2s), which, of course, have the
newer offset.

The installation of the spacers and studs does require a bit more
effort than the simple "bolt on" operation one might imagine.  The
spacers on the front (at least the ones I bought from Automotion)
required a slight bit of machining in order to clear the dust covers on
the front hubs.  I paid a machine shop $20 to do this on their lathe
to maintain "hub-centricity".  The installation of the studs on the
front hubs is straightforward, since they are easy to remove and take
to your vise, press, etc.  Unfortunately, the rear hubs are (as far as
I can tell) impossible to remove without destroying the one-piece
wheel bearing assembly.  You need to rig up something (using, e.g., C
clamps and a metal bar) to press out the old studs (don't use a
hammer).  In order to get the clearance you need to slip the new
longer studs into place, you will need to remove the parking brake
linkage from the trailing arm so that use can use the cavity it
normally occupies to get clearance for slipping the new longer studs
behind the hub.  The studs can then be pulled into the hub using a lug
nut (preferably a steel one rather than the softer alloys).

 -Milo

Date: Thu, 18 Nov 93 10:45:45 -0600
From: Kyle Ehler <kehler@daisy.wichitaks.NCR.COM>
Subject: Summary of bulletresistant systems for cars.

 Greetings Porschephiles.
 Here is a summary of articles I received from the various respondents
concerning bulletresistant system upgrades for cars.
 Please contact the individual who posted the response.

From: Jeff Deifik <jdeifik@isi.edu>
To: kehler@daisy.wichitaks.NCR.COM
Subject: spectra

spectra is made of ultra high molecular weight polyethylene.
it is very strong stuff, much stronger than fiberglass or kevlar.
I got two samples of the cloth. One is very finely woven stuff, 4 yards * 50
inches. I will use this for backpacks and clothing. The other is 30 yards * 50
inches. It is a similar weight (about 4 oz per square yard), but a
much coarser weave, making it unsuitable for clothing and the like.
Think of it as loosely woven fiberglass. I might use the coarse stuff
for building racing seats, or other fiberglass type uses.

You could use it also. I don't know if spectra shield has some kind of
resin like epoxy mixed in. I would guess that it does. The stuff costs
about $20 per yard. If you can't find a better source, I could sell
you some from my coarse roll.
- --

From: Matt Devries <devries@romulus.rutgers.edu>
To: kehler@daisy.wichitaks.NCR.COM
Subject: spectra shield

I dont know how you would go about buying straight yardage of spectra shield,
but it is some pretty high-tech stuff.  It is kind of like suped up kevlar
offering higher class protection.  It is used primarily in class IIIA, and
classIII vests intended to stop bullets from light assault weapons.  Kevlar
tops out at class II which is a light load .357 for max protection.  spectra
will pretty much stop any of the handgun rounds except for maybe hot-load .44
and some hard to come by .357 rounds, and of course the newer .50AE magnum
round. In a car it definitely will.  It'll stop lighter assault rifle rounds. 
.223 (m-16), and 7.62(ak-47), but the latter will probably only take a direct
hit or two before it becomes total trash.  Unfortunatly the stuff is extremely
expensive, a little rectangle for a trauma plate  (about 12"X15") costs a
couple hundred dollars.  so forget lining your doors with it.  Thats why an
armouring job costs around 50grand.
- --

From: THE KILLER + a 900RR <ehc29224@uxa.cso.uiuc.edu>
Subject: car jackings

I was just reading my Chicago Tribune and there is going to be this new
product on the market.  Basically, it is some type of thine film clear
adhesive coating that is put onto your windows.  When the window is hit by
a would be car jacker with a tire iron, basball bat, rock, etc...the
window does not shatter.  In fact, you'll get is this whole layer of
shattered glass that I wouldn't want to stick my finger through.  The product
is not designed to stand up forever to unlimited blows from whatever the
car jacker is swinging.
it is however designed to give you enough time to wake out of your stupor
and get the pedal to the metal.  For example, only after 8 blows from
a tire iron...did a small hold large enough to put one's hand through
appear.  
the article mentioned that for a chevy cavalier, the treatment of all side
window would cost approx $200.  Other cars would vary according to surface
areas,etc.  The company did not recommend coating the front windshiel
as supposedly most cars today have a front windshield already manufactured
not to break and scatter in a million pieces, but this would obviously
not apply to old vintage porsches.

if there is interest in this article...i can look up the company name and
post it and perhaps mail the article to those interested
eric
- --

<kyle sez:>
Eric has pointed out an unexpected idea.  I have seen this myself and perhaps
this is a small item that could be of use.  Many of you are using a 
sun control film on your windows...I have personal experience in
the effects of this film on how glass breaks.  An impact from a blunt
object will not penetrate the glass/film combination on first attempt.
Since the film is on the inside, you are given a small measure of protection
from flying glass and penetration because the film tends to hold the glass
together.  A few moments may be all one needs to make a getaway.

If there is now a commercial product available for this application, it
may be the cheapest and least invasive of all.
