Date: Thu, 18 Nov 93 14:58:57 EST
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: 915 box in '70 911


     Anyone out there know if a 915 box will bolt in to a 70 911
   body? or do you have to make mods to the car? If so, what's
   required? Just bending some sheet metal? or are the mounts
   different?

Tony,
The 915 will bolt into any '64-'86 911 chassis as far as the mounts are
concerned. But I've heard that since the rear upper shock mount point
is different on the pre-'72 cars there might be an interference
between the rear shocks and the halfshafts in this conversion. You
might want to explore this a little further before attempting the
swap. Let us know if you find out please.

Date: 18 Nov 93 15:32:04 PST

 From: Wilkinson, Joe (WJOB)
 To:  OPEN ADDRESSING SERVI-OPENADDR
 Subject:  911 E vs S
 Date: 1993-11-18 17:28
 Priority:

 ------------------------------------------------------------------------------


 Yes, there is an old '72 vintage Road and Track article comparing 2.4ltr T E
 and S and in this article the E was faster than the S up to about 100 mph.

 However, there also was a '74 vintage R&T article on the then new 2.7ltr and
 in that article they commented on how much smoother running the new CIS FI
 was compared to the MFI 911's they previously tested in '72 (true or false I
 don't know and this is not my point). They went on to mention that in their
 last test the S version was especially running poorly. I think this
 indicates something was wrong with the tune on the S and this is how the E
 was able to beat it to 100 mph.

 After all, an S has more HP and power, throughout the RPM range, than the E,
 weighs the same, and has the same gearing...How could an E be faster?

 A brief commentary - I have heard this article often quoted as sole evidence
 in several E vs S debates. I try not to believe everything I read, including
 car magazines. I would bet a lot of their writers are journalists, not car
 people, and they probably would just as soon be working at Glamour or Vogue.
 For example, aren't these the same guys who ranted and raved about he Dodge
 Stealth and Mitsubishi 3000...what a slug]]

 Joe Wilkinson

To the Nay-Sayers,

What's so hard to believe about 1200 HP from a turbocharged
350 V8.  Back in the days before MPG requirements, pop-off
valves, and methanol, indycars routinely made this kind of power
with half this displacement.  All you have to do is crank up the
boost.  Since Monterey, Hans will be the first to tell you that
a twin-turbo, intercooled Porsche engine can make 1200 HP.

I would like to tell you what some people at this institute are
doing, with only 18 more months of education than a Senior at Rice,
but then I would have to kill you.  Suffice to say, it would
put a 1200 HP, 300 mph earth-bound vehicle to shame.

Bob Mohan

Stan,

Date: Fri, 19 Nov 1993 08:42:40 EST
From: zeigler@merck.com
Subject: 0-60 times



Wade says:

>According to some previous posts, the 72-73 'S' was faster in top
>speed (and pulled away better in the 60-100 slot), but (for some
>reason) the 'E' had a better 0-60.  Go figure.

Although I have a '73 911E and would like for this to be true,
I don't think it agrees with Porsche's official figures. As I
recall, the E is a couple of 10ths of a second slower.

A couple of reasons an E might perform faster than an S 0-100
on any given day -- There's more area "under the cam" (keeps
valves open longer and gives the car more low-end power) That's
why S owners scarf up all of the E cams when they rebuild. --
The S ports in the heads and air horns are bigger (that helps
high end performance at a slight sacrifice to low end performance)
An extreme example of this is like putting too much carburettor on a 
car. You know what that's like - you end up with a car that will pass 
anything but a gas station, but gets beat by Yugo's at every stop 
light. 

My guess is that an S with E cams and modified cam timing would
clean the clock on any E -- but they'e going to be as scarce as 
speedsters in a few years. It's fairly easy, however, to rebuild
an E to S specs (keeping the better cams)


 - Reid

There is a myth abroad in the land that cats cannot be
trained.  This is false.  All it takes is the application

solution.

Date: Fri, 19 Nov 93 9:18:37 CST
From: tonye@weissach.eng.hou.compaq.com (Tony Eggers)
Subject: Will a 915 trans fit in 1970 911 body?

Netters,
  I asked the question if the 915 transmission would fit into a 1970
911 body. Here are the responses I got for the archives:

Stan wrote:
> 915 is larger than a 901. The tranny mount poinst are *slightly* different
> as a result. Last time I did this, I made Delrin mounts that mated with
> the body on one side and the 915 on the other.

ramans@microsoft.com wrote:
> I've got a 915 in my '71 so I guess it'll fit, as far as I know the only mod 
> that has to be made is a small dent with a hammer in the tunnel, to allow a 
> bolt head to rest there. I think that's it but this is hersey, I didn't put 
> the tranny int he car, it came with it.

John Colasante wrote:
> The 915 will bolt into any '64-'86 911 chassis as far as the mounts are
> concerned. But I've heard that since the rear upper shock mount point
> is different on the pre-'72 cars there might be an interference
> between the rear shocks and the halfshafts in this conversion. You
> might want to explore this a little further before attempting the
> swap. Let us know if you find out please.

I've gotten similar responses from others via phone for the "sheet metal wor
required", but not anything on the half shafts.

I've decided not to pursue this avenue.....

Tony

tonye@weissach.eng.hou.compaq.com

Date: Fri, 19 Nov 1993 11:51:36 -0600
From: smaardyk@rs6000.houston.wireline.SLB.COM
Subject: Msg. for Will Hartje


My apologies to the net, but all direct e-mail to Will has been returned.


Will,

I received your message, wrote down the number (incorrectly, as it turned
out) and tried to call.

Anyway, I just talked to Sam Shalala at ProTechnik here in Houston who did
the work last year.  They used a 924 turbo disk and heavy duty 944 parts for
the flywheel and pressure plate.  The machining was on the flywheel to
accomodate the 924 disk.  They also replaced the guide tube and throwout
bearing.  The whole job was expensive (~$1500) but should last the life of
the car.

Sam says he has talked to Sachs about the problems with the rubber hubs.
They say, basically, they would rather sell lots of replacement clutch
disks than fix the problem.  Further, PAG refuses to acknowledge their
flawed design decision.  In fact, the 968 is still using the rubber hub.
Sam also said the statements about the rubber hubs being 'kinder' on
components than the spring-centered parts is just BS put out by Porsche
to keep folks off the track.

By the way, I have been extremely with the spring-centered setup.  It even
seems to feel better than the original system.

Hope this answers your questions.  [oh yeah, the heavy duty 944 parts
mentioned above are 944 Turbo items]  If you would like to chat some try
calling me again or send me an e-mail message.

Regards,
John		smaardyk@rs6000.houston.wireline.slb.com

PS  Your name sure looks Dutch but you voice sounds British.

I prefer the paintball gun approach. Not only does it provide
the animal with a physical reminder not to repeat the behavior,

in two different areas of the brain, improving the possiblility
of affecting the behavior.

Date: Fri, 19 Nov 93 09:59:59 EST
From: mvmas@mvusa.att.com
Subject: re: Hans' skepticism

>To the Nay-Sayers,
>
>What's so hard to believe about 1200 HP from a turbocharged
>350 V8.  Back in the days before MPG requirements, pop-off
>valves, and methanol, indycars routinely made this kind of power
>with half this displacement.  All you have to do is crank up the
>boost.  Since Monterey, Hans will be the first to tell you that
>a twin-turbo, intercooled Porsche engine can make 1200 HP.
>
>I would like to tell you what some people at this institute are
>doing, with only 18 more months of education than a Senior at Rice,
>but then I would have to kill you.  Suffice to say, it would
>put a 1200 HP, 300 mph earth-bound vehicle to shame.
>
>Bob Mohan
>
>rmohan@afit.af.mil

I don't think there's anything hard to believe about a 1200hp engine.
I doubt this whole story (and thank Hans, er Chris for saying so first)
because it doesn't seem logical.  Sure, I can see a project of building
a powerful engine.  It's possible for any of the other improvements, too,
like a body designed from scratch for low cf, with active panels; like
ABS; like a 4WD system; like sophisticated computer engine control.

However, what would undergraduates (any number of them) be doing putting
all of this together in the same project spanning 2 years so far?!  I was
at an engineering school only a few years ago, and our school had the
Mini-baja and the SAE Methanol marathon projects.  These are projects you
can get your hands around.

I would think funding would dictate a smaller project, and at least, it
seems like if you needed to get 1200 hp on a budget, a chevy would be
a much better choice than a 928GTS (not even just a plain old 928 or 928S).
                                                                        
That all being said, I would not mind looking stupid if this
thing materializes.

                            labeling." - seen on package of Lysol towelletes

Date: Fri, 19 Nov 1993 11:14:44 -0800 (PST)
From: Cherie Woodward <cherie@cirrus.com>
Subject: RE: Cats


There are special sabots made for cat repelling. They are a soft felt plug
that will fit most .177 air pistols. Benjamin makes a pump air pistol that
has no power limit, i.e., you may pump it until desired fps is attained. This
in conjunction with the felt projectile is a great cat deterrent. The pellet
won't break the skin, yet provides a hell of a sting.

Cats not only ruin your finish, but it is DANGEROUS to eat vegetables out of
a bed that cats use as a litter-box.

Dog owners a forced by the city/county to restrain their pets, why aren't cat-
owners made responsible? It's really criminal to allow an animal to stray as 
this opens the animal up to many other hazzards than us Porsche owners can
plan for them. 

Personally, I think birds do more damage to my finish.

Good luck - Happy hunting

CW

Date: Fri, 19 Nov 93 13:24:57 PST
From: edp@berlioz.nsc.com (Ed Phillips 17751)
Subject: RE: Cats


I live in a Condo so I don't have an enclosed garage, just under-
ground style parking.  I do cover my car though.  I used to see
cats paw prints on the cover but didn't mind too much, cover can
be washed.  Then the critter slept on the cover one night and left
some of his fleas behind, I rolled up the cover and stored it in the
rear, presto, fleas in the carpets and bites all over my legs.
I had to set off a flea bomb to get rid of them.  After that I still
covered the car but then I safety pinned a thin sheet of plastic on
the surfaces that the cat would likely try to sleep, that got rid of
it.  One of my cat loving neighbors :-) suggested a saucer of anti-
freeze for the little bugger, I didn't, but......

Then there was the time it brought it's "kill" onto my cover, but
that's another story.

Ciao for now,

Ed.
'83 Light metallic blue 944

Date: Fri, 19 Nov 1993 17:27:12 -0500
From: John N Berry <jberry@stealth>
Subject: Thanks and Winter Storage (Again)


First, thanks for the comments on Zims - I'll send some business their way
this weekend.

Secondly, I was finally getting a bookcase re-stocked (only six months after
moving - I'm getting better at this! ;-) and came across an old issue of the
local magazine for the Roadrunner Region in New Mexico.  There was a reprint
of an article from West Texas Region about putting Porsches away for the
winter.  The author suggested that instead of running the motor for 30 minutes
every couple of weeks, you could:  

1)  Disable the fuel pump (take out the fuse, relay, unhook wires, etc.)

2)  Crank the motor until the oil pressure light goes out.

3)  Repeat a couple of times a month.

The idea was to send oil squirting around in the motor but not produce any
harmful by-products from combustion (like water vapor!)  The fuel pump trick
is to keep from washing down the cylinders w/ gasoline.  The article also 
mentioned that you could start the car and run it but you should make sure
that it comes up to operating temp. - drives off water in the oil and dries 
the motor innards.

In reading the article I realized that I may have a hard time getting the oil
up past 100 deg F this winter w/o driving the car.  It's a 2.7L that doesn't 
make it up to 210 deg F unless it's ran hard on a 100 def F day.  So, I may
decide on the *cranking but not firing* idea.  

Comments?  Has anyone out there ever had a 911 in storage for about six months?
I'm beginning to hate this Ohio life-style!!  Give me salt-free roads and
twisty mountain passes or give me a lobotomy so I can enjoy driving a Chevy
Blazer!  Enough said.  Have a good weekend.

John
jberry@afit.af.mil
'75 Peru Red 911S that's not looking forward to winter in Ohio  :-(

Date: Fri, 19 Nov 93 20:40 EST
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Exhaust Plugs??

>On a 928 S4 head there are four threaded plugs directly below each
>exhaust port.  When the plug is removed, there is a small (about 1.5mm)
>hole through to the exhaust port visible.  I can think of no possible
>reason for this, except that it is a great place to mount a thermocouple.
>Perhaps it is there for a hookup to some testing equipment?  Does anyone
>have a clue?

Sounds like air injection ports into the head.  The 928's used to have
this sort of air injection to achieve emissions.  Apparently, during
engine warm-up air injection would be applied to the heads to deal
with the very rich mixture used during cold start.  After warm-up, that
would stop and air injection would be done down-stream of the lambda
sensor, so that the sensor could do its job.

I am quoting someone else here, obviously.  I only have half a 928
engine in my car!  (A little humor for us 944S owners!)

Kevin

------------------------------

Date: Fri, 19 Nov 93 20:33 EST

shims get stored, as it were, on the other side of the pulley.

Kevin

Now for some info... A few weeks ago, I posted that I had some FE play on my
car that I was able to trace back to an inner tie rod bushing. I was looking
at replacing the whole tie rod, but my contact Dennis at Race Works
 (1-800-523-2610) took the time and found that the bushing was available by 
itself. The bushing connects the steering rack to the tie rod. I hope this helps
some of you with similar problems.

Date: Sun, 21 Nov 93 21:13 EST
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Yet another article

More fodder for the PCA newsletter editors among us.

- -----------------------------------------------------------------------

Book Review                                       Kevin Gross

Motorsports Medicine
Dr. Harlen C. Hunter and Rick Stoff
published by Lake Hill Press

Two years ago, when yours truly took on the task of being our region's 
Safety Chair, I did so not knowing a whole lot about safety stuff.  I was 
equipped with a certain amount of common sense, and of course had 
available all sorts of "received knowledge" (perhaps better referred to 
as "oral history"), for what they're both worth.  Probably not much.

So my plan was to educate myself.  There are thousands of books out 
there on cars and motorsports; just take a look at Classic Motorbook's 
catalog.  Surely there were at least a few having to do with safety sorts 
of things, right?  Well, no.  The only sources I came up with were 
published by the Sports Car Club of America (SCCA): their General 
Competition Rules (GCR's), and their Medical and Safety Manual.

Both documents have quite a bit of interesting information.  The 
GCR's describe acceptable safety equipment, such as roll bars and 
cages, harnesses, and so forth, and how to install and use them.  It 
really is not a good source of the "why" sort of information, though.  
The Medical and Safety Manual is largely devoted to staffing racing 
events with medical and emergency people and apparatus, and to 
managing medical emergencies.  It does have a few other tidbits, for 
example, brief safety guidelines for fuel system design.

Then along came Hunter and Stoff's book.  To get right to the point, it 
is a terrific book, and I recommend it not only to the safety czars of the 
world, but to anyone involved with high performance or competitive 
driving.

The book's subtitle is "Race Faster! Longer! Safer!" and in its 250 
pages, the authors do their best to suggest how that might best be 
accomplished.  A good half of the book is devoted to driver 
preparation, how to get in shape, what sorts of foods to eat and drink, 
vision and hearing issues, and the role of age (good news here for us 
post-teenagers).  They also give attention to matters of sleep, 
dehydration, exposure to the sun, and so forth.  And after all, what's 
the point of having that MFI tuned to 100% if the driver's only 
running at 80%?

The rest of "Motorsports Medicine" has chapters covering safety 
equipment and medical issues, such as what sorts of injuries take 
place.  On the latter, quite a few surprises.  You might guess that the 
most common injury to a driver is to his or her neck.  But how about 
burns: most are due to contact with coolant, not fire.  "Wheel whip" 
injuries to the hands and wrists are also quite common; ask Nigel 
Mansell about that.

The only problem you're likely to have with the book is finding a copy
of this privately published book.  Locally, European Performance
Engineering has a number of copies in stock (508 651 1316).  You can also
order it from Mittler Bros. Machine and Tool in Missouri, (314 673 2464).
Those Santas out there looking for a stocking item for their car-
crazed spouses would do well to throw a copy of  "Motorsports 
Medicine" in the back of the sleigh.

Date: Mon, 22 Nov 93 07:59:53 +0100
From: Magnus Ottenstrom <magot@weald.air.saab.se>

I presume it's the same design as for my '82 Coupe. Then you have to remove
the backs of the rear seats and to remove the whole panel which holds the
speakers. That's no problem. The problem is to find replacement speakers
to fit that tiny space. I would guess that the Blaupunkt speakers that I

Carrera RS) and to place 6X9" in the box that I then have to build...

Date: Mon, 22 Nov 93 10:03:27 EST
From: fwk@hogpa.ho.att.com
Subject: Re: 914 1.8L tranny woes :-(

>	I just lost all forward and reverse gears on my 914 the other
>week.  Let me describe what happened so that I can ask for help from
>porschephiles.
>
>	I was accelerating in 2nd gear when all of a sudden the gearshift
>jumped out of gear and I was coasting along.  As a side note:  whenever
>I accelerated in the car, the gearshift would deflect - why did it do
>this?

Based on the gearshift diflection on acceleration prior to the more
serious symptoms, I would suspect a motor mount problem. To relieve
(or exaserbate) your anxiety, you may want to disconnect the shift
linkage at the transmission, shift the transmission into a gear manually
at the transmission itself, and prove that there are gears still inside.
Then you can chase the linkage problems, from the motor mounts or
otherwise, more calmly.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@hogpa.att.com

Date: Mon, 22 Nov 93 08:51:59 PST
From: tsjje2@chevron.com
Subject: '84 Carrera

FROM: Scott Jessen
'     CITC ATD/Upstream, CPDN Chevron Tower IT Team
'
SUBJECT: '84 Carrera

In response to the question on the high mileage '84 Carrera....

I wouldn't be afraid of the mileage, although it will affect resale. You can
expect 250 - 300K miles out of a 3.2 motor that's maintained fairly decently.
Things off the top of my head: Have the cam chains been replaced? Should
figure this in to the price if not. Alternators fail every 60 - 80K or so. If
it has a ltd. slip it'll need rebuilding (won't hurt anything, just doesn't
work well). Compression test isn't good enough for me -- I'd do a leak down
test, it's the only *real* way to know the top end condition. Rotors could
need replacing, depending on what type of driving (city vs. highway).
Depending on the options, the price might be ok. If only a couple or none of
the desirable options are on it, I'd work on the seller over the price.

Take it to the *best* independent shop you can find in the area and have them
do a full "buyer checkout" -- should reveal any hidden items that will affect
your offer or even whether you want to purchase it at all. I once came really
close to buying a '78 SC that was still with orig. owner (wife only drove the
car, etc.), but failed the leakdown -- it crushed me and the owner (I really
wanted it, it was beautiful in all other aspects), but it had a cracked piston
or broken ring according to my trustworthy shop, and wouldn't have been worth
the hassle or expense (seller didn't believe my mechanic and thought I was
after a lower price) -- I just passed on it...

When I was out in CA last week I looked at a couple of '84s that had the
*right* options and in excellent low (for '84) mileage condition in the $22 -
$23K range. We've decided to keep ours until we get back to CA where the
market is better (offers in the $19K range aren't gettin' it..).

Hope this helps you some...       good luck!

 -Scott

Subject: Misc Questions

Lou writes:

>1. I would like to replace the rear speakers on my '84 Targa. Has any one of
>you
>done this before? Do they come off from the inside of the car or from the
>engine compartment?

Yes. They come out topside.  Try Circuit City, I found some really nice ones
to replace the stock junk that came with the car -- they had a bunch to choose
from to fit your budget/ear range...

>cleaned the undercarriage in my car. I found a cylindrical tank hiding
>in front of the oil tank, right behind the door latch. It has some hoses going
>into the engine compartment. Can someone tell me what is it function?

It is an Oil Thermostat, assuming it's not *just* in front of the oil tank,
but rather in front of the rt. rear wheel and in the the fender well behind
the door.  This thermostat sends the oil to the front fender cooler when it
gets hot enough (192 deg F or so)

 -Scott

Date: Mon, 22 Nov 93 08:53:13 PST
From: albrecht@nosc.mil (Robert Albrecht)
Subject: Re: ignition woes again

     I don't have a solution for your ignition problem, just a
little insight.  The coil will generate a high-voltage (spark)
whenever the primary current is *CHANGED*.  That is why you get a
spark when you turn the ignition switch on, and again when you
turn it off.  This indicates that the high-voltage portion of the
ignition is OK (coil and wiring).  The problem is either in the
distributer triggering mechanism (points, magnetic, or photo
triggering depending), or the electronics controlling current to
the primary winding of the coil.  Too bad it's so expensive to
just swap parts 'till you find it.

Bob
^Z



------------------------------

Date: Mon, 22 Nov 93 09:05:15 PST
From: albrecht@nosc.mil (Robert Albrecht)
Subject: Re: Speakers

     I recently replaced speakers in my '83 Targa.  It made a BIG
difference.  In my car, the rear speakers were removeable without
fussing with any of the interior upholstery.  Just remove the
four screws (ea) lift them out and disconnect the wires.  When
you look at them it will be obvious why the sound is poor.

     I replaced mine with Boston Acoustics C700 plates (woofer
and tweeter).  These are rectangular units which stand about 1"
above the mounting surface.  The "plate" configuration solves the
problem with lack of depth clearance associated with an oval co-
axial configuration.  The woofer magnet fits through the old
hole.  You don't have to do any cutting, although you will put
screws through the upholstry to mount the plates.  This isn't a
problem unless you want to go back to the originals at some later
time.  They sound great!  You can save some $ by ordering them
mail order.

Bob
^Z

these shops knew Bob Grigsby and had great things to say about him
(and they are competitors!).  Grigsby has his own shop, and does all
the work himself.  He goes out of the way when he services your car
to look for warning signs and other problems you should be alert to.
His prices are comparable to the others, maybe even 5% higher or so
(but then he's doing all the work not some hourly grunt).  And he's
never failed me in the 5 years I've been going to him.

more than 20 years.  His wife, Linda, is some kind of parts specialist
for Carlsen's.  They both drive 356s and show their cars.  Obviously
your typical Porsche-crazy family.

Date: Tue, 23 Nov 93 15:02:20 +0100
From: Kelvin Ross <ross@hera.informatik.uni-dortmund.de>
Subject: Factory Tour....

I went on a tour of the Porsche Factory yesterday.  I thought some of you may be interested in some of the things I heard.  Mainly trivial facts.

Each car is made on order.  It must be prepurchased from the customer or dealer.  They have a spec for the thing that is to be produced, and the configuration must come together at the right points to give the right model, engine, colour, interior, country requirements, etc. as specified for the customer.  I think BMW do the same as well now.  But it sounds like a bit of organisation to get it all there at the right time....`

Apparently there are already 400 orders for the boxer from the U.S.  Production release is Sept. 1995- ready for '96.  Sales of the 993 (next generation 911) is quite good and they seem to think that this is going to pull them out of the s__t.

It takes average of 3 1/2 hours to assemble a 911 engine.  These engines are assembled on a production line by a number of mechanics.  On the other hand a 911 turbo, 928, and 968 engine are assembled by one person only, he (they only employ males on the production lines) follows the engine around the line.

Someone talked about the dyno testing and breaking in of engines.  An engine must be between 100 and 105% of the designed performance figures.  They also take into account the atmospheric conditions in doing the testing.  An engine, even when over powered is returned to be reassembled - or repaired - if it does not meet the perfomance criteria.  Except with special engines such as for the 911 RSR, here the overpowered engines are also sold to the customer, but completely at random, you can't choose the engine you want.

In the paint shop (didn't really get to see much there, except a poster describing what is happening) they do not use robots to do the painting.  It is still all done by hand.

The 993 - next generation 911 - was in production.  Main differences seem to be sloped back headlights, like on the 959.  Also the rear guards sort of looked like nicer shaped 944 box guards.  It looks real good.

There was a 3.8 911 RSR on the 993 production line when I was there.  I wonder who santa is visiting this christmas.  It was plain white with a huge rear tail on it.  It was about to go into the line where the "technical interior" is added (i.e. all the suspension, engine, etc.).  All the 911s at this stage have the interior, except for front seats in place.  The RSR only had its roll cage and the drivers seat mount - I suppose thats about all you want in a race car...

It takes them about 15 mins to bolt the car to the engine and transmission.  The car is lowered onto the engine and rear transmission with the rear engined thingies.  The engine is sitting on a trolley which starts to roll along as the car is moving on the production line.  The workers are mostly sitting on stools which roll along as well when the are doing this part.

The last thing we saw was the upholstery room.  Your 911 requires about 13 square metres of leather for the interior.  There is about 30% wastage in the cutting for cow hide, 40% for buffalo, and 70% for osterrich.  Yeah this guy got his leather interior done in osterrich leather.  He only wanted the leather which had the nice feather pattern as well (and it was died purple).  It cost about 20,000 DM for this option.  But Porsche will do it in whatever you can get hold of (same with paint colout).  I wonder what they would think of kangaroo or koala (bear)  (Oops, forgot about the animal libbers B^).  I think they said it takes about 10 hours to make a leather dashboard.

After seeing the factory and finding how out how long it takes to make things I have some serious problems with my mechanic, my painter, my upholosterer, etc....  I think I might pull my engine on a Saturday when I get home, you know pull it apart and put it back in on Sunday.

As we were about to leave the museaum Aerosmith (heavy metal band touring Germany at the moment) turned up to do a tour of the factory - and most likely pick up cars as well.  Our guide/ public relations woman was telling us that she hates taking heavy metal people around.  She told us that she showed Judas Priest around once and he started off into the production line, picked things up and waved them around, got carried away, etc.  Wonder what these boys got up to???

They had some Japanese guys wandering around the factory, looking over shoulders.  Apparently they are brought in from some managment consultants to improve production.  So management had all these signs for the workers saying try to work 10% more while they are here.  Doesn't seem to make sense to me - shouldn't they be seeing how they are normally working.

The museaum wasn't quite as good as I expected (especially after seeing BMW and Mercedes).  They only had about 30 cars.  While in Stuttgart you should also see the Mercedes museum, it has the first automobile there, as well as many others.  It includes many cars developed for Mercedes by F. Porsche no. 1.  I went there on a Sunday.  They said during the week it is quite likely you can just slip into a group for a factory tour there as well without booking (providing your not a large group).  However I did not really have the opportunity to go back for this.

At the Porsche factory I would have liked to have seen the body fabrication, I don't know why they didn't show us that.  I wanted to see some welding robots working, maybe at mercedes they would have that.  Anyhow keep that in mind if your heading to Stuttgart.  Next time I should find someone that can get me to Weisach as well.

Anyhow just a bit of trivia.

Cheers
Kelvin

Currently on exchange at the University of Dortmund, Germany.

Date: Tue, 23 Nov 93 09:51:44 -0700
From: Bob_Pentecost <bpenteco@hpbs987.boi.hp.com>
Subject: Cats and Dogs


The suggestion of tying a dog to the bumper reminded me of what happened 
on one of our region members a couple of years ago. He took about 30 minutes
to tell the entire story (it was the first time he could talk about it
since it had happened). Of course, a few beers and his spouse explaining
her side of the story made it quite humorous.

The dog was acquired when his spouse found it as a stray. They were headed
out of town the next day and she talked him into letting the dog go along.
The dog gets car sick and pukes on the leather seats. While visiting 
his brother, the dog messes up the white carpet in the living room.
Departing early, they head home and stop to eat at a restaurant. Not wanting
to leave the dog in the car, they tie it to the bumper. When they come out
of the restaurant there is something on the car. As they get closer to the
car they make it out to be -- the dog. Closer inspection reveals that the
dog had a really hard time getting up on the car. The scratches were so bad
that the car had to be repainted.

It was a couple of weeks before he spoke to his spouse. I don't know what 
happened to the dog (it did come home with them, inside the car - not tied 
to the bumper).

They joined one of our tours recently and they had a parrot in the back
seat (caged). I think there's an animal lover in the family.

Bob Pentecost
Silver Sage Region, PCA
'83 Surinam 944
'76 Light Blue Chevy Pickup (until side-impact, then '76 Charred Black...)

Date: Tue, 23 Nov 93 10:57:46 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 912 compression

Compression is 9.5:1, so figure that at roughly 120 pounds nominal (from
14.5*(9.5-1), due to the way compression gauges work). Spec book says the
mean working pressure is 14.3 kp/cm**2, if you want to convert... 8{)

Nominal variance is 5-10% per cylinder, with worst no more than 10%
less than best.

Stan

Subject: 931 Intercooler

Hello!

Some of you may recall me lamenting that the wastegate diaphragm on my
faithful (?) 931 "bought the farm" a couple of months ago.  Since then, 
I have been on a quest for parts to get the car back on the road.  During the
course of this work, I discovered that it is strongly recommended that the
factory boost NOT be increased on these machines, either by putting in a 
different wastegate spring or by using one of the variable "dial-in-boost" 
methods, in the absence of an intercooler.  Too bad, because these 
much-demeaned little 2-liter engines ("...really an Audi..., etc.") are 
really capable of a large output under high boost.


My naive question then: Does anyone know of any 924 Turbo Carrera 
intercoolers gathering dust somewhere that might be for sale at a reasonable 
cost?  If so, I would appreciate it if you would let me know.


Many thanks.

Date: Tue, 23 Nov 93 14:21:55 -0800
From: Ken Dove <kfd@sequent.com>
Subject: Looking for 3.0 911 Engine

Well the winter is upon us, and just in time my 2.4 engine
broke a valve and caused much related damage in 2 cylinders.
It was getting about time for a rebuild anyway. (probably cut this one
too close!) So I was in process of trying to decide 
whether to rebuild do a straight rebuild, bring it to "S" specs
or just swap in a 3.0 motor.  Haven't examined the bottom end
yet so how much work "down there" is unknown.

As long as I've already gone down the golden path of spending
more $$$ it seems like a good time to consider swapping up to 
a 3.0 engine.  The engine already has webbers so rejetting would
be needed. The transmission is a 915 series.  The car is a 71 911

Date: Wed, 24 Nov 93 08:53:46 EST
From: fwk@hogpa.ho.att.com
Subject: Re: leakdown test

>    Several people have mentioned the 'leakdown' test to evaluate the
>condition of an engine. Please, can someone describe the test method,
>the equipment used, and significance of the results in terms of
>engine condition.

A leakdown test is in effect a measurement of the rate of air flow
into each cylinder with all of the valves closed. The magnitude of the
air flow determines the total leakage from all valves plus the rings
(plus anywhere else it manages to leak). It is possible to determine the
source of the leak (which valve, etc) by listening for the sound of
escaping air at the intake manifold, exhaust pipe or crankcase breather.

A leakdown tester is typically constructed by measuring the pressure
drop across a restrictor (just like measuring current by measuring
voltage drop across a resistor, if you think in electrical analogys).
Thus the physical appearance is an input to connect to a source of
air pressure (perhaps 80-100 PSI), a pair of pressure gauges (or a
pressure regulator and a single gauge) to measure differential pressure,
and an output hose to connect to an adapter which screws into a spark
plug hole. Leakdown measurements are most frequently expressed as a
pressure difference, rather than translated into an actual flow rate.

In use, the engine is first brought to operating temperature, so
everything fits. Then, with the engine stopped, each cylinder is brought
to TDC on the compression stroke, the plug removed, the adapter
substituted, and the pressure difference (e.g. flow rate) measured.
It is also possible to measure the leakdown at other than TDC (given
that the valves are still closed) if a means is provided to hold the
crank from rotating against the pressure (80 PSI x roughly 12 Sq. In.
for a 4" piston = lots of force). This allows measuring ring leakage at
other locations along the cylinder wall. You can get away without holding
the crank at TDC if you set it accurately enough.

Having said all this, does anyone happen to know if there are standards
for the flow restriction which allow readings from a leakdown tester
made by one supplier to be compared to readings from another supplier's
tester?
An alternative would be a "calibrated leak", formed by a certain size
drill, for instance, which produces a given pressure difference.
I would like to know if my leakdown tester produces readings comparable
to others, so that when I talk about an engine with 3 PSI leakdown I
know how it compares to others.

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@hogpa.att.com

------------------------------

Date: Wed, 24 Nov 93 10:06:11 EST
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: leakdown test


   .......LEAKDOWN TEST........
       Several people have mentioned the 'leakdown' test to evaluate the
   condition of an engine. Please, can someone describe the test method,
   the equipment used, and significance of the results in terms of
   engine condition.

Test Method: Bring cylinder to TDC (top dead center) compression
stroke, remove spark plug and insert tester. Pressurize cylinder with
shop air (approximately 100 psi) and measure the ratio of input
pressure to cylinder pressure. The result is what is called "percent
leakdown" and is expressed as a percentage. For example, if the input
source air pressure is 120 psi from the shop compressor, and the
cylinder gauge is reading 105, then the percent leakdown is:
(120-105)/120=13%. Note: shop air is kept on during entire test so
there is some "flow" through the system at all times. This helps to
determine problems, see below.

Equipment Used: Leakdown tester is very simple. Device has an input
for high pressure air (shop air) with a one way valve and a gauge to
read input air pressure. There is also a gauge to read cylinder
pressure so you can get the ratio. In order to pressurize the cylinder
there is a fitting to thread into the cylinder head spark-plug hole,
often interchangeable for different sized plugs.

Significance of Results: For a healthy engine, the measurement used is
"percent leakdown" which refers to the ratio of input pressure to
cylinder head pressure. There is always some leakdown, even in a new
engine, but it should be fairly low, like <10%. As long as the number
is low, no further diagnostics are required. *But* if the number is
high, then the device can be used to pinpoint the bad part, because
the excessive leakdown will make an audible "hiss" that you can hear.
For example, you do a leakdown on #3 cylinder of your 944 and you get
45% leakdown, and you examine further and hear hissing coming from the
exhaust it is possible you have a problem with the exhaust valve
seats, bent valve etc. Other area to look for air leakdown are bubbles
in the coolant (cracked head, blown head gasket, cracked block),
hissing in the intake manifold (bent valves, cracked head) or pressure
building up in the crankcase (rings worn, cracked block). This is only
one tool in the arsenal but it is very useful. 

Don't throw away the compression gauge however, it can do something a
leakdown tester can't, and thats give a nice time-history of the
combustion chamber pressurization. So one is not necessarily "better" 
than the other, they just tell you different information.

- --
- ----------------------------------------------------------
John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

Date: Wed, 24 Nov 1993 11:49:40 -0500
From: Robert B Mohan <rmohan@afit.af.mil>
Subject: Re: leakdown test

>Having said all this, does anyone happen to know if there are standards
>for the flow restriction which allow readings from a leakdown tester
>made by one supplier to be compared to readings from another supplier's
>tester?
>An alternative would be a "calibrated leak", formed by a certain size
>drill, for instance, which produces a given pressure difference.
>I would like to know if my leakdown tester produces readings comparable
>to others, so that when I talk about an engine with 3 PSI leakdown I
>know how it compares to others.

Rather than give results in absolute pressure difference from the
gages, I cumpute it as a ratio or percentage, so it can relate to
other measurements.  An easy way is to set the test pressure at 50 psi,
and compare this to the cylinder pressure.  For example, a cylinder
pressure of 47 psi would indicate a cylinder leak down percentage of
6%.  This could be compared to someone else's test done at another
pressure.  Porsche engines should always have less than 5% leak-down.

Bob Mohan

rmohan@afit.af.mil

Date: Wed, 24 Nov 93 14:01:33 EST
From: fwk@hogpa.ho.att.com
Subject: Re: leakdown test

>>Having said all this, does anyone happen to know if there are standards
>>for the flow restriction which allow readings from a leakdown tester
>>made by one supplier to be compared to readings from another supplier's
>>tester?
>>An alternative would be a "calibrated leak", formed by a certain size
>>drill, for instance, which produces a given pressure difference.
>>I would like to know if my leakdown tester produces readings comparable
>>to others, so that when I talk about an engine with 3 PSI leakdown I
>>know how it compares to others.
>
>Rather than give results in absolute pressure difference from the
>gages, I cumpute it as a ratio or percentage, so it can relate to
>other measurements.  An easy way is to set the test pressure at 50 psi,
>and compare this to the cylinder pressure.  For example, a cylinder
>pressure of 47 psi would indicate a cylinder leak down percentage of
>6%.  This could be compared to someone else's test done at another
>pressure.  Porsche engines should always have less than 5% leak-down.

Bob, as well as others, suggests that using percentages solves the
calibration problem. Percentages may solve the problem that one
tester measures at 50 PSI and another at 80 PSI, but it doesn't
solve what I see as the larger and also more subtle source of error,
which is the nature of the restriction across which the pressure
difference is measured. Depending of the restriction, a given pressure
difference (or percentage, if you like) can correspond to radically
different flow rates, which are what you are really trying to measure.
If electrical analogies help, think about measuring current by knowing
the voltage difference but not what resistance it is measured across.
Percentages don't help much.

This could be managed by comparing the details of the restrictions
in each different type of leakdown tester, but that is buried in the
instrument. Another approach is an external >>flow<< standard, like
a calibrated hole. I made such a standard by putting a pipe plug
(brass) into a quick disconnect fitting and then drilling a hole in
it, expanding the hole until the tester read 5 PSI drop at 80 PSI
(or about 6%). My intent was to take this "standard leak" to the
local Porsche guru, who is also a friend, and ask him to see what
his leakdown tester read with the standard connected. I made the
standard a year or two ago, but the right circumstances never came
up to get it measured by another tester other than mine.

If anyone else on porschephiles is interested, this may be another
forum for the same test. I don't remember the size hole I ended up with
(I do remember being surprised that the hole was relatively large-
perhaps 0.050"?), but I can measure the hole tonight. If someone else
wants to build the same "standard" with the same hole size we can
do the calibration remotely. The flow may not be exactly the same due
to the details of air flow, even with the same hole size, but it seems
that it ought to be in the ballpark.

Is anyone interested (who has a leakdown tester)?

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@hogpa.att.com

 ----------

>Equipment Used: Leakdown tester is very simple. Device has an input
>for high pressure air (shop air) with a one way valve and a gauge to
>read input air pressure. There is also a gauge to read cylinder
>pressure so you can get the ratio. In order to pressurize the cylinder
>there is a fitting to thread into the cylinder head spark-plug hole,
>often interchangeable for different sized plugs.

------------------------------

Date: Thu, 25 Nov 93 07:15:08 +0100
From: Magnus Ottenstrom <magot@weald.air.saab.se>
Subject: alternator

Blaine wrote:
>"I recently had my alternator on my '80 SC rebuilt (all the insurance
>company would cover).  It seemed okay until the other day, when I got all
>the signs of a bad charging system - tach flying all over the place,
>engine running rough, then dying followed by 'rrn rrn rrrrr<silence>' when
>I turned the key.  The only missing sign was the alternator light not
>coming on."  
 
When you get this kind of failure mode it can be that one of the brushes
are broken. You then don't get any current to the alternator light when
turning the key. And, of course, you don't get any warning that the 
alternator doesn't work when driving. I had this problem with my '82 SC 
with Paris-Rhone alternator (WHY a French alternator in a quality sport car??) 
so now I always check that the alternator warning light comes on when turning 
the key.

Magnus

Porschephiles #581                         Sat Nov 27 03:00:42 CST 1993
Date: Fri, 26 Nov 93 8:48 GMT
From: Trond Vidar Olsen <trond.vidar.olsen@hrp.no>
Subject: Heater controls without owners' manual

  | Ok.  So how exactly *do* the heater controls in a `71 911T work?

I am not sure whether you are refering to *how* the heater system
works,or just how the heater control handles should be manouvered.

The theories behind the heating system in the 911 is very similar to
most conventional heating systems.The transfer of excessive heat energy from
engine to the driver's compartement.

Practically there are a few ways to do this,the most common with a
heat exchanger connected to the cars cooling system.This works
fine,....as long as there is water in your hoses.The water in your
cooling system should be about 85-90 deg. C.The water transfer a lot
of this energy into the air surrounding the heat exchanger.This air is
led into the car via ducting.

As the allmighty 911 is cooled by air,the solution mentioned above was
sort of awkward,.....of course being rather familiar with air cooled
engines the Porsche people knew the solution.They had to use the
exhaust gases for this purpose. By 'clamping' a heat exchanger upon
the exhaust tubes,one transfers some of the heat energy.The efficiency
is not as high as the water heated exchanger but the exhaust gases hold a
much higher temperature than the cooling water.
With some 'ducting' from the engine cooling fan,you can put pressure
into this system.Then the heated air is blown forward,via channels in
the chassi and into the car.Then by using a valve you can shut off the
air from the cooling fan,if the Arizonian Summer allows it :)
Some newer 911's have an extra electric blower too.This could be good
for city driving.Remember you need a steady revving engine to keep
that hot air flowing........;)

 To keep the heater system in good order,it is important to use it now
and then.Moisture is trapped inside the heater channels,so be sure to
use the system sometimes on sunny days too.That will assure you a good
working long living heating system.

cheers,
Trond

Date: Fri, 26 Nov 93 13:37:12 PST
From: David L. Gardner <gardner@physics.nps.navy.mil>
Subject: Some Leakdown Details

To Whom It  May Concern,
	The leakdown test is a relative, not absolute, measurement.  The flow of 
air through the testor orifice is, among other things, a function of the 
pressure difference, fourth power of orifice radius, and orifice length, and 
described by Poiseuille's formula (Landau & Lifshitz, Fluid Mechanics, page 
57).  
	The question becomes one of how large an opening is permitting the air to 
escape from the cylinder.  If no air is escaping, then the pressure in the 
cylinder will equalize with the supply pressure.  Further, the qustion 
becomes the opening size relative to the port dimension.  For example, if 
both a large engine and small engine have the same size defect in a valve 
seat, both will give the same reading during a leakdown test.  However, the 
effect on a small 356 engine vs. a large dragster engine would be 
substantial.  Piston ring end gaps, etc, obviously contribute to the reason 
why the test is only relative.  
	When the test is made, the differences between cylinders is the 
information of value.  However, were someone to test a new or newly rebuilt 
engine and use that as the benchmark for comparison for similarly sized 
engines using the same tester, the leakdown test provides a bit more 
information as to the health of an engiine.  Another advantage of the 
leakdown test is that one may use a piece of hose to determine the source of 
the leaks, i.e. rings, exhaust valve, or intake valve; one does this by 
placing a piece of hose between the ear and the oil filler, tailpipe, and 
induction system, respectively.
	I have built two testers and compared them to the Snap-on tester.  The 
comparison was made by filling a plastic jug with water and inverting it in a 
filled sink.  I then inserted an air nozzle, which was connected to the 
tester, into the jug and measured the time required to fill the jug with air.  
Using a small valve in the fabricate testers, I was able to adjust the air 
flow to match that of the Snap-on tester.  As I recall, the time was 4.8 sec 
for a gallon of water (average after 10 trials).
	For my own purposes as a hobbyist, I have establised a value of 10% 
maximum pressure loss for any 2.0 - 3.0 911 engine cylinder before I become  
concerned (I only work on 911 engines).  At the 30% level, I would probably 
do a rebuid, the extent of which would depend on the source of leak.  

Hope this helps.

David Gardner
Close Enough Engineeering

Date: Thu, 25 Nov 1993 06:51:13 -0800
From: kweber@fmiapd.com
Subject:  Porsche and Ferrari - Why?

A few comments on the recent subject of who owns which type and
why, with a few guesses for your consideration.

I've experience with both types, with both clubs, and with both
at concours.  Recently I've watched the level and content of the
messages here at porschephiles, and I've been watching the same
on USEnet "exotic.cars".

My observation is that a far higher percentage of Porsche people
have access to and interest in Internet communications than do
Ferrari people.  Porsche people are often involved in engineering
jobs and are still climbing within their profession.  They
embrace new technology (internet, email, computers) and have an
appreciation for the technolog embodied in their car.  Therefore,
we on this network see a strong following via
porschephiles@tta.com.

On the other hand, many Ferrari owners are involved in
professions that isolate them from internet, or have reached that
point in their lives where they get their information from others
and use their experience to act on it; they have no direct
contact with a networked computer.  They are involved in enjoying
what they have.  The land developers and private attorneys and
doctors and, well, we could go on.  These are the people who pay
cash for a new $190K Ferrari TR 512 primarily for show, to add a
notch on the goals in life chart.  Note that there is no group on
internet like porschephiles; there should be, but thus far the
critical mass required has not be assembled.

We could rage for pages on the reasons for owning each, and there
are as many valid reasons as there are people.  And certainly the
points I made above are generalizations, where exact opposites to
my assertions do exist.  I, for one, have no Porsches (now), do
have two Ferraris, and am still climbing within my profession (I
think) and am actively on internet, BBS's and play with
computers.  While I don't fit the profile, I do believe the
majority profiles are as I mentioned above.

Final note: I'll bet if we all had our way, we'd have one of
every sports car we saw, just to make sure we could experience
each one at will.  That's what I need!

Any comments?

Ken

Ferrari 246 GTS
Ferrari 308 GT/4
Shooting for Porsche '87 targa

Date: Sun, 28 Nov 93 16:04:02 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: 914 vs 914-6

>I've finally been catching up on the group. One thing that I wanted some 
>more info on is building a high output type 4 motor for the 914. What
>I was wondering:

>What is involved in building a 130 HP type 4 motor?

You need to remember this: any time you ask about how you do X,
or how much X will cost, where X is *ANY* non-stock operation, the
answer is *ALWAYS* "It Depends."

In this case, you have lots of answers *I* need before I can answer:
do you have to worry about emissions issues to keep the car legal
and on the road? is it a racing or competition vehicle, and if so
what class under which sanctioning organization? where do you want
to make the power - under 4k RPM or over 5K RPM?  do you have the 
necessary parts/tools/experience to do this yourself (answer to this
one I know: NO - or you wouldn't be asking)? do you have the budget
to support the development of something like what you're looking for?

>What kind of life expantancy can be built into one?

Depends. Can build anything from a 195 HP 1.8 legal for SCCA Production
class road racing with a 6-10 *HOUR* life expectancy, to a say 135 HP
mostly stock 2.0 with factory EFI and a 50,000 + mile life expectancy.

>What would the displacement be? 

See above. General rules are as follows: smaller displacment means increased
heat of combustion to reach a given HP target. Heat is bad for air cooled
engines, and will shorten life of engine. Larger displacement than 2.0 liter
means you need *special* parts **AND** machine work to make things hang 
together (stroker cranks mean longer rods mean custom barrel spacers and
custom exhaust... big bore means bigger holes in the case and need for 
lightened parts... etc.).  You also have to factor in RPM limits -- HP ==
torque * RPM, right? (OK, more or less, but its good for a 1st order guess)
High RPM means you need stuff that will spin fast without breaking or breaking
the engine case. Low RPM means you have to optimize differently, *AND* that
you may have to put RPM limiters on the engine to keep from blowing it up
if you get rev-happy...  So it's an optimization problem, really...

>Could I reach that kind of hp with my 1.8? I have 40MM carbs and a bursch
>  pipe on it now. It will need rebuilt soon (110K, but still runs strong).

If you have carbs now, then you don't have to worry about emissions questions
or at least, you haven't yet (beware if you live in any about-to-be-interdicted
area, such as much of Texas-- what you can get away with TODAY will be fatal 
in 1995). 

Something else: the cases on these suckers are *IDENTICAL*. 1.7 == 1.8 == 2.0
*EXCEPT* for classes where you need to "prove" you started with a certain
engine, and then the serial number on the case matters. For street stuff, 
a 1.8 is as good a place to start as a 2.0, particularly if you don't have
EFI.

>I've clip the mail I saw from awhile back for those who didn't see it.

This is a frequent topic of discussion. As one of the resident 914 guys,
and as a racing constructor for a variety of Porsche based classes, I
*NEVER* let this go by without comment. So, there is a lot of commentary
to be read.

BTW, the prices you were given in one note are bullshit. A racing grade 
engine will cost you at least $6k, and a full 195 HP 1.8 EProd engine
will be *AT LEAST* $12k. For the street, the best deal I'm aware of is
Elmer Fohey's "street/autox" grade rebuild, which is $750 plus parts.
And parts can add up in a hurry -- a set of high-flow heads is over
$1k (although a racing valve job and some port matching is included in 
his base price), counterbalanced crank is $400, Carillo rods are $900 a 
set, pistons and cylinders are $600 a set, etc. Obviously the higher your
requirements are, the more go-fast parts you need.

>I'd appreciate any feedback.

Hope you mean that...

Stan

From: gwj@techapp.com (Gregg Jensen)
Subject: success (at least partial)


Well I have achieved a partial success in getting my 77 911S running (for
anyone that really wanted to know).  It was GREAT learning the inner workings
of the ignition system, only to find out it was a loose connection. But,
that IS why I bought a non-running 911.  For the success, after the
ignition and a new fuel filter, I finally got it started and it ran.  The
partial side is that it seemed to run on 90% oil and 10% gas, judging on
the oil that was splattered on the wall behind the exhaust.  I roughly
estimate the amount to about 1/2 a quart.  During this short run (about 30
to 45 seconds) I had the air filter/air box cover off.  After I assesed the
damage to the floor and wall behind the exhaust I noticed there was at
least a quart in the air box (and running out of the corner and down to the
floor).  I understand this is NOT good ;-) but, I would like to hear a
possible reason there was SO much oil in the air box.  Where would it come
from; loose fitting somewhere, missing connection?  Would bad rings cause
this much oil to surface?  I do understand that the engine is probably
history. But, the way I look at it, it provides me with a great opportunity
to break into it without hurting it any worse than it is (again one of the
reasons I bought a non-running one is to learn about more about the
insides of the engine).   Now to my questions; when pulling the engine and
breaking it open, are there any hints, "watch out for"s or "make sure you
do"s that I should be aware of?  And last of all, is it possible to make it
run again (barring structural damage)? 

NOTE: this engine had a major rebuild within the last 2 years and has 15k
miles since the rebuild.  It was running fine up until the beginning of this
year when it was diagnosed to have some bad rings.

Yes, I will get another engine for it in the future, but I would like to
learn more with this one first.

BTW, a BIG thanks to those that helped with troubleshooting the ignition
system.

Gregg Jensen
gwj@techapp.com

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 1993 00:24:05 -0500 (EST)
From: "James L. Fry" <JFRY@uoft02.utoledo.edu>
Subject: $931 Woes

Dear Fellow Porschephiles,

Thanks for all of your helpful comments regarding my questions 
on 931 intercooling and "other than 85 mph" speedometers.  I 
thought I'd bring you up to date on developments with my 
machine.

Following replacement of a ruptured wastegate diaphragm 
(Andial, $100) I decided, upon the recommendation of the Andial 
tech guys, NOT to increase the factory boost without an 
intercooler.  There is no doubt that increased boost will 
create more power from the U.S. spec'ed 931, but IMHO, I feel 
there is a significant risk of having a "hand grenade" under 
the hood without the intercooler (especially with 103K on the 
clock of mine!).  BTW, judging from the response to my question 
about intercooler availability, there does NOT seem to be a 
superabundance of old 924 Carrera GT intercoolers gathering 
dust anywhere.  (On the other hand, it has occurred to me that 
there must be a lot of thermostatically fan-cooled intercoolers 
from Renault Fuego Turbos that could be picked up "for a song" 
in junkyards all over the place.  These are compact and might 
be fitted under the hood of a 931.  Just a thought.)

I did replace the original 85 mph speedometer with a 170 mph 
VDO and seem not to have experienced any calibration problems.  
(I had calibrated the original VDO against the tach in 5th gear 
from 55 mph and up and the replacement seems to be reading 
within a few % of the original).

Finally, after having the car in the shop for more than two 
months and spending more money than I would like to think about 
on esoteric, factory-available-only turbo bolts (@ $50 ea.!) 
etc., it was time to adjust the valves and get on the road for 
one last run before the winter salt trucks appear here in the 
rust belt.  Guess what, $931ers and others interested in a sad 
tale?  A camshaft with the hardened lobe surfaces worn away, 
exposing the soft, fast-wearing inner metal!  Ditto for most, 
if not all, of the lifters!  Oh well, what's another $350-400 
for a camshaft and about $70 each per lifter?  Anybody else had 
this problem?  I am told by a mechanic for whom I have a great 
deal of respect that it's probably not much of a gain to put a 
"hot" cam regrind into a $931 because the original is already 
so "peaky".  Any comments about this?

Best regards.

Jim Fry	'82 931

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 1993 10:53:36 +0000
From: katz_p_m <katz_p_m@bt-web.bt.co.uk>
Subject: Impressions of driving a 911 and a 968CS


	In my quest to change my 1985/2 944 for something faster, I went to
my local Porsche dealers on Saturday to test drive a 911 (1989 3.2 Carrera
Sport) and a 968 Club Sport, the first time that I have driven either of
these cars.

	First off was the 911, in Baltic Blue, with 46k miles on the
clock. Perhaps I was in awe of the car (after wanting one for so long), or
perhaps it was trying to cope with the offset pedals, or perhaps it was
the unfamiliar road, but I was disappointed :-( I'm quite happy to admit
that the fault was probably more to do with how I was driving it than with
the car itself, but it never seemed to have the "kick in the back"
acceleration that I found in a BMW M5, and it was only towards the end of
the test drive that I discovered that much of the power only comes in at
about 3500rpm. Low-end torque wasn't much to write home about and the
gear-change not very well defined (at least not by 944 standards).

	The 968 Club Sport (*very* yellow, with 5.5k miles), on the other
hand, was instantly familiar (a 944 S3?). The Kevlar seats are very
awkward to get in and out of, but very comfortable once you are in them.
The steering wheel is small, padded and has perfectly weighted power
assistance. The 6-speed box is very smooth and tons of power is there in
both 2nd and 3rd gear (I didn't get a chance to try any high-speed
motorway driving ... perhaps the 911 would have shone here?), though I did
stall it a number of times when pulling away from rest! Porsche have saved
weight by removing everything unnecessary from the car (sunroof, electric
windows, rear seats, coathooks!, etc) along with some they should have
kept (rear wiper). The car has ABS, though. A fantastic car, but just a
bit too easy to drive and lacking excitement - though I never took it
anywhere near the limit. 

	So ... I want a 911 but with the power and handling of the 968CS!
I've got to go and drive another 911 or two, just to make sure that I
wasn't mistaken. But, if they all disappoint, I may well go for the M5
(heresy!) ... or some performance driving lessons!

	Any comments welcomed.

Philip

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 93 09:38:54 EST
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: '77 911 oil leaking


   I haven't had a good look under my car yet, but have noticed that every
   time I stop, I am losing several tablespoons of engine oil from two spots
   under the engine.  Also, driving around, especially with the heater (if
   you can call it that!) on, gives an aroma of burning oil and gas. 

   I have not lost a significant amount from driving 100 miles, so I am
   hoping that the burning oil is from a leak from the valve covers, and
   dripping on a hot surface.  


Here's something I posted a while back, hope it helps on oil leaks:

For the 911, the following oil leaks can be fixed with the engine in
the car:

* indicates more commonly seen leak.

*1. Oil thermostat O-ring
 2. Oil pressure/temperature sender seal
*3. Oil return tubes (collapsing type)
*4. Camshaft O-ring/paper seal
 5. Any oil lines
 6. Valve covers
 7. Timing chain covers
 8. Timing chain housing to case <-- not 100% sure about this one.
*9. Front crank seal
 10. Intermediate shaft seal

Cannot be fixed with engine in car (without major difficulty and
perhaps some cutting of sheetmetal):

 1. Case halves leaking
 2. Cylinder to case seal (accompanies broken head stud)
*3. Cam housing to head
*4. Rear main seal

John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

Alright, another Weissach owner!  I've got a metallic black Weissach - is
yours platinum?  Yeah, these cars came with all available options as far
as I know.  I've heard they were meant to sate the American public since
no Turbos were being imported - that's where the tail comes from.  The
alarm system goes off whenever any interior lights go on, or at least it
is linked to those switches, i.e. when my left driver's door switch was on
the fritz, I would sometimes open it with the alarm on, not notice it
since the interior lights didn't light, then as i sat down - WONK WONK
WONK!  BTW, the 'siren' is just the car's horn - no wonder it couldn't be
found.  The keys are easily made at a locksmith's - cost me about $20 and
got to listen to a guy describe being a repo man/bounty hunter and what he
thought of his 911 Targa (wonder how he came by it?).

Other options that were standard on the Weissach:
1. all power accessories, sunroof, heated mirrors, headlight washers (not
sure how many of these had become standard by 1980), antenna, etc.
2. Whale's tail (same as '76 Turbo Carrera I believe - my favorite tail)
3. Custom interior and exterior (colors - interior: Dorian grey with
burgundy piping, exterior [2 variations, 200 copies each]: metallic black
with metallic platinum wheel accents & all metallic platinum 
4. Bilstein shocks 
5. I believe an upgraded stereo

As mentioned above, 400 of these were made, 200 in each color scheme.  If
you need to know anymore, please let me know...

Blaine

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 93 09:59:42 EST
From: johnc@gdstech.grumman.com (John Colasante)
Subject: success (at least partial)


   estimate the amount to about 1/2 a quart.  During this short run (about 30
   to 45 seconds) I had the air filter/air box cover off.  After I assesed the
   damage to the floor and wall behind the exhaust I noticed there was at
   least a quart in the air box (and running out of the corner and down to the
   floor).  I understand this is NOT good ;-) but, I would like to hear a
   possible reason there was SO much oil in the air box.  Where would it come
   from; loose fitting somewhere, missing connection?  Would bad rings cause
   this much oil to surface?  I do understand that the engine is probably

One reason for oil to enter the CIS airbox is from overfilling. The
oil enters the airbox and also pours out of the oil breather vent
which is located *in front* of the oil cooler (hard to see) and
usually has a little rubber nipple over it. Don't assume your engine
needs a rebuild yet until you check compression and/or leakdown. What
you are experiencing is *normal* for a overfilled CIS engine.

John Colasante                   johnc@gdstech.grumman.com

Date:     Mon, 29 Nov 93 10:33:30 EST
From: "Hugh A. Huntzinger" (CCAC-LAD) <huntzing@PICA.ARMY.MIL>
Subject:  RE: 911 security

 ( I originally posted this on 7/20/93, if it sounds like a repeat to anyone )

The factory alarm system for an '85 911 is the "key in the side of the
drivers door" and uses factory controlled keys that are impossible to
replace (I tried).  Only option is a new tumbler (with 2 new keys; ~$75).

After a bonehead event, I'm at my locksmith (Master Grinding, Denville, NJ.)
to get my key fragment removed.  He comes up with a blank that works!
The ID# of the blank that he got to work is YM22...a SAAB key, I think.

It took a second try on the cutting machine to get it right, so it's not
perfectly straightforward, but for only $10, I got the fragment removed
and *three* new alarm system keys.

Non-1985's should be the same if it uses the same tumbler as the '85;
I have no way of checking part#s.  But at only $2/key...what the hey?

Given its vulnerability to human error resulting in a broken key, I wish
that I would have tried the locksmith before I broke the original, as it
would have been easier to make a duplicate had it been intact.  I'm not
too concerned about how it probably hurts the 'concours' value of the car
some far, distant day in the future, but it is a consideration for others.

 -hh

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 93 16:30:13 GMT

Philip Katz writes

| 	First off was the 911, in Baltic Blue, with 46k miles on the
| clock. Perhaps I was in awe of the car (after wanting one for so long), or
| perhaps it was trying to cope with the offset pedals, or perhaps it was
| the unfamiliar road, but I was disappointed :-( I'm quite happy to admit
| that the fault was probably more to do with how I was driving it than with

I think you hit the nail on the head there, my 3.2 Carrera only starts
to rip up the road at around 4100-4200 rpm. I think that to get maximum
thrill factor from an 911 you need to be pushing up towards the redline for each
gear change, this gives optimum torque in the next ratio. It took me
some time to learn how to do this. Fear that I would die in an untimely
but glorious RTA was the main constraint. 

Also, it is somewhat tricky to take a 911 to the limit while test driving
(especially as a 911 novice) as it tends to produce white knuckles and brown 
trousers in the unfortunate sales droog.

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 1993 10:50:45 -0500
From: William M Cheman <wcheman@stealth>
Subject: Re: '77 oil leaking

I use the expando oil return tubes on my '79 SC 3.0. They're from Imparts, 
though, not Automotion. The seal in the middle is simply rubber o-rings- 
no snap-rings.  I used motor oil to lubricate the end rings (in the head 
and case), no gasket sealer, etc.  The middle seals work fine, a couple end 
seals have very minor leaks- much better than before replacement. I'm 
satisfied until I can do a proper reconstruction (for broken head stud 
replacement- different story). Another leak source was the fitting for the 
external oil cooler line at the case.  I have no suggestions to fix this one.
There have been previous discussions concerning motor oil selection, with 
a partial consensus in favor of synthetic oils. 
As well, there is more than a little unhappiness with the 2.7, switching to 
a 3.0 would be a _good thing_.

Bill
'79 SC coupe

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 93 13:51:12 CST
From: wpcostel@porsche.aud.alcatel.com (William Costello)
Subject: 911 Security System

When I purchased my 1986 911 Coupe, I received only one key for the factory
alarm system. The Porsche dealer said that Porsche did not sell alarm system
key blanks and that I would need to purchase a new alarm system lock if I
needed more that one key. I took the car and the one key to an old locksmith
who matched it to a BMW ignition key blank. The key he cut works fine and only
cost a $1.50 or so.

Also, the siren for the factory alarm is located behind the front bumper in the
luggage compartment.

W.P. Costello (wpcostel@porsche.aud.alcatel.com)

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 1993 15:32:49 -0500
From: sundt@cc.bellcore.com (Mark Sundt)
Subject: Re: '77 911 oil leaking

>I haven't had a good look under my car yet, but have noticed that every
>time I stop, I am losing several tablespoons of engine oil from two spots
>under the engine.  Also, driving around, especially with the heater (if
>you can call it that!) on, gives an aroma of burning oil and gas.
>
>I'd be interested in knowing if anyone has bought the Anti-Leak Carrera
>valve covers on page 129 of the Automotion catalog and/or the expandable
>oil return tubes on p. 155 and had any luck with these parts?
>
>My engine is a 2.7 original from the '77 911S, and has 125K on it.  To my
>knowledge, the engine has never been rebuilt.

I used to own a '77 911S with 110K miles and over a couple of years I
tracked down and fixed many sources of leaks. The lower valve covers and
the oil return tubes were two of the first things that I replaced. The
lower valve covers were so prone to leaking that they may have already been
replaced on your car, so I would check first before replacing them. The
originals were smooth, the later models were ribbed to eliminate the
warping tendencies. I'm not sure which expandable oil tubes I used (there
are probably several types available).

Additionally, I replaced all gaskets and O-rings on the chain tensioner
covers (you can do this without removing the chains). Next I replaced all
gaskets and an O-ring on the top front of the engine at the breather
hose/oil thermostat connections. Finally, the engine oil radiator developed
a leak and I replaced it. After all this the engine was *almost* completely
dry - only a drop every once in a while.

Hope this helps.

Mark

- --
Mark Sundt, Bellcore, (908) 758-3134
sundt@cc.bellcore.com or uunet!bcr!sundt

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 93 16:01:09 EST
From: fwk@hogpa.ho.att.com
Subject: Re: Some Leakdown Details

>To Whom It  May Concern,
>The leakdown test is a relative, not absolute, measurement.  The flow of 
>air through the testor orifice is, among other things, a function of the 
>pressure difference, fourth power of orifice radius, and orifice length, and 
>described by Poiseuille's formula (Landau & Lifshitz, Fluid Mechanics, page 
>57).  

It seems to me that the leakdown tester at least has the opportunity to
be an absolute measurement, with the units of measurement being flow
(i.e. liters/min., or other similar units). As you say, the flow is dependent
on many things, but a leakdown tester should, in concept, be able to have
the parameters you listed considered in the design, or at least included by
a calibration process, perhaps at a given input pressure.
As you note below, the interpretation of the
leakage rate (expressed as an absolute flow rate) is still application
dependent, but you could at least compare flow readings from similar
engines made with different leakdown testers and draw meaningful
conclusions.

>The question becomes one of how large an opening is permitting the air to 
>escape from the cylinder.  If no air is escaping, then the pressure in the 
>cylinder will equalize with the supply pressure.  Further, the qustion 
>becomes the opening size relative to the port dimension.  For example, if 
>both a large engine and small engine have the same size defect in a valve 
>seat, both will give the same reading during a leakdown test.  However, the 
>effect on a small 356 engine vs. a large dragster engine would be 
>substantial.  Piston ring end gaps, etc, obviously contribute to the reason 
>why the test is only relative.  

I don't understand your reasoning here. Leakage past piston ring end
gaps is still leakage, and indicative of reduced performance. Why isn't
an absolute measure of total leakage, including piston ring end gaps,
of interest as a measure of the need for a rebuild? Again, the threshold
for a rebuild might be dependent on cylinder displacement.
 
>	When the test is made, the differences between cylinders is the 
>information of value.  However, were someone to test a new or newly rebuilt 

I believe that racers, and presumably others who track the aging of
a particular engine, use the evolution of the leakdowns of each cylinder
over time as a useful measure, even if all of the cylinders are
uniformly increasing in leakage. I used to do this with my race
engines, anyway.

>...
>I have built two testers and compared them to the Snap-on tester.  The 
>comparison was made by filling a plastic jug with water and inverting it in a 
>filled sink.  I then inserted an air nozzle, which was connected to the 
>tester, into the jug and measured the time required to fill the jug with air.  
>Using a small valve in the fabricate testers, I was able to adjust the air 
>flow to match that of the Snap-on tester.  As I recall, the time was 4.8 sec 
>for a gallon of water (average after 10 trials).

Do you recall what the Snap-On tester read (in differential pressure, or
percent) with a leakage rate which filled the gallon bottle in 4.8 Sec.?

Frank Kerfoot
fwk@hogpa.att.com

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 93 16:04:48 EST
From: BlaineDeLancey <Blaine.Delancey@lambada.oit.unc.edu>

> 
> P-netters,

Since you mentioned four socket head bolts, these are probably older
half-axles which also have dowels holding them in place (2 I think). 
Between them and the gaskets, I think you just need to apply some elbow
grease.  I wouldn't just drop the engine with them attached - ouch!

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 93 18:01:11 EST
From: vayda@erim.org (Alan Vayda)
Subject: Re: 951/944 headlight motor

This is probably obvious, but you should check to make sure that
nothing is interfering with the mechanism that raises the headlights.
On my car (1983 944), the windshield washer pump occasionally falls
off and jams my headlights.  In my case they usually don't close fully.

Another thing to check is the light switch on the dashboard.  A few
months ago when my headlights wouldn't raise or turn on the dealer
replaced the switch to fix it. 

Alan Vayda
vayda@erim.org

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 1993 17:51:46 EST
From: zeigler@merck.com
Subject: Porsche / Ferrari


In response to Ken's observations:

>My observation is that a far higher percentage of Porsche 
>people have access to and interest in Internet communications
>than do Ferrari people.

I think a lot of Ferrari owners have access to internet - they
just don't have a fearless leader like Stan willing to coordinate
all this chaos at such a favorable price!

I've seen Ferrarri and Lamborghini drivers at Watkins Glenn and
Lime Rock driving schools and they seem to fall into two
categories (neither one technical) those that drive around the
track like old ladies on the way to the market, so they won't
cause any damage to their life-savings-on-wheels, and those who
WILL NOT BE PASSED no matter what the speed of the other vehicle.
One guy a couple of years ago got into a testosterone contest
with a Mustang and forgot to turn-in - a $75,000 hormonal
problem after previously being black flagged for poor sports-
manship! 


I'm sure that a lot of "car people" end up driving these cars
too, but as you said, they seem to attract people who buy them
as an icon of success.

Road and Track had an article some time ago about the Rolls
Royce Owners Club and talked about what an ordinary group of
people they all were - of varying means, but all with an 
undying enthusiasm for the marque.

In our local PCA region there is a healthy mix of techies,
socialites, track rats, etc. I've come to respect all of them.
What Porsche needs more than anything right now is sales volume.
That doctor, lawyer or broker who is buying a new 911 turbo 3.8
right now needs to be encouraged to join a group like this so
that he/she can take good care of the car and pass it along to a
mere mortal like me some day.

 -Reid

From: "Peter Barlow, SIERRA Systems Consultants, MAPS B.C." <PBARLOW@crlv01.srm.crl.gov.bc.ca>
Subject: Removing half-axles


A little rubber hammer persuasion usually does the trick.  Once loose,
the "flange" part of the joint that bolts to the trans can be backed
away a good inch or so. 

Of course, this assumes you've got CV joint axles, not the u-joint nadellas.
