Date: Mon, 29 Nov 93 16:06:54 PST
From: albrecht@nosc.mil (Robert Albrecht)
Subject: 911 heater fans

     I was poking around the engine (83 SC) after a turkey dinner
and noticed the "can" on my heater blower motor was loose - not
the mounting clamp, but between the motor and the squirel (sp)
cage housing.  Thinking I would just tighten it up, I removed the
heater blower.  Well, it seems the clips that the two screws
attach to to hold the thing together, are gone.  Rattling along
loose also broke the piece of composite stuff that holds the
brushes.  Perhaps I could fix the composite stuff (epoxy) and
fabricate a couple of clips, so I quickly made a blocking plate
to cover the hole in the "Y" duct resulting from removal of the
fan (to preserve the cooling efficiency of the engine.)

     Driving to work this AM, the heat output seemed as strong or
stronger than normal. Could it be the defective fan was actually
blocking the airflow from the engine fan?  This thing may not
have worked since I've had the car!  To my taste, the heater has
been working fine for San Diego weather, even on chilly days sans
top.  I'm *CERTAINLY* not going to spring $245 for a new one!

     Here's the question - It occured to me that I could clean up
the engine compartment if I could "Y" the ducting to the heat
exchangers at the engine shroud, rather than continuing to use
the stock plastic duct up to where the blower motor used to
mount.  How is the ducting set up on pre '75 cars?  Has anyone
else removed the heater blower from their '75+ engine
compartment, and come up with a sanitary arrangement for the air
ducting?  Is there a pre '75 part that would do the job?

Bob

	| that the fault was probably more to do with how I was driving it than with
	| the car itself, but it never seemed to have the "kick in the back"
	| acceleration that I found in a BMW M5, and it was only towards the end of
	| the test drive that I discovered that much of the power only comes in at
	| about 3500rpm.
	
	I think you hit the nail on the head there, my 3.2 Carrera only starts
	to rip up the road at around 4100-4200 rpm. I think that to get maximum
	thrill factor from an 911 you need to be pushing up towards the redline for each
	gear change, this gives optimum torque in the next ratio. It took me
	some time to learn how to do this. Fear that I would die in an untimely
	but glorious RTA was the main constraint. 
	
	Also, it is somewhat tricky to take a 911 to the limit while test driving
	(especially as a 911 novice) as it tends to produce white knuckles and brown 
	trousers in the unfortunate sales droog.

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 93 19:58 EST
From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
Subject: Re: Removing half-axles

>In trying to remove my engine this weekend, the final step in the
>workshop manual is to disconnect the half-axles.  It does not say to
>completely remove them and the photo shows the axles hanging from the
>wheels as the engine is being lowered.  I removed the four socket head
>bolts that hold the half-axle to the tranny, but was unable to release
>the axle.  

Having backed out all the CV bolts, the CV should be released from the
stub axle in the transmission.  I suppose that corrosion could bind it
in there, but have never seen/heard of that.  There's nothing wrong with
undoing the end at the semi-trailing arm as well, and then just getting
the whole half-axle out of your way.

Unless I'm missing something...

Kevin

------------------------------

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 93 19:28:52 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: first posting

>Anyway, my question is:  can a VW engine
>be temporarily installed in a 914 or 912 with no cutting or welding?  

Quick answer: 914 already had a VW engine, so "yes". But I know you 
meant "VW Type I" engine, for which, alas, the answer is "no".
Hmmm, well you *could* have meant "GTI engine", for which the answer
is also no, but the results would at least be more entertaining
(a former list member races a 914 on a dirt track series with a
water-cooled GTI engine bolted up to his 901...odd, but it *does*
work...)

For the 912, the answer is "kinda". You can bolt a Type I engine to
the 901 transmission, but making it work is another matter entirely.
You need a custom machined flywheel, to fit the VW crank with the
Porsche pressure plate and clutch disk. You also no longer have *any* 
engine sheet metal that works -- which means you *WILL* overheat the engine.

But as a stop-gap, it's doable. Just don't plan on (a) using it for
any length of time or (b) using the VW engine for anything else
without a rebuild when you're done with it. Oh, and there is the matter
of needing the flywheel...

Stan

Depending on the year etc, the half axles are held additionally by big
dowels so you may have to pry them off after removing the socket head
bolts. Also, try tapping with a mallet or wiggling them. They will
separate but sometimes they're stubborn.

Phillip,
  Driving a 911 for the first time can be somewhat of a disappointment if
your used to a 944. The 944 is a much better driver right out of the box
where the 911 takes some getting used to.  Driving the 944/968 can be a no
brainer when you want it to be.  If you want some quick 911 excitement, dive
into an exit ramp from the local expressway at about 110 and lift at the
apex :->  Seriously, the 911 does not come to life until you get the revs up
as you found.  For a daily driver the 944/968 is probably a better all
around car but there is more magic in a well driven 911.
  As to the M5, remember, Friends Don't Let Friends Drive BMWs.

Date: 30 Nov 93 06:41:00 -0600
From: Phil_Ryan-P26421@email.mot.com
Subject: Re: Removing half-axles

I've done several CV joints where they just seem to want to stay on real
tight.  I've
found the easiest way is to wipe them clean enough to see the gasket between
the joint and the gearbox flange, and use a chisel and mallet _on the gasket_.
Just tap away at one spot until you see the joint pull away from the flange,
then rotating the wheel will
cause the joint to pull free.  It totals the gasket, but then, you were going
to replace that anyways, right?
  I also recommend immediately putting the CV joint in a baggy, taping it shut
  around the driveshaft.  There's no easy way to clean and repack the joint
  installed in the car.
  I didn't catch what kind of car you are pulling the motor from, but if it's a
  911,
remove the throttle linkage from the gearbox lever to the engine before you
drop the joints, it gets in the way.

Phil

(83 SC Targa
 74 911
 70 914/4)

Date: 30 Nov 93 06:55:00 -0600
From: Phil_Ryan-P26421@email.mot.com
Subject: Re: 911 heater fans

      Here's the question - It occured to me that I could clean up
the engine compartment if I could "Y" the ducting to the heat
exchangers at the engine shroud, rather than continuing to use
the stock plastic duct up to where the blower motor used to
mount.  How is the ducting set up on pre '75 cars?  Has anyone
else removed the heater blower from their '75+ engine
compartment, and come up with a sanitary arrangement for the air
ducting?  Is there a pre '75 part that would do the job?

Bob

  The 74 911 uses CIS and has a clean hose setup.  You might want to look at
  changing to that style.  It takes a hose off of either side of the fan
  straight down through the shroud.  You would have to find the air director
  (?) for at least the right side of the fan, if not both.  I seem to recall
  seeing them in Pano.  I'd check to make sure everything would fit befor
  spending any money, but off hand it looks to me like it would.

Date: Tue, 30 Nov 1993 10:46:13 -0500
From: John N Berry <jberry@afit.af.mil>
Subject: Re:  911 Brake Light Switches

Howdy.

>I have found a problem with my 1986 911 Coupe brake lights. In slow traffic,
>depressing the brake peddle with low to moderate force will not cause my rear
>brake lights to come on 

I had a similar problem w/ both my 75 911 and 74 914.  With those models you
simply (!?) adjust the little screw/locknut set-up on the switch.  It can
take a little work to see what you are doing but it's not hard.  The arm on the
switch works against a washer on the master cylinder rod - as the rod goes down
the switch closes.  I have no info on an 86 (I WISH I had one to worry about :-)but it might be something akin to the old style.  Good luck.

john
jberry@afit.af.mil

'74 Used-To-Be-Ravenna-Green-And-I-Wish-It-Still-Were 914 1.8L

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 93 23:01:54 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: 951/944 headlight motor

>     I had a problem with the headlight motor on my 86 951 this
> weekend.  Start the car up, turn on the lights, there was a
> noticeable pause before they came up.  Then I realized they were
> not all the way up.  I turned them off (they went up a bit
[stuff deleted]

Paul, I researched headlamp motor circuit operation a few years ago 
(figures, doesn't it?).  Your problem is that you drove the car in bad 
weather over the holiday.  Come on, admit it, you got salt on the car 
didn't you?

Anyway, it sounds like your "up" direction wiring (headlamp switch, 
wires, connectors, motor contacts) is flaky.  It would take a little 
diagnosis to determine the exact problem.

The other symptoms (motor runs all the up, then back down when lights 
turned off) is normal - that's how the system works, as long as the 
"down" circuit is intact.  Call me for further, exhausting details (try 
this Friday/Saturday, I'm in NY).

		JBS

Date: Tue, 30 Nov 93 9:26:35 PST
From: Mike Weihman <mikew@hpsadpe.sad.hp.com>
Subject: re: headlamp motors

Just from the sound of it, your problem may be the rotating contacts
in the headlamp motor assembly. These contacts tell the motor when to
stop running (it's a motor turns counter, essentially.)  There could
be a bad spot on the contact which causes the motor to stop
prematurely... but when you manually move the motor, the contact wiper
again makes good contact and the motor turns.

Repair of the problem involves minor motor assembly surgery and
perhaps (ugh) motor replacement if the contacts are truly trashed.  I
posted a wiper/headlamp motor rebuild procedure some time back; email
me and I'll send a copy to you.

mikew@sad.hp.com                      mailstop 1UR-M

Mike Weihman                          Microwave Instruments Division
Hewlett-Packard Company               1212 Valley House Drive

Date: Mon, 29 Nov 93 23:05:41 TZ
From: Jim Selders <jimseld@microsoft.com>
Subject: RE: 951 Upgrade chips !

> I am interested in hearing from anyone who has had experience with the
> so-called UPGRADE chips used  to increase the output of the 951's to 240-275
> hp. Also I was wondering if other alterations are suggested when using these
> chips and how do they affect engine/gearbox wear ??

I've had the chips for 5-6 years (?) with only 2 blown head gaskets to 
show for it!  I have an '86 951 with boost pushed to 1.8 on the gauge.  
In the early days, with a drilled cycling valve and 1.9-2.0 boost 
(thanks for NOTHING Autothority!), I got tremendous power and 
not-so-hot reliability.  Now it's OK, though on long tracks you might 
upset the motor after a period of time.  Dunno about the xmission, but 
the CV's wear a bit faster...

My 217 HP is somewhere around 250-265 now, I'd estimate.

No other alterations are necessary with these chips.  Well actually a 
higher maintenance budget is a good idea.

		JBS

Date: Tue, 30 Nov 93 12:06:14 PST
From: Ed Mellinger <meed@mbari.org>
Subject: 911 Heater Hose Retrofit

> 
>      Here's the question - It occured to me that I could clean up
> the engine compartment if I could "Y" the ducting to the heat
> exchangers at the engine shroud, rather than continuing to use
> the stock plastic duct up to where the blower motor used to
> mount.  How is the ducting set up on pre '75 cars?  Has anyone
> else removed the heater blower from their '75+ engine
> compartment, and come up with a sanitary arrangement for the air
> ducting?  Is there a pre '75 part that would do the job?
> 
> 
I did this to my '83 SC, and it definitely sanitizes the engine
compartment... lets you get a good look at that ugly mess of CIS
plumbing hiding behind! :(  Plus you save the 5 lb of blower back there
at the wrong end.  Look around till you find a cheesecake shot of an
early 911 engine bay (I think the "Illustrated Buyer's Guide" has one)
and note the heater plumbing: two tin bits, one each side of the engine
shroud, that duct off some blower air and send it down to the heat
exchangers.  I don't have a part numbers, just went to a Porsche
dismantler and asked and gestured and got something that works.  You
have to re-contour the left one a bit to fit around the later
distributor.  

I find the setup fine for CA and it was even okay back in Massachusetts;
stop-and-go driving in the rain is the only dicey bit... but
that was a problem even with the old system (sure wish
Weissach had invested in decent defroster vents rather than that klugey
tacked-on blower!).  You *will* have to adjust the heater levers more
often, like any time your enthusiasm (=rpm) level goes up significantly.
Engine cooling didn't measurably change (my CHT sensor is on #4) and you
only use the heater when it's cool out anyway, no?

 -Ed

P-Netters,
 
Last friday after work, my 911/76 2.7S would not start. It cranks

Date: Tue, 30 Nov 93 21:00:58 -0600
From: stan (Stan Hanks)
Subject: Re: Gas heaters for air-cooled w/o heat

>It's getting cold...

Aw, come on -- it's still motorcycle weather!

Seriously, I'm also interested in sources for 6 volt 356 gas heaters.
Also for US "fresh air" heater systems -- that's one option mine
doesn't have...

In the meant time, I refer you to...

>Also, I'd like to hear any zany
>ideas from do-it-yourselfers who may have rigged up a system that
>works via home-brew. 

One of the crazier ideas I've seen for a while comes from VW land --
see, you block off your oil cooler, and run a line from the block-off
fitting through the back bulkhead of the car to a heat exchanger under
the back seat. There you also have a fan and some duct work, and
voila! instant heater... Of course, you also usually need another 
heat exchanger somewhere else to really cool the oil, and all other
appropriate plumbing, like some way to turn this off in the summer or
if the Fat Man sits in your back seat or...

Hey! I *seriously* considered doing this to my 914 racer before we
decided to not go ice racing on an asphalt track last weekend...
You can get kits for it -- see Hot VWs magazine.

Stan

Date: Tue, 30 Nov 93 15:11:54 PST
From: wade@nb.rockwell.com (Wade Guthrie)
Subject: Re: how do you tell what year a 911 is

Well, I'll do my best to present some approximate answers  -- I'm doing this 
from memory, so please bear with me (I'm sure others can help me get the
details, here).

First year 911 had the '911' diagonal across the lower-right corner of the
engine cover (I guess I'll call this the 'hood').

If you're looking at a targa, the back window goes from the soft-type to 
glass between the '68 and '69 model years.

I believe it was 1969 when the 911 first got any flair at all to the fenders.

The door handle went from push-button to pull-lever between the '69 and '70
model years.

If you're really lucky, you'll get a '2.2' (for 1970 and 71) or a '2.4'
(for 1972 and 73) badge on the grille over the engine.

In 1972, only, there's an oil filler flap on the front of the left (?) rear 
fender (this should be the same side as the fuel flap, which is on the front 
fender).

The 911 script changed from the skinny kind to the fat kind in the '73 model
year -- and, of course, there's no more 'T' model (I believe that you're limited
to the 911, 911S, and 911 Carerra).  That's also the year that you get the fat 
bumpers and, I believe, the reflector across the whole back that reads 
'P O R S C H E'.  Along with the fat bumpers, the trunk (front of the car) 
goes from curved-over to flat where it hits the bumper.  This is also when the
side-view mirrors went from the small metal to thick body-colored variety.

In 1978, you get the '911SC' with in-dash (rather than under-dash) air 
conditioning, cruise control, and electric windows.  I don't know if the 73-77 
cars had these features.  When do the really wide fenders (the SC style, not
the 930 style) arrive?

In 1984, you get the Carrera.  The fog lights are inset into the valence 
(according to James Fuerstenberg) on the Carrera where they stick-out on the 
SC.

In 1987, I believe, you lose the American-style "fat-boy" headlight rims in
favor of the, IMHO, prettier thin euro rims.

In 1988, I believe, you go from the clear 'P O R S C H E' on the back reflector
to the black name.

In 1990, I believe, you get the Carrera 2/4 and the new-style bumpers.


What am I missing, here?

Wade
wade@nb.rockwell.com

------------------------------
